DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.

 

825 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hatchamovitch


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


40 A.D.2d 556; 334 N.Y.S.2d 565; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3998



July 13, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and motion denied. The trial of the action is to proceed forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, operators of an unlicensed abortion clinic, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which, in an action for a permanent injunction pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 28, granted the motion of plaintiff, State of New York (people), for a temporary injunction enjoining the operators from operating their clinic.


OVERVIEW: The people sought to temporarily enjoin the operation of an unlicensed abortion clinic, which relief the supreme court granted pending a trial on the action for a permanent injunction. The court reversed the injunction, finding that such relief should not have been granted because there was doubt as to the ultimate right to the relief sought. The court found that the people had no right to the temporary injunction because it had not shown that the operation of the clinic reasonably might result in injury to any person, as required by N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2801-b, nor was it clear that the clinic required a license under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 700.2(a)(6). Thus, the court concluded that the order constituted an improvident exercise of discretion because temporary injunctions which, in effect, granted the same relief to be expected by final judgment should only be granted with great caution and only when required by urgent situations, and then only upon clear evidence. Furthermore, the court found that the injunction granted exceeded the scope of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2801-b and prevented the individual defendants from certain clearly lawful medical practices.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the supreme court granting the preliminary injunction, denied the motion, and ordered the trial of the action seeking a permanent injunction to proceed forthwith.

 

826 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sweeney v. Murphy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


39 A.D.2d 306; 334 N.Y.S.2d 239; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4019



July 6, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed on the law and facts with costs and judgment granted in favor of plaintiffs in accordance with the opinion by Moule, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Town Law §§ 300-309, which provided that adjoining landowners were jointly responsible to install and maintain fences, plaintiff landowners appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Herkimer County (New York), which denied cross-motions for summary judgment filed by the landowners and by defendant dairy farm.


OVERVIEW: The landowners shared a common boundary with the dairy farm, but had no cattle of their own and no desire or need for a fence. The trial court refused to determine the matter without a trial. On appeal, the court found that the matter was a question of law only and so was amenable to summary judgment. The court noted a strong presumption of validity to legislation, but also noted that a statute whose effect was to curtail the liberty of persons and whose justification was claimed to lie in the exercise of the police power of the state had to bear a reasonable relationship to the alleged public good. While the statute might have served a valid public interest when it was first enacted in 1788, it was now arbitrary and confiscatory under some circumstances. The court held that the statutes acted to deprive the landowners of their property because they would be compelled to erect and maintain at their own expense fences that they neither needed nor wanted. The statutes were, therefore, unconstitutional insofar as they applied to the subject property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which denied the landowners' motion for summary judgment, and granted judgment in favor of the landowners.


HEADNOTES

Fences -- maintenance of division fence -- Town Law ( §§ 300-309), which requires owners of adjoining tracts of land to make and maintain division fence between lands unless they agree to let their lands lie open, unconstitutional insofar as they apply to plaintiffs' property -- statutes would deprive plaintiffs of their property, since they would be compelled to erect and maintain fences which they neither need nor want.

1. Section 300 of the Town Law provides in part that: "Each owner of two adjoining tracts of land, except when they otherwise agree, shall make and maintain a just and equitable portion of the division fence between such lands, unless both of said adjoining owners shall agree to let their lands lie open, along the division line, to the use of all animals which [***2] may be lawfully upon the lands of either". Section 303 provides that any dispute between adjoining owners shall be settled by two fence viewers of the town. Section 305 provides that any party who refuses to erect or maintain a division fence shall be liable for the expense in making or repairing the fence. Sections 300 through 309 of the Town Law insofar as they apply to plaintiffs' property are unconstitutional.

2. There is no question of fact and under these circumstances summary judgment is proper. These statutes would deprive plaintiffs of their property, since they would be compelled to erect and maintain at their own expense fences which they neither need nor want. Requiring an adjoining owner, under the circumstances in this case, who does not keep livestock, to share the cost of the fence for the benefit of his neighbor is not reasonably necessary to any legitimate public purpose and is oppressive.

 

827 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


County of Orange v. Public Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


39 A.D.2d 311; 334 N.Y.S.2d 434; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4022



July 6, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Petitions dismissed on the merits and opinions and orders confirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner County of Orange and petitioner Public Service Commission of the State of New York (commission) brought proceedings under N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 128(1), art. 7, to review three opinions and orders of the commission (New York) involving respondent utility, intervenor towns, respondent commission, and intervenor utility concerning an electric transmission line.


OVERVIEW: The utility applied under art. 7 of the N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law §§ 120-130, to construct an electric transmission line, which was allowed and contested by petitioners. The court dismissed the petitions on the merits and confirmed the opinions and orders without costs. The court held that the notice provisions of art. 7 were constitutionally adequate, so property owners were afforded a sufficient opportunity for judicial review of the finding of need for a line. The court concluded that there was no merit to petitioners' contentions. The court found substantial evidence supported the commissions' findings of a need for the line and that the route was the most desirable under the relevant factors. The court found substantial evidence supported finding that it was appropriate to construct the line above ground, and the decision to approve this line was not arbitrary or unreasonable. The court found no merit to petitioner's assertion that art. 7 violated the home rule provision of N.Y. Const. art. IX by authorizing the commission to override local laws and regulations. The court found no denial of notice and the opportunity to be heard under that constitution's eminent domain provisions.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed on their merits all of the petitions in these proceedings brought by the petitioners pursuant to statute. The court confirmed, without costs, the commission's opinions and orders regarding the utility, the towns, the commission, and the utility with respect to the electric transmission line.


HEADNOTES

Public utilities -- siting of transmission lines -- in proceeding to review determination of Public Service Commission (PSC) made under Public Service Law (art. 7) granting certificate of environmental compatibility and public need for siting of north-south transmission line, there is ample proof that need is great and that route approved is most desirable one -- determination to certify north-south leg and continue proceeding as to east-west leg was within power of PSC -- article 7 does not contravene home rule provisions of State Constitution insofar as it authorizes PSC to override local laws found to be unreasonably restrictive -- article 7 does not unconstitutionally deprive owners of adequate notice -- question of need for taking is for Legislature -- taking from property owners of prior statutory right to litigate question of need in condemnation proceeding  [***2] did not deprive them of constitutional right; court review in article 7 proceeding is accorded -- if notice inadequate under article 7, property owner could institute action for injunction -- orders of PSC are confirmed.

1. In a proceeding to review a determination of the Public Service Commission (PSC) made under article 7 of the Public Service Law (L. 1970, ch. 272; §§ 120 through 130) granting a certificate of environmental compatibility and public need for the siting of a certain north-south transmission line, there is ample proof that the need is great and pressing and that the route approved is the most desirable one. Substantial evidence also supports the finding that it would be best to build the line aboveground, and such finding is not arbitrary or unreasonable.

2. The determination to certify the north-south leg and continue the proceedings with respect to the east-west leg is within the power of the PSC since section 126 broadly authorizes it to grant the application for certification "as filed or * * * upon such terms, conditions, limitations or modifications of the construction * * * of the facility" as it deems appropriate. There is proof of the reasonableness [***3] and propriety of that determination.

3. Article 7 does not contravene the home rule provisions in article 9 of the State Constitution insofar as it authorizes the PSC to override local laws and regulations which it finds "unreasonably restrictive" (§ 126, subd. 1, par. [b]) with respect to the location of major transmission facilities. Both article IX of the State Constitution and section 11 of the Statute of Local Governments have reserved to the State Legislature the power to enact general laws relating to matters of substantial State concern, even though they may be intermingled with matters of local concern, and article 7 of the Public Service Law is a general law relating to matters of substantial State concern. In this case PSC's exercise of its statutory power to override unreasonably restrictive local laws and regulations adequately conformed to the requirements of section 126 (subd. 1, par. [f]) of the Public Service Law.

4. Article 7 of the Public Service Law, read with section 4 of the Condemnation Law, does not unconstitutionally deprive property owners of adequate notice and an opportunity to be heard on the issue of need for the taking of their property for a [***4] utility transmission line. Notice to State Senators and Assemblymen from the affected areas, and to the executive officers of the counties, towns and villages in the affected area, to interested State departments, and to property owners by publication in newspapers in the affected area is adequate to satisfy the requirements of due process. Moreover, the actual location of the transmission line cannot be known until the PSC decides which of various proposed routes should be approved. Therefore at the time the hearings are scheduled, the parties whose land will eventually be taken are unknown and it is unreasonable to hold that they are entitled to due process notice.

5. The question of need for a taking is for the Legislature, not the courts. The courts cannot review such legislative determination and therefore notice and a hearing on that question are not essential to due process.

6. Property owners were not deprived of any constitutional right when in 1970 the Legislature took away their previous statutory right to litigate, in the condemnation proceeding, the question of need for a taking for a utility transmission line. A court review of the finding of need is accorded by [***5] an article 7 proceeding such as this one.

7. Assuming that the notice accorded under article 7 is constitutionally inadequate, a property owner would still be able to institute an action for an injunction and a declaratory judgment to bar the taking of his property on the ground that the finding of need was corrupt or irrational.

8. The orders of the PSC should be confirmed and the petitions dismissed.

 

828 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bennet


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


39 A.D.2d 320; 334 N.Y.S.2d 350; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4107



July 5, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the County Court, Nassau County, rendered October 14, 1971 in the first above-entitled matter, reversed, on the law, and appellant remanded to the County Court for resentence in accordance with the opinion rendered herewith.

Appeal from judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, entered October 20, 1971 in the second above-entitled matter, dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court, Nassau County (New York), which convicted defendant, on a plea of guilty, of criminally selling a dangerous drug in the fourth degree and sentenced her to an indeterminate term of three years; and, from a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special in Nassau County (New York), which dismissed defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The question before the court was whether on sentencing the trial court had to impose a prison term on a narcotic addict without considering the other alternatives open in cases of other offenders, simply because the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission (NACC), for reasons arising out of its own internal management, would not at the time accept involuntary criminal certifications of addicts. The court reversed defendant's sentence and held that imprisonment was not the only course that the sentencing court had to follow and that the usual sentencing options provided by law were available. When the NACC was forced by financial pressures to refrain from carrying out the manifest and salutary intent of N.Y. Penal Law § 60.15, then § 60.15 had to be construed to authorize the court to apply any of the sentencing options which were appropriate under the circumstances to the convicted addict. There was no reason of constitutional stature that could justify difference in treatment of persons of similar standing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentence and remanded for resentencing in accordance with its opinion. The court dismissed the appeal from the order that denied habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

Mental health -- drug addiction -- criminal sentence -- when, for lack of funds, Narcotic Addiction Control Commission refuses to accept involuntary criminal certifications of drug addicts (Mental Hygiene Law, § 210-a; Penal Law, § 60.15), court may apply any sentencing option, such as probation, conditional discharge, reformatory or indeterminate term, etc.,  [***2] which is appropriate to circumstances of addict and his crime (Penal Law, § 60.10) -- statutes construed to afford equal protection of laws.

When, for lack of funds or facilities, the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission refuses to accept involuntary criminal certifications of drug addicts (Mental Hygiene Law, § 210-a), and thus refrains from carrying out the rehabilitative intent of the laws, the constitutional principles of equal protection of the laws require that the sentencing laws should not be strictly construed to limit the court to imposing a prison term, but rather should be construed to authorize the court to apply any one of the sentencing options -- such as probation or conditional or unconditional discharge or reformatory or indetermiate term, etc. -- which is appropriate to the circumstances of the particular addict and his crime (Penal Law, §§ 60.10, 60.15; cf. § 65.00, subd. 1; § 65.05, subd. 1; § 65.10, subd. 2; § 65.20, subd. 1; Mental Hygiene Law, § 208, subd. 4; cf. § 200; § 204, subds. 1, 9). Accordingly, the convicted drug addict in this case is remanded by the Appellate Division to the sentencing court for an appropriate resentence.

Roberts v. County Court of Wyoming County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


39 A.D.2d 246; 333 N.Y.S.2d 882; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4112



June 29, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Application unanimously denied and petition dismissed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Bronx County District Attorney (district attorney) applied for a writ of prohibition against the exercise of jurisdiction by respondent Wyoming County Court over a habeas corpus proceeding initiated by a prisoner that challenged the constitutionality of his conviction.


OVERVIEW: A prisoner who was convicted in Bronx County brought a habeas corpus proceeding in Wyoming County, alleging that his conviction was unconstitutional. The Wyoming County Court ordered Bronx County to conduct a hearing. The district attorney applied for a writ of prohibition against Wyoming County and the court denied the application. The court held that the writ of prohibition was inappropriate because the district attorney had an adequate remedy at law and equity: an appeal of the habeas proceeding. Failing to appeal did not make the writ of prohibition appropriate. When more than six months had elapsed before the district attorney brought the application, it was too long a delay to justify. In addition, the Wyoming County Court had authority to remand a prisoner to the authorities of another county and to order that certain proceedings be had in any court of original jurisdiction in the state where the matter before the court was properly cognizable in a habeas corpus proceeding. Practicality and necessity justified the use of habeas corpus; consequently, habeas corpus was an appropriate remedy for the prisoner.


OUTCOME: The court denied the district attorney's application for a writ of prohibition against the jurisdiction of the Wyoming County Court over a habeas corpus proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Habeas corpus -- prohibition -- assertion that judgment of conviction violated fundamental constitutional rights may be made by habeas corpus, even though coram nobis would also be proper -- Wyoming County Court has jurisdiction to remand prisoner who was convicted in Bronx County to Bronx County authorities, and to direct that another post-conviction hearing be held in Bronx County as to said defendant's sanity at time of his trial -- application by Bronx County District Attorney to Appellate Division, Fourth Department, for writ of prohibition, denied.

1. Habeas corpus is a proper procedure for an assertion that a judgment [***2] of conviction was rendered in violation of fundamental constitutional rights, even though coram nobis would also be a proper procedure.

2. The Wyoming County Court has jurisdiction to issue a writ of habeas corpus and to remand a prisoner, who was convicted in Bronx County Court, to the authorities of Bronx County, and to direct that another post-conviction hearing be held in Bronx County as to said defendant's sanity at the time of his trial in Bronx County, and to direct that such sanity hearing be held before a different Judge than the one who had presided both at the trial and at the post-conviction hearing in 1962 in Bronx County.

3. The Appellate Division, Fourth Department, denies this application made to it by the Bronx County District Attorney for a writ of prohibition against such order of the Wyoming County Court. The District Attorney did not make this application to the Appellate Division within a reasonable time (cf. CPLR 217), and he failed to appeal from said County Court order.

831 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Shelofsky v. Helsby


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


39 A.D.2d 168; 332 N.Y.S.2d 723; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4426; 80 L.R.R.M. 3229



June 1, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgment directed to be entered, without costs, in favor of defendants declaring that chapters 503 and 504 of the Laws of 1971 are constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, employees of the State of New York and the certified representative of public employees brought an action against defendant New York Public Employment Relations Board to test the constitutionality of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §§ 214, 201(7). The controversy was submitted upon an agreed statement of facts pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3222.


OVERVIEW: The employees received important benefits from belonging to their certified representative. N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §§ 214, 201(7) provided that they could not be a member of any employee organization in which employees of the same employer were members if they were designated confidential or managerial. The Director of the New York State Office of Employee Relations requested that the employees be designated managerial or confidential. The employees brought an action objecting that the statute unconstitutionally deprived them of their benefits. The court held that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 214 did not fail for vagueness and did not violate the employees' rights of due process, freedom of association, and equal protection. The statute provided a reasonable means for limiting the number of employees designated by the employer. The government could rightfully require that those charged with the responsibility of implementing and administering collective bargaining agreements be free from any possible conflict of interest either because they were the beneficiaries of the agreements they were implementing or because they were unsympathetic to management's interests due to associational bias.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment in favor of the Public Employment Relations Board declaring that the statute was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- membership -- employee organization -- amendments to Civil Service Law ( § 201, subd. 7; § 214) which provide that certain managerial and confidential public employees, as designated by public employer, should not hold office in, or be member of employee organization in which employees of same employer are members, do not impair plaintiffs' constitutional rights -- definition of "public employee" (§ 201, subd. 7) not void for vagueness -- not invalid for failure to identify who is to act for State, "public employer" -- delegation of authority within permissible limits -- standards sufficient under established administrative law -- no violation of rights of due process, freedom of association, and equal protection.

1. Amendments of the Civil Service Law ( § 201, subd 7; § 214) provide that certain managerial and confidential public employees, as designated by the public employer upon application to the Public Employment [***2] Relations Board (PERB), should not hold office in or be a member of any employee organization in which employees of the same employer are members. Subdivision 7 of section 201 which defines "public employee" does not fail for vagueness. It is not invalid for failure to identify who is to act for the State, the "public employer". It is unrealistic to expect that it would name the responsible individual for every imaginable case. The "public employer" is sufficiently defined in the Civil Service Law ( § 201, subd. 6) in terms of the various political subdivisions and authorities, commission's agencies or departments, each of which can be readily identified in any given case.

2. Under the statutory plan, the public employer designates the subject employees to PERB, which, acting under its grant of authority in paragraph (j) of subdivision 5 of section 205, may approve or disapprove the designations, an administrative decision-making process calling for the exercise of discretion and expertise. This is a permissible delegation of authority limited by the direction that it be "reasonably" exercised within the standards set by the Legislature.

3. The statute provides that the employees [***3] designated must "(a) formulate policy" or "(b) assist directly in the preparation for and conduct of collective negotiations" or "have a major role in the administration of agreements or in personnel administration provided that such role is not of a routine or clerical nature and requires exercise of independent judgment". The standards are sufficient under established administrative law.

4. The amendments do not violate plaintiffs' rights of due process, freedom of association, and equal protection.

5. The amendments do not unconstitutionally impair plaintiffs' contractual rights.

 

832 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Keystone Associates v. State


Claim No. 49592


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


39 A.D.2d 176; 333 N.Y.S.2d 27; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4428



June 1, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law and the facts, and motion to dismiss claim denied, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Court of Claims (New York), which granted the motion by respondent state, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211, to dismiss appellant's claim on the grounds that the court did not have jurisdiction of the subject matter and that the claim had failed to state a cause of action. Appellant had sought recover for damages for wrongful appropriation of property by the state.


OVERVIEW: By prior decision 1966 N.Y. Laws ch. 691 had been declared unconstitutional because it unreasonably deprived appellant of the beneficial use of its property without payment of just compensation. After that determination appellant filed the instant claim against the state seeking damages for the temporary appropriation of its interests during the period between the enactment of the statute and when it was finally issued a demolition permit. The state argued that it had no liability for damages and the claims court granted summary judgment to the state. On appeal the court reversed and held that: (1) the claim could not be dismissed at this stage of the proceedings for failure to allege an appropriation; and (2) the state was the proper party responsible for the de facto appropriation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which had been in favor of the state, and ordered that the motion to dismiss appellant's claim be denied.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- de facto appropriation -- decision in Matter of Keystone Assoc. v. Moerdler (19 N Y 2d 78) requires determination of de facto appropriation -- de facto appropriation requires compensation to same extent as de jure appropriation -- said decision also requires determination that State is responsible party for appropriation -- motion to dismiss claim should be denied.

1. In Matter of Keystone Assoc. v. Moerdler (19 N Y 2d 78), the court held that chapter 691 of the Laws of 1966 was unconstitutional in that it unreasonably deprived the claimant of the beneficial use of its property without payment of just compensation. After this determination, claimant filed the instant claim against the State for damages for the temporary appropriation of its interests during the period between the enactment of the statute and January 17, 1967, when [***2] it was finally issued a demolition permit. Because of the prior decision, it cannot be held that, as a matter of law, there was no de facto appropriation. There was in fact an appropriation of property interests, necessarily temporary in nature, prior to the final determination by the courts as to the validity of the special law. A de facto appropriation requires compensation to the same extent as a de jure appropriation.

2. The power of eminent domain was exercised by the State of New York whether directly or indirectly through the created corporation. Since chapter 691 of the Laws of 1966 was declared unconstitutional, there is no autonomous corporation which could be liable. There is no basis for liability on the part of the City of New York and no impediment to liability on the part of the State. Particularly because of the decision of the Court of Appeals in the prior action, the State is the proper party responsible for the alleged de facto appropriation. The motion to dismiss the claim should be denied.

 

833 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cuda


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


39 A.D.2d 133; 332 N.Y.S.2d 558; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4472



May 25, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed on the law and information dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the County Court of Steuben County (New York), which found defendant, who had been adjudged a wayward minor, guilty of violating his probation, which revoked defendant's probation, and which sentenced him to a reformatory term under the jurisdiction of the New York State Department of Correction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the information charging him with being a wayward minor, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 913, should have been dismissed on the grounds that the language of § 913-a was too vague and ambiguous to be constitutionally valid and that the provision of § 913-b, which authorized a trial by a magistrate without a jury, was also unconstitutional because punishment under § 913-c could exceed six months of imprisonment. The court, in reversing the trial court's judgment, agreed with both of defendant's assertions. First, the court, noting that § 913 applied to defendant's case even though it was omitted from a recent change to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law, found that the § 913 provided for a punishment in excess of six months and was unconstitutional because it failed to afford defendant a jury trial. Next, the court found that the language of § 913-a, that required findings of moral depravity, was unconstitutional as vague and ambiguous and, because the information and judgment against defendant was based on a finding of moral depravity, dismissed the information.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, on the law, the trial court's judgment that revoked defendant's probation and sentenced him to a reformatory term. The court dismissed the information charging defendant with being a wayward minor.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- wayward minor -- Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 913-a) defining wayward minor unconstitutional since words "morally depraved" are vague and ambiguous -- unconstitutional also to provide for punishment in excess of six months without affording defendant jury trial -- information dismissed.

Section 913-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure provides that "a. Any person between the ages of sixteen and twenty-one who either * * * (5) is willfully disobedient to the reasonable and lawful commands of parent * * * and is morally depraved or is in danger of becoming morally depraved or (6) who without just cause and without the consent of parents * * * deserts his or her home * * * and is morally depraved or is in danger of becoming morally depraved * * * may be deemed a wayward minor." A person adjudged to be a wayward minor may be punished under section 913-c of said law by being placed on probation up to two years or committed for an indeterminate [***2] term to a reformatory institution not to exceed three years. Since the statute provided for punishment in excess of six months, it was unconstitutional for failure to afford defendant a jury trial. Because of the vagueness and ambiguity of the words "morally depraved" in section 913-a, the section is unconstitutional and void. The information should be dismissed.

 

834 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cohen v. Levitt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


39 A.D.2d 141; 332 N.Y.S.2d 662; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4475



May 25, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state employees sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which dismissed the state employee's action against respondent state officials under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to obtain certain service credits pursuant to 1970 N.Y. Laws ch. 457, § 33.


OVERVIEW: The state employees worked for the Division of Employment in the New York State Department of Labor. They were all hired by the United States Employment Service during World War II when the Division of Employment was federalized. When the Division was returned to the jurisdiction of the state, they became state employees. The state employees filed an application sought to obtain credit in the state retirement system under 1970 N.Y. Laws ch. 457, § 33 for the time spent in service of the federal government. Section 33 allowed those employees that had been transferred to the employ of federal government and then back to the state to obtain such credit under certain conditions. State officials denied the application and the trial court upheld that decision. The court affirmed the trial court's decision and found that § 33 did not apply to the state employee's because they were hired by the federal government and were not transferred to federal employ from the state. Furthermore, § 33 was not unconstitutional because there was no evidence that the legislature did not have a reasonable basis for making such a distinction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision in favor of the state officials.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- retirement -- Laws of 1970 (ch. 457, § 33) provided that member of New York State Employees' Retirement System, transferred to employ of Federal War Manpower Commission when State employment service was federalized during World War II and who returned to State service thereafter, might obtain retirement credit for period of service with such commission -- petitioners admittedly not in employ of New York State Division of Employment when it was federalized -- no basis to extend benefits of statute to them -- no denial of equal protection of laws.

1. Section 33 of chapter 457 of the Laws of 1970 provided: "A member of the New York State employees' retirement system * * * who was transferred to the employ of the federal war manpower commission when the state employment service was federalized during world war II and who returned to [***2] state service when the state employment service was returned to state control, may obtain retirement credit for his period of service with such commission". Since petitioners admittedly were not in the employ of the New York State Division of Employment when it was federalized, there is no basis upon which to extend the benefits of the statute to them. They were properly denied such credit.

2. Petitioners' contention that the statute is unconstitutional in that it deprives them of the equal protection of the laws is without merit. There is nothing in the record to establish that there is no reasonable basis for the legislation.

903 Park Ave. Corp. v. City Rent Agency


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


39 A.D.2d 694; 332 N.Y.S.2d 522; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4527



May 23, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant apartment owner sought review of an order and judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied its motion for preliminary injunction and declared New York City, N.Y. Local Law No. 7 (1972) was not violative of any State legislative rent regulation proscription in 1971 N.Y. Laws 372, 1012.


OVERVIEW: The Apartment owner filed a complaint against respondent rent regulator, which sought a declaration that New York City, N.Y. Local Law No. 7 (1972) was unconstitutional and also sought a preliminary injunction against its enforcement by the rent regulator. The trial court denied the motion for preliminary injunctive relief. On appeal, the court affirmed. It noted that the city council, when it passed Local Law No. 7 had expressed its awareness of 1971 N.Y. Laws 372, 1012 and stated that the extension of the rent exemption to cover rent increases due to the maximum rents established in 1972 was not more stringent or restrictive than those then in effect. The court agreed that New York City, N.Y. Local Law No. 7 (1972) was not "more stringent" nor "more restrictive" in its provisions affecting the accommodations of eligible elderly than those already in effect at the time of adoption of 1971 N.Y. Laws 372, and therefore was not antithetical to that legislation. The court also noted that irreparable harm was not shown and that the challenged regulation advanced important social policies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that denied the apartment owner's request for preliminary injunctive relief.

Devito v. Imbriano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


39 A.D.2d 796; 332 N.Y.S.2d 577; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4573



May 18, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed, with costs to the Workmen's Compensation Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an employer and its insurance carrier, filed an appeal from decisions of respondent Workmen's Compensation Board that found that the accident that killed an employee arose out of and in the course of his employment and assessed a penalty of 20 percent of the award against the carrier pursuant to N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 25(3)(c) for its failure to pay death benefits within 10 days.


OVERVIEW: The employee was a laborer in the employer's landscaping business. The employer had no fixed work location and transported the employees to the work locations each day. The employee was hit by a car and killed when he descended from the employer's truck at the end of a work day. The Workmen's Compensation Board found the accident arose out of and in the course of employment, awarded compensation, and assessed a penalty of 20 percent of the award against the employer's carrier. The employer and carrier appealed the decision, and the court affirmed. The court concluded that the Board properly concluded that the employee was within the class of "outside workers" entitled to workers' compensation protection from the time that they leave home until they return. The court also upheld that penalty assessment awarded under N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 25(3)(c). The court concluded that the amendment to the section that removed the automatic stay prevision previously contained in the law was valid and constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's decisions.

 

837 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Myers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


39 A.D.2d 122; 332 N.Y.S.2d 242; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4606



May 15, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Two judgments of the Supreme Court, Queens County, both rendered March 25, 1971, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York), which convicted defendant of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the third degree and criminal possession of a hypodermic instrument upon pleas of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was initially arrested for public intoxication pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 240.40, and the search of his person incident to the arrest led to discovery of contraband. Defendant entered guilty pleas for criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the third degree and criminal possession of a hypodermic instrument. Defendant appealed on grounds that § 240.40 was unconstitutional and that the contraband should have, therefore, been suppressed. The court affirmed the lower court. The court examined the statute to determine whether the effect of its stricture had a direct relationship to the achievement of a legitimate purpose advancing public morality, health, and order under the police power. The court held that § 240.40 met the test because it did not exact punishment for a mere chronic condition, but for a condition from which it could fairly be expected that a resulting danger from the accused's conduct would arise. Section 240.40 did not violate the Eighth Amendment because it punished the behavior as a result of defendant's intoxication. The circumstances observed by the officers, namely erratic walking, justified defendant's arrest for public intoxication.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that convicted defendant of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the third degree and criminal possession of a hypodermic instrument.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sale and possession of drug and hypodermic instrument -- public intoxication -- defendant, convicted of attempted sale of dangerous drug and criminal possession of drug and hypodermic instrument, was properly initially arrested for public intoxication (Penal Law, § 240.40) -- search incident to arrest revealed contraband -- statute (Penal Law, § 240.40) not unconstitutional -- direct relation between conduct condemned by statute and legitimate interest of community in preserving public order -- no cruel and inhuman treatment -- motion to suppress evidence properly denied.

1. The defendant, who pleaded guilty to the crimes of attempted sale of a dangerous drug, criminal possession of a dangerous [***2] drug and criminal possession of a hypodermic instrument, was initially arrested for public intoxication (Penal Law, § 240.40) and the search of his person incident to the arrest revealed the contraband. Section 240.40 of the Penal Law provides: "A person is guilty of public intoxication when he appears in a public place under the influence of alcohol, narcotics or other drug to the degree that he may endanger himself or other persons or property, or annoy persons in his vicinity." Two police officers, cruising in a patrol car in the evening, saw the defendant walking erratically on the sidewalk. They accosted him to ascertain his name, his residence and his destination but he did not reply. The police officers came to the conclusion that he was under the influence of narcotics and a danger to himself. He was not known to the officers and they testified that there were no other persons in the vicinity and that nothing indicated to them that the defendant was dangerous to others. The statute is not unconstitutional.

2. There is a direct relation between the conduct condemned by the statute and the legitimate interest which the community has in preserving the public order. The [***3] statute adds to the requirement of defendant's public intoxication that it also appear that "he may endanger himself or others or property, or annoy persons in his vicinity." The statute does not exact punishment for merely a condition, but for a condition from which it may fairly be expected that a resulting danger or annoyance from the accused's conduct will arise.

3. The statute does not provide for cruel and inhuman treatment. It may be cruel and inhuman punishment to direct sanctions against chronic intoxication or drug addiction, but that is not the thrust of this statute.

4. While the arrest depends on the judgment of the arresting officers that the intoxication of the accused is of such a degree that future behavior of the accused will endanger or annoy others, the test of whether the exercise of judgment was right is the reasonableness of the judgment in the light of the circumstances. It must be left to the judgment of the arresting officers, subject to trial and cross-examination, to determine whether the action of the accused causes the reasonable belief that the latter may create peril or annoyance to himself or to others.

5. The circumstances observed by the officers [***4] justified the defendant's arrest and the Criminal Term was correct in denying his motion to suppress the evidence found after the arrest.

In re Stanley M. (Anonymous)

839 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schneider v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


38 A.D.2d 495; 331 N.Y.S.2d 270; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4783



April 25, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners appealed an order and judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term Albany County (New York), which granted the motions of respondents, the governor and others, for summary judgment declaring that 1972 N.Y. Laws ch. 11 was a valid apportionment statute under the Constitutions of the United States and of the State of New York.


OVERVIEW: After the Legislature introduced bills to create state Assembly and Senate districts based upon the 1970 federal census, the legislation was signed into law by the governor. Petitioners challenged its constitutionality, arguing that the law violated both U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. III. The trial court dismissed the petition and declared the law to be valid. On appeal, the court held that the state prohibition against dividing counties or towns was subordinated to the federal doctrine of "one man, one vote," and thus, it was not discriminatory to cross boundaries of political subdivisions to achieve as nearly as possible equal population among legislative districts. Further, the mandates of N.Y. Const. art. III, §§ 4 and 5 were followed because the districts were in "as compact form as practicable" and consisted of "contiguous territory." While agreeing that some districts resulted in oddly shaped designs, the requirement to meet population equality made such an end necessary. Last, the court held that the increase in the number of senators complied with N.Y. Const. art. III, § 4 and the three-day requirement of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 14 was also followed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and judgment dismissing the petition and declaring the apportionment statute valid.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- apportionment of Legislature -- chapter 11 of Laws of 1972 which apportioned Legislature did not unconstitutionally divide counties in creating districts since prohibition against division of counties must give way to one man, one vote doctrine -- requirements of Constitution that districts be "compact" and "contiguous" were not violated -- there was no gerrymandering -- increase of Senate from 50 to 60 members complied with Constitution -- bill was on desks of legislators for three calendar days -- census figures used were latest corrected figures.

1. Chapter 11 of the Laws of 1972 apportioned the Legislature of the State. It divided 9 minor counties in the formation of Senate districts and 11 minor counties in the formation [***2] of Assembly districts. The constitutional prohibition against dividing counties or towns must be subordinated to the Federal doctrine of one man, one vote. It is not discriminatory to cross boundaries of political subdivisions to achieve as nearly as possible equal population among legislative districts. Chapter 11 does not violate constitutional provisions by its division of counties in the formation of districts.

2. The requirements of the State Constitution that districts be in "as compact form as practicable" and to consist of "contiguous territory" were not violated. While some districts are shaped in odd designs, the requirement that there be population equality leads to such an end. In none of the districts cited as "noncontiguous" is it necessary to travel through an adjoining district to keep within the boundaries of the districts. The requirement of contiguity is not violated because part of the district is divided by water.

3. There was no gerrymandering in violation of public policy. If the mathematics of new district lines are acceptable, the courts should not review claims of partisanship.

4. The increase of the Senate from 50 to 60 members was in compliance [***3] with the formula of section 4 of article III of the Constitution.

5. The constitutional requirement that the bill be on the desks of the legislators for three calendar days was met.

6. On October 15, 1971 the Bureau of the Census advised the Joint Legislative Committee on Reapportionment by letter that its review of the 1970 census figures was complete and accurate. Accordingly, the population statistics used in drawing the district lines described in chapter 11 were the latest corrected Federal census figures.

 Matter of Schneider v. Rockefeller, 68 Misc 2d 869, affirmed.

 

840 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ferlito v. Judges of County Court


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


39 A.D.2d 17; 331 N.Y.S.2d 229; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4785



April 24, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of prohibition as prayed for by petitioner granted, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused filed a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for a judgment prohibiting respondents, county judges and district attorney, from retrying the accused on a grand jury indictment on the ground that reprosecution would have constituted double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: The accused was indicted on a criminal charge. A trial by jury was waived, and a bench trial was commenced, at which a witness testified for the People and was extensively cross-examined by defense counsel. The next day, the trial judge, sua sponte, declared a mistrial on the ground that his chambers had been contacted by the father of the accused, who was acquainted with the judge's cousin. Over the objection of defense counsel, who asserted the accused's double jeopardy rights under U.S. Const. amend. V. and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, the trial court placed the action on the calendar for a retrial. The accused filed a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, seeking a judgment prohibiting the county's judges and district attorney from reprosecuting the action. The court entered judgment in favor of the accused. The court held that there was no manifest necessity for the trial judge's declaration of a mistrial, and a reprosecution after a mistrial had been unnecessarily declared would have subjected the accused to personal strain and insecurity in violation of both the United States and New York constitutions, regardless of the motivation underlying the trial judge's action.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment prohibiting the judges and district attorney from retrying the accused.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- former jeopardy -- proceeding in nature of prohibition is traditional remedy where double jeopardy is claimed to exist -- in trial without jury, after first witness testified, Judge declared mistrial because petitioner's father had once called his chambers about petitioner; such basis for mistrial was insufficient; retrial would subject petitioner to double jeopardy.

1. A proceeding under article 78 CPLR in the nature of prohibition is the traditional remedy where double jeopardy is claimed to exist. Petitioner should not be compelled to submit to a retrial and there raise the defense of double jeopardy.

2. The morning after petitioner's trial before a Judge without a jury opened with a police witness, the Trial Judge held a conference in chambers with opposing counsel and announced that he recalled petitioner's father having telephoned the Judge's chambers and had been told [***2] to consult his lawyer. The Judge then stated that "perhaps it would be improper for me to adjudicate this matter" and that "the recalling of this incident could in some conceivable way have an [effect] upon my judgment". He thereupon declared a mistrial. Such "iffy" reasons for declaring a mistrial are unacceptable especially since the Trial Judge was told that petitioner would oppose a retrial on the ground of double jeopardy. Petitioner is entitled to judgment prohibiting respondents from retrying him.

 

841 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


S. E. Nichols Herkimer Corp. v. Herkimer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


38 A.D.2d 456; 330 N.Y.S.2d 747; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4843



April 19, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant department store operator sought review of a decision from the Supreme Court of Onondaga County (New York), which granted respondent village's motion for summary judgment in its action against the village to have an ordinance restricting the business hours of establishments on certain holidays declared invalid.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that the five-hour restriction on sales from 10:30 a.m. to 3:30 p.m. was a valid exercise of the village's power and that the ordinance had a real and substantial relationship to a proper and orderly observance of the holidays. Upon review, the court agreed with the trial court's decision. The ordinance was specifically authorized by N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 86 and was not prohibitory in scope. Also, the operator failed to prove that the ordinance bore no rational relationship to the end sought by the village. The restriction on sales for a period of five hours was reasonably related to the observance of the two national holidays and, therefore, did not amount to a taking of property without due process of law. The restriction of sales businesses and not others did not deny the operator equal protection of the law. While the ordinance could have regulated other businesses, there was no necessity that it did so.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the village in the operator's action to have the ordinance declared invalid.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- power to enact ordinance -- ordinance enacted by defendant village making it unlawful, with stated exceptions, to offer articles of property for sale on Memorial Day or Independence Day between 10:30 A.M. and 3:30 P.M. specifically authorized by section 86 of General Municipal Law -- ordinance does not amount to deprivation of due process -- meaning of word "souvenirs" not vague -- no denial of equal protection of law.

1. Section 86 of the General Municipal Law grants the municipalities in the State power to enact "ordinances regulating the nature of commercial and business activities that may be conducted on Memorial day and Independence day and the hours within which * * * business activities may be conducted". Pursuant to such authority, defendant village enacted an ordinance on June 7, 1971 which provided, in part, that, with stated exceptions, "It shall be unlawful, in the Village of Herkimer, New York, for any [***2] person, firm or corporation to sell or offer for sale any article of property on Memorial Day or Independence Day during the hours of 10:30 a.m. and 3:30 p.m." The ordinance is specifically authorized by section 86 of the General Municipal Law and is not prohibitory in scope. ( Herkimer Corp. v. Village of Herkimer, 34 A D 2d 371, distinguished.)

2. In determining that an ordinance amounts to a deprivation of due process, it must be found that the law is unreasonable, arbitrary or capricious, and that the means selected have no real and substantial relation to the object sought to be obtained. Ordinances have a strong presumption of validity and the burden of proof to show that they bear no rational relationship to the end sought is on the person challenging the law. No such proof is present. The restriction on sales for a period of five hours is reasonably related to the observance of these two national holidays and, therefore, does not amount to a taking of property without due process of law.

3. The ordinance is not unconstitutional because the word "souvenirs" is vague and indefinite. The meaning of the word "souvenirs" is clear.

4. Restricting sales businesses and [***3] not restricting other businesses does not deny plaintiff equal protection of the law.

 

842 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jeffreys v. Jeffreys


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


38 A.D.2d 431; 330 N.Y.S.2d 550; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5045



April 3, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Kings County, dated December 26, 1968, reversed insofar as appealed from, without costs, and plaintiff's original motion to compel payment by the City of New York of the expense of service by publication of the summons in this action denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York) that held that the city had to bear the expense of the publication of plaintiff indigent's summons for divorce against defendant husband.


OVERVIEW: The indigent obtained an order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1101 that granted her permission to proceed as a poor person. The indigent sought to obtain a divorce. The indigent was permitted to serve her husband by publication, with the city bearing the expense. The city sought reconsideration of its consent to service by publication on the indigent's husband with the city bearing the expense. The city argued that in absence of specific statutory or constitutional authorization, it was forbidden to make the payment requested even though the payment had to be made from public funds. The court reversed the trial court's judgment. The court determined that it was the responsibility of the state and not the city to pay for the cost of service on the indigent's husband.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that ordered the city to pay the expense of the indigent's service by publication. The court denied the indigent's motion to compel payment by the city for publication of the summons.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- divorce -- cost of service of summons by publication -- burden of paying cost of service by publication of summons in indigent woman's action for divorce has not been imposed by specific statute on City of New York and hence, obligation is that of State -- issue is not moot and will be decided even though city has already paid such cost since issue will recur and is of major importance.

1. By requiring payment of the cost of service by publication of an action for divorce, a State unconstitutionally [***2] denies access to the courts by indigent persons ( Boddie v. Connecticut, 401 U.S. 371). In an action for divorce by an indigent woman requiring service of the summons by publication, since there is no specific statutory authority imposing such cost on the City of New York, an order of Special Term which imposed that cost on the city is reversed. ( McCandless v. McCandless, 38 A D 2d 171, disapproved.) Until the Legislature determines that aid to the needy for this purpose shall be provided by the city, the obligation to pay such cost lies with the State.

2. In this case although the city paid the cost of service by publication and is not likely to sue plaintiff to recover that expense, such payment does not make the matter moot. The issue affects many indigents similarly situated, will arise again, is one of major importance and will therefore be entertained and decided.

Levine v. Long Island R. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


38 A.D.2d 936; 331 N.Y.S.2d 451; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5221



March 3, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law, by (1) striking therefrom all the decretal paragraphs, except the third, which denied the cross motion to dismiss the complaint, and (2) providing, in lieu thereof that it is adjudged that (a) the portion of the fare scheduled promulgated by defendants on January 21, 1972 which declared that 10-trip tickets issued prior thereto would be honored only until midnight of March 1, 1972, after which refunds on unused tickets would be made on a pro rata basis reflecting the number of unused trips is valid and constitutional and (b) plaintiffs have no right to use previously purchased 10-trip tickets [***2] henceforth. As so modified, judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant railroad sought review of a decision from the Supreme Court of Nassau County (New York), which declared the 10-trip tickets issued to plaintiff interested passengers valid, enjoined the railroad from refusing to honor the tickets, and denied the railroad's cross-motion to dismiss the complaint. The railroad asserted that the trial court erred in issuing an injunction and finding the tickets valid.


OVERVIEW: A decision was made by the Metropolitan Transit Authority (MTA), which owned and operated the railroad, that its 10-trip tickets were going to be discontinued and would no longer be honored after March 1, 1972. Interested passengers filed a complaint seeking a declaratory judgment that the10-trip tickets were valid, enjoining the railroad from refusing to honor the tickets, and denying the railroad's cross-motion to dismiss their complaint. The passengers asserted that the railroad was required to hold additional public hearing on the issue of changing its tariff schedule so as to dishonor the previously sold tickets. The trial court noted that a new hearing was not necessary, issued the declaration, and the railroad appealed. The court affirmed the judgment as modified to strike the declarations that the tickets were valid and enjoining the railroad from refusing to honor them after March 1, 1972. The railroad was allowed to implement its original tariff rescheduling plan because the court held that to rule otherwise would seriously impair the railroad's and MTA's ability to adjust fares as needed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment as modified to strike the declarations that the tickets were valid and enjoin the railroad from refusing to honor them.

In re Abrams


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


38 A.D.2d 334; 329 N.Y.S.2d 364; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5222



March 2, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Respondent's name struck from the roll of attorneys and counselors at law in the State of New York.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, Co-ordinating Committee on Discipline of the Association of the Bar of the City of New York, New York County Lawyers' Association, and Bronx County Bar Association, initiated disciplinary proceedings against respondent attorney seeking to strike his name from the roll of attorneys, pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 90(4), for making filing false instruments, which was a felony under N.Y. Penal Law § 2 and N.Y. Penal Law § 2501.


OVERVIEW: The attorney filed false instruments, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 2 and N.Y. Penal Law § 2501. The Bar Associations initiated disciplinary proceedings against him, seeking to strike his name from the roll of attorneys, pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 90(4). The court determined that where the attorney had been convicted of violating 18 U.S.C.S. § 2 and 18 U.S.C.S. § 1001, he was considered to have violated N.Y. Penal Law § 2 and N.Y. Penal Law § 2501. The court held that because the attorney had been convicted of the felonies, he ceased to be an attorney and counselor at law. The court granted judgment in favor of the Bar Associations and struck the attorney's name from the roll of attorneys.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment in favor of the Bar Associations and struck the attorney's name from the roll of attorneys after he committed felonies of filing false instruments.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- disciplinary proceedings -- respondent, attorney at law, convicted in Federal court of willfully making false statements in affidavit and application to extend client's temporary stay in this country (cf. U. S. Code, tit. 18, §§ 2, 1001) was guilty of felony under New York law and his name is stricken from roll of attorneys (Judiciary Law, § 90, subd. 4).

1. Respondent, attorney at law, was convicted in United States District Court of willfully making false statements in two documents -- an affidavit and an application to extend a client's temporary stay in this country -- submitted by him to the United States Immigration and Naturalization Service, in violation of sections 2 and 1001 [***2] of title 18 of the United States Code. An attorney so convicted is considered to have been convicted of a crime cognizable as a felony under sections 2 and 2051 of the former New York Penal Law ( Matter of Mantzaris, 37 A D 2d 308).

2. Although section 90 (subd. 4) of the Judiciary Law provides for automatic disbarment upon conviction, respondent's constitutional guarantee of due process was safeguarded by his jury trial and appellate review. Pursuant to that statute, respondent ceases to be an attorney and counselor at law. Accordingly, his name is stricken from the roll of attorneys.
