People v. Keough


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


38 A.D.2d 293; 329 N.Y.S.2d 80; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5294



February 25, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who ran a student newspaper, challenged the decision of the Monroe County Court (New York), which convicted them of disorderly conduct. Defendants printed, publicly displayed, and distributed a publication containing photographs which "defied and cast contempt upon" the American flag, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 20.00 and N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 136(d), (f).


OVERVIEW: Defendants ran a student newspaper. The newspaper published and distributed photographs depicting a nude female with the flag draped from her posterior, tightly enfolded in and through the otherwise naked pubic area, with the terminus of the flag partially covering but one of her naked breasts. The trial court convicted defendants of disorderly conduct for violations of N.Y. Penal Law § 20.00 and N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 136(d), (f). The court affirmed the convictions, holding that a legitimate public interest to be protected was threatened by the violations of which defendants were charged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the students' convictions for disorderly conduct.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- defilement of flag -- section 136 of General Business Law is constitutional -- section not erroneously applied to defendants who published photograph showing flag draped about naked body of female defendant -- indictment, which charged violation of section 136, was not required to charge breach of peace to warrant defendants' plea of guilty to charge of disorderly conduct -- printing of photograph and flag not authorized by provision of section 136 that section does not apply to periodical on which flag is printed -- no showing of discrimination in enforcement.

1. Section 136 of the General Business Law which makes it a misdemeanor for any person to defile the flag of the United States of America is constitutional.

2. Said section was not erroneously applied to defendants who caused to be printed in a college periodical a photograph depicting the nude female defendant with the flag draped from her posterior tightly folded in and through the otherwise naked [***2] pubic area with the terminus of the flag partially covering one of her naked breasts.

3. Although the indictment charged defendants with violation of section 20.00 of the Penal Law and section 136 of the General Business Law, they pleaded guilty to the reduced charge of disorderly conduct. It was not required that the indictment charge either a breach of the peace or that there was a present danger of such a breach.

4. The printing of the photograph and flag was not authorized by a provision of section 136 that the section not apply to any periodical on which a flag is printed.

5. There has been no showing of a conscious or intentional discrimination in enforcement of the section.

 

846 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Byrn v. New York City Health & Hospitals Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


38 A.D.2d 316; 329 N.Y.S.2d 722; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5315



February 24, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Queens County, entered January 7, 1972, reversed, on the law, without costs; plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction denied; cross motion of defendant [***2] New York City Health & Hospitals Corporation to vacate an order of the same court, entered December 3, 1971, granted; and case remanded to the Special Term for entry of a judgment in favor of defendants declaring the rights of the parties.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, hospitals and attorney-general, challenged the issuance of a preliminary injunction by the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York), which enjoined defendant hospitals from performing any abortions pending final determination of the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 125.05(3).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff individual was appointed guardian ad litem for a class of unborn infants of less than 24 weeks' gestation scheduled for abortion. Plaintiff filed suit to challenge the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 125.05(3), which permitted abortion within 24 weeks of conception. The trial court issued a temporary injunction prohibiting all abortions not necessary to preserve the life of the female. The court reversed and remanded for entry of judgment in favor of defendants, hospitals and attorney-general. The court found no error in the appointment of plaintiff as guardian ad litem when considering the lack of time to try the merits and the inherent conflict of interest between the mother and fetus. The court found the class adequately defined and not overbroad. However, the court concluded that the provisions of U.S. Const. amend. V, providing that no "person" would be deprived of life without due process was not intended to include fetal life. The statute was not found to be unconstitutional beyond a reasonable doubt. The court stated that the determination of the extent to which fetal life should be protected was a value judgment best made by the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of a preliminary injunction prohibiting all abortions not necessary to preserve the life of the female and remanded for entry of judgment in favor of defendants, hospitals and attorney-general. The Fifth Amendment's protections did not extend to fetal life and the state statute permitting abortions within 24 weeks' gestation was not unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- justifiable abortional acts -- subdivision 3 of section 125.05 of Penal Law, as amended to add provision that abortional act is justifiable when committed upon female with her consent by duly licensed physician acting within 24 weeks after commencement of her pregnancy, is constitutional -- there is reasonable basis for it and it violates no constitutional right of fetus of less than 24 weeks' gestation -- ex parte order appointing plaintiff, who challenges constitutionality of statute, guardian ad litem of class of unborn infants of less than 24 weeks' gestation, scheduled for abortions in public hospitals, was proper when made, but should now be vacated.

1. Subdivision 3 of section 125.05 of the Penal Law, as amended in 1970 (L. 1970, ch. 127) to add a provision that an abortional act is justifiable when committed upon a female with her consent by a duly licensed physician acting within 24 weeks after the commencement [***3] of her pregnancy, is constitutional. There is a reasonable basis for it and it violates no constitutional right of a fetus of less than 24 weeks' gestation.

2. The present action, challenging the constitutionality of the statute upon the ground that such a fetus was a person within the protection of the constitutional guarantees of due process and equal protection of the laws, was commenced by plaintiff after he procured an ex parte order appointing him guardian ad litem of a class of unborn infants of less than 24 weeks' gestation scheduled for abortions in public hospitals. That order was proper when made. However, in view of the determination of the substantive issue, now made upon an appeal from an order which had granted plaintiff a preliminary injunction against the performing of abortions other than those necessary to preserve the life of the female, and which had denied a cross motion for vacatur of the order of appointment, it should be vacated.

 

847 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


American Locker Co. v. Gallman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


38 A.D.2d 105; 327 N.Y.S.2d 973; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5548



January 20, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, with costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer appealed from the determination of respondent state tax commission (commission) (New York), which found that the taxpayer was principally engaged in the business of storing tangible personal property not held for sale in the regular course of business and that the receipts from these services were taxable sales of services within the meaning and intent of N.Y. Tax Law § 1105(4)(c).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer owned patented, coin-controlled lockers in which baggage and other articles for personal property were stored by the public. The contracts with the operators of the lockers provided that any taxes levied or assessed on the conduct, maintenance, operation, or gross receipts of the locker service were borne by the taxpayer and each operator in the same percentage as they shared the gross revenues. The commission imposed a sales tax against the taxpayer on all revenues flowing through the lockers. On appeal, the court affirmed the commission's determination. The short term checking of baggage was storage of tangible personal property within the meaning of § 1105. The contractual relationship between the taxpayer and the operators was encompassed within the statute, and the taxpayer was a vendor who was liable for the sales tax within the meaning of § 1101.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination of the commission that assessed a sales tax against the taxpayer for all revenues flowing through the locker rental service.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- sales tax -- petitioner, owner of coin-controlled lockers for storage of baggage leased them to owners of air, bus and railroad terminals; revenues are collected by owners who pay percentage to petitioner; Commissioner of Taxation determined that petitioner was vendor liable for sales tax; determination is confirmed -- tax applies to "storing" of personal property, even though for short time, in petitioner's lockers -- sales tax is not unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce -- petitioner's agreements do not create nontaxable sale for resale -- petitioner is "vendor" within Tax Law, liable for tax -- petitioner cannot claim exemption as agency of government or charitable organization.

1. Petitioner, the owner of coin-controlled lockers in which baggage and other articles are stored for a 24-hour period or less, leases them to the owners [***2] of air, bus and railroad terminals. The revenues are collected by such owners who pay a percentage of the gross to petitioner. Respondent Tax Commission determined that petitioner, as vendor, was principally engaged in the business of storing tangible personal property not held for sale in the regular course of business and that the receipts from these services were taxable sales of services within paragraph (4) of subdivision (c) of section 1105 of the Tax Law. Such determination is confirmed.

2. Section 1105 (subd. [c], par. [4]) provides that a tax is imposed upon the "receipts from every sale, except for resale", of the service of "storing all tangible personal property not held for sale in the regular course of business and the rental of safe deposit boxes or similar space". There is no valid distinction between "checking" and "storing". The term "storing" within said section does not necessarily connote such duration as to preclude its application here, and there is no reason to limit its application to long-term custody. When a patron deposits his tangible personal property within one of petitioner's lockers, he is storing his property even though only for a limited [***3] duration.

3. A sales tax on the use of locker storage facilities is not an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce and freedom to travel.

4. The contractual agreements between petitioner and the various owners of air, bus and rail terminals do not create nontaxable sales for resale.

5. The Tax Law renders the vendor of the service responsible for the collection of the tax and liable for the tax imposed. It defines a vendor as including one who makes "sales of tangible personal property or services" (§ 1101, subd. [b], par. [8], subpar. [i], cl. [A]) or one "who solicits business either by employees, independent contractors, agents or other representatives * * * and by reason thereof makes sales * * * of tangible personal property" (§ 1101, subd. [b], par. [8], subpar. [i], cl. [C]). The contractual relationships established between petitioner and the various owners are of the type encompassed by such definition and petitioner, rather than the owners, is the vendor.

6. Section 1101 (subd. [b], par. [8], subpar. [i], cl. [E]) of the Tax Law provides that the State, its agencies and political subdivisions are to be considered vendors when such an [***4] entity sells services or property of a kind ordinarily sold by a private person. Section 1116 (subd. [a], par. [1]) grants an exemption to a government agency or charitable organization where the sale is "by or to" such agency. Since petitioner is the vendor, it cannot claim any such exemption.

 

848 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gorenberg v. Onondaga County Bd. of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


38 A.D.2d 145; 328 N.Y.S.2d 198; 1972 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5552



January 20, 1972 



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant students challenged the judgments of the Supreme Court, Onondaga County, and Supreme Court, Oneida County (New York), which dismissed their complaints against respondents, board of elections and commissioner of elections. The trial courts held that N.Y. Elec. Law §151 was valid and did not contravene the students' voting rights.


OVERVIEW: The students, who attended colleges in the two counties, argued that § 151, which required the students to prove they were residents of the counties, violated due process of the Fourteenth Amendment, the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions, and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1971(a)(2)(A), which prohibited different standards to be applied to various classes of voters. The trial courts dismissed the students' complaints, and on appeal, the court affirmed the dismissals. The students were entitled to the same treatment and test as any other voter and a determination of residency was to be made on grounds no more stringent or restrictive than any other. Under N.Y. Elec. Law § 151, the students were required to provide evidence of bona fide residences just like any other voter. Section 151 stated that for purposes of registering and voting, no person gained or lost a residence by his presence of absence while a student at any institution of learning; a student on the campus of an educational institution did not, without more, accomplish a change of voting residence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that dismissed the student's complaint, which challenged the constitutionality of a statute that required voters to provide evidence of residency and that stated that the students did not accomplish a change of voting residence by being at the colleges.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- students' right to vote -- Voting Rights Act of 1970 gave 18-year-olds right to vote; section 151 of Election Law which states that person does not gain or lose residence by reason of presence or absence while student and that student must present statement to prove residence is not unconstitutional; student is treated same as any other voter.

Section 150 of the Election Law provides that a person may vote if he "shall have been a resident of this state, and of the county, city or village for three months next preceding an election and has been duly registered in the election district of his residence". Section 151 provides that for "the purpose of registering [***2] and voting, no person shall be deemed to have gained or lost a residence by reason of his presence or absence * * * while a student of any institution of learning; * * * Any person applying for registration who claims to [be a student] shall file with the board * * * a written statement showing where he actually resides and where he claims to be legally domiciled". The Voting Rights Act of 1970 (U. S. Code, tit. 42, § 1973 aa) enfranchised 18-year-olds for Federal, State and local elections. In these proceedings by 18-year-old students challenging the constitutionality of section 151, they are entitled, under the statute, to the same treatment and test as any other voter and a determination of their residency is to be made on grounds no more stringent or restrictive than any other voter. If a student seeks to register in his educational community, he should be required, just as everyone else, to present evidence of a bona fide residence. The Voting Rights Act was not intended to grant students a preference over any other citizen. The requirement in section 151 that a voter present an affidavit as to his residence is not constitutionally discriminatory. Mere presence in a district [***3] does not create a presumption of voter residence.

 Gorenberg v. Onondaga County Bd. of Elections, 67 Misc 2d 766, affirmed.

 

849 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Desmond v. Socha


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


38 A.D.2d 22; 327 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2602



December 22, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order reversed, on the law and the facts, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant client sought review of an order from the Supreme Court for Schenectady County (New York), which, in an action brought by plaintiff attorney to collect attorney fees, amended and modified a prior order and fixed the attorney's fee and granted him a lien on certain real property owned by the client pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 475.


OVERVIEW: The attorney successfully represented the client in a contest that started when the client applied to the town board for a zoning permit to erect multiple family dwellings on his land. The representation lasted two years and resulted in a ruling that the zoning ordinance was unconstitutional as it affected the client's property. The supreme court awarded attorney fees and imposed a charging lien upon the determinations, decisions, orders and judgments rendered and the specific property benefitted thereby. On review the court reversed and held that before there could have been a charging lien there had to be something awarded by the judgment to which the lien could have attached. The court noted that where the attorney's services did not create any proceeds but consisted solely of defending a title or interest already held by the client, there was no lien on the realty. The court noted that the client's property did not become the proceeds of the litigation or of the resulting judgment and the attorney had to bring a plenary action to determine and recover his fee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the supreme court's judgment and dismissed the action because there was no valid charging lien when the client's property did not become the proceeds of the litigation.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- lien -- fees -- attorney successfully represented client who had applied for zoning permit with result that zoning ordinance was held unconstitutional as it affected client's property -- no right to lien or determination of fees in proceeding under section 475 of Judiciary Law -- attorney's services did not create proceeds but merely defended title or interest held by client -- plenary action to recover fee necessary.

1. An attorney successfully represented his client in a contest which started when the client applied for a zoning permit to erect multiple family dwellings and ended two years later when the zoning ordinance was held unconstitutional as it affected the client's property. In this proceeding pursuant to section 475 of the Judiciary Law, Special Term erred in awarding the attorney a sum of money [***2] for legal services and imposing a charging lien upon the client's real property to secure that sum. An attorney may have a lien on books or papers in his possession or upon a cause of action and the proceeds of it. The statute (Judiciary Law, § 475) allows the attorney to secure payment of his fee by satisfying it from the fund created by his efforts. Before there can be a charging lien, however, there must be something awarded by the judgment to which the lien can attach. Where the attorney's services do not create any proceeds but consist solely of defending a title or interest already held by the client, there is no lien on the realty. Here, there was nothing recovered or created by the attorney's effort to which the lien could attach.

2. If there is no charging lien under section 475 of the Judiciary Law, the attorney must bring a plenary action to determine and recover his fee. The court is without power to summarily determine the amount of the fee under the statute.

 

850 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


College of New Rochelle v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


37 A.D.2d 461; 326 N.Y.S.2d 765; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2714



December 13, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law and the facts, so as to delete the third decretal paragraph which remanded the matter to the Commissioner for further proceedings, and to grant the petition, and, as so modified, affirmed, with costs to the College of New Rochelle.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties cross appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, which set aside a determination by respondent Commissioner of Education that petitioner college was not eligible for state aid pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6401 and remanded the matter to respondent for further proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The court initially determined that the Commissioner's determination that the college was not eligible for state aid was an administrative decision and that the procedure followed by the Commissioner was reasonable and not arbitrary. The court next turned to whether the N.Y. Constitution prohibited state aid to the college as provided by N.Y. Educ. Law § 6401. The court determined that the college's practice of prayer together was not sufficient so as to conclude that a denominational tenet or doctrine was taught to a degree sufficient to proscribe state aid. Moreover, the court concluded that the college was not an institution wholly or in part under the control or direction of a religious denomination. Finally, neither the state constitution nor the federal constitution required that the college be denied state aid. There was not such a degree of entanglement as would violate the First Amendment. The court concluded that the college was an institution whose predominant higher education mission was to provide its students with a secular education. As such it should not have been denied state aid. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment was modified.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment, on the law and the facts, so as to delete the third decretal paragraph which remanded the matter to the Commissioner for further proceedings and to grant the petition and, as so modified, affirmed, with costs to the college.


HEADNOTES

Colleges and universities -- eligibility for State aid -- petitioner, women's college founded and operated by Ursuline Nuns, was neither  [***2] "school * * * in which any denominational tenet or doctrine is taught" nor "under the control or direction of any religious denomination" so as to be ineligible for public moneys under "Blaine Amendment" (N. Y. Const., art. XI, § 3), nor would State aid to petitioner advance or inhibit religion or result in excessive entanglement of State with religion in violation of United States Constitution (1st Amdt.); accordingly determination of respondent Commissioner of Education holding petitioner constitutionally ineligible for State aid (Education Law, § 6401) was arbitrary and capricious and was properly set aside.

1. Petitioner College of New Rochelle, a liberal arts college for women founded by the order of Ursuline Nuns who share in its administration and comprise part of its board of trustees and faculty, cannot be said to teach a doctrine of a particular religious denomination exclusively nor is it so controlled or directed by a religious denomination as to attempt to, or actually, inculcate the doctrine and faith of the denomination. Accordingly it was neither a "school or institution of learning wholly or in part under the control or direction of any religious denomination"  [***3] nor one "in which any denominational tenet or doctrine is taught" in aid of which the "Blaine Amendment" (N. Y. Const., art. XI, § 3) prohibits the use of public moneys. Section 6401 of the Education Law which authorizes respondent Commissioner of Education to apportion and pay State aid to private institutions of higher learning, provides (subd. 2, par. d) that the institution must be eligible for State aid under provisions of the State and Federal Constitutions. A determination by respondent Commissioner that petitioner was not eligible under the State Constitution for State aid pursuant to section 6401 was arbitrary and capricious and, in a CPLR article 78 proceeding was properly set aside by Special Term. However, since petitioner should not have been denied State aid, Special Term should have granted the petition rather than remanding the matter to respondent for further proceedings.

2. Furthermore, State aid to petitioner would neither advance nor inhibit religion. Moreover, there would result no such degree of excessive entanglement of the State with religion as would violate the First Amendment. Petitioner, accordingly, is eligible also for such aid under the provisions [***4] of the United States Constitution.

3. The procedure followed by respondent Commissioner, including a request that petitioner fill out and return a pertinent questionnaire as to its purposes, policies, governance, faculty, student body, curricula and programs and an invitation to petitioner's representatives to meet with respondent's staff to ensure that all information was current, complete, and accurate, was reasonable and not arbitrary. Inasmuch as respondent's determination, made pursuant to authority delegated to him by the Legislature, was administrative rather than quasi-judicial, no adversary hearing was required so long as the determination had a rational basis and was reasonable and fair.

 

851 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rabin v. Onondaga County Bd. of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


37 A.D.2d 471; 326 N.Y.S.2d 599; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2834



December 2, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York), which dismissed the petitions challenging the rule requiring residency in the county for three months before the voters could register to vote.


OVERVIEW: The voters had not lived in Onondaga County for three months but still attempted to register to vote for an upcoming election. Their applications were refused because they did not meet the residency requirements. The voters brought an action challenging the three-month residency requirement contained in N.Y. Const. art. II, § 1 and N.Y. Elec. Law § 150. The trial court upheld the residency requirement and dismissed the voters' petitions. The voters sought review. On appeal, the court found that the proper standard of review was the reasonable basis test and the laws passed the test. The court found that the requirement tended to insure that new members of a community would have a genuine stake in that community and would become familiar with local problems and issues before exercising their right to vote.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court rejecting the voters' challenge to the residency rule.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- residency requirement -- constitutional law -- New York State Constitution (art. II, § 1) and Election Law ( § 150), which provide that citizen of New York State is not qualified to vote if he is not resident of county, city or village for three months next preceding election, are not in violation of Fourteenth Amendment to United States Constitution.

Section 1 of article II of the New York State Constitution and section 150 of the Election Law, which provide that a citizen of the State of New York is not qualified to vote if he is not a resident "of the county, city or village for three months next preceding an election", are not in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution. The test to be applied is whether the three-month residency requirement is reasonable (cf. Drueding v. Devlin, 234 F. Supp. 721, affd. 380 U.S. 125). [***2] The requirement has reasonable basis. It tends to insure that new members of a community will have a genuine stake in that community and will become familiar with local problems and issues. But even the stricter standard of a compelling State interest is satisfied, the Legislature having deemed that the three-month residency requirement is necessary to prevent fraud.

 

852 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York State Water Resources Comm'n v. Liberman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


37 A.D.2d 484; 326 N.Y.S.2d 284; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2872; 3 ERC (BNA) 1910; 2 ELR 20470



December 1, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Order entered October 7, 1968 modified, on the law and the facts, so as to dismiss the first, third, fourth, fifth, and seventh defenses, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs; order entered November 4, 1968 affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state water commission sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Tompkins County (New York), which denied the state water commission's motion to dismiss the first, third, fourth, and fifth defenses in the respondent landowner's answer. The landowner sought review of the trial court decision that granted the water commission's motion to dismiss its second defense.


OVERVIEW: The landowner sought to build a boat marina adjacent to a lake. Although the landowner had not received a grant of land under the lake's navigable waters, he placed fill in order to build the marina. The state water commission brought an action against the landowner for violations of N.Y. Conserv. Law § 429-b and sought an injunction and civil penalties. The landowner asserted seven affirmative defenses, which the state water commission sought to have dismissed. The trial court denied the motion as to all but one ground, and the state water commission appealed. The court modified the order. The court held that the landowner's assertion that the action was time-barred should have been dismissed. The court said that the six-year statute of limitations for spoliation or misappropriation of state property found in N.Y. C.P.L.R. 213(5) and N.Y. Exec. Law § 63-c applied only to actions that involved personal property. The court also held that the affirmative defense that the state water commission sought retrospective application of the Conservation Law should have been dismissed because the state sought penalties only for actions that took place after the statute's effective date.


OUTCOME: The court modified an order that refused to dismiss the landowner's affirmative defenses against the state water commission.


HEADNOTES

Waters and watercourses -- banning of fill in lake -- in action to enjoin defendant from placing fill in lake adjacent to his property, defense that action is barred by CPLR 213 (subd. 5) must be dismissed; subdivision does not apply to real property -- defenses based on premise that plaintiff is seeking penalties for violations which occurred prior to effective date of section 630 of Conservation Law are dismissed -- section 429-b of Conservation Law requiring permit  [***2] before placing of fill in navigable waters is constitutional and defenses based on defendant's ownership of land are dismissed -- defense that State was guilty of laches in commencing action was properly dismissed -- permission to amend answer to allege discriminatory enforcement of Conservation Law was properly granted.

1. In an action to enjoin defendant from further placing of fill in the navigable waters of Cayuga Lake without first obtaining a permit to do so as required by section 429-b of the Conservation Law and its predecessor section 31 of the Navigation Law, a first defense that the cause of action is barred by subdivision 5 of CPLR 213, which provides that an action by the State founded on the "spoliation or other misappropriation of public property" must be commenced within six years, must be dismissed since such provision applies only to personal property and not to real property.

2. A cause of action alleges that defendant violated the provisions of section 630 of the Conservation Law and by reason thereof has become liable for the penalties prescribed therefor. Defenses based on the premise that plaintiff is seeking penalties for violations which occurred prior [***3] to the effective date of the statute are dismissed since the only violations are those which occurred after the effective date of the penalty statute.

3. The public interest in, and the necessity for, regulation of the water resources of the State in the manner provided by section 429-b of the Conservation Law render the statute constitutional as within the police power of the State. The enforcement of the permit requirements of section 429-b is a proper method for regulating rights under the State's police power and does not constitute a taking of private property for public use. Since the State has the power to regulate waters even when title to the land under water is in private ownership, the issue of title is not involved in the action. Defenses that defendant has title to the lands and that his property has been taken for public use without just compensation should be dismissed.

4. A defense that plaintiff was guilty of laches in commencing the action was properly dismissed. Limitations of time within which the State must act must be statutory, and laches does not apply against the State in respect to its right to recover and protect property held in trust for the people.

 [***4] 5. Permission to amend defendant's answer, to allege discriminatory enforcement of the Conservation Law against him, based on facts in his affidavit, was properly granted by Special Term.

 

853 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Blodgett


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 1035; 326 N.Y.S.2d 14; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2937



November 24, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the County Court of Broome County (New York), which convicted him of sexual abuse in the first degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 130.65.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant claimed that § 130.65 was unconstitutional because the term "intimate parts" was vague and uncertain. The court affirmed his conviction. The court found that under § 130.0(3) "sexual contact" meant any touching of the sexual or other intimate parts of a person for the purpose of gratifying sexual desire of either party. The legislature intended the phrase "sexual contact" not to be restricted to reproductive organs. The language was neither vague nor uncertain. Defendant's guilt was established beyond a reasonable doubt, and there was ample corroborative proof.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of sexual abuse in the first degree.

People v. Gilmore


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 912; 325 N.Y.S.2d 455; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3154



October 28, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Erie County Court (New York), which sentenced defendant to prison for attempted assault in the second degree. Defendant had been previously sentenced by a city court to the care and custody of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission (NACC) after defendant's conviction of a misdemeanor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his sentence to prison while he was serving a sentence under the custody and control of the NACC. Defendant also challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 208. The court held that the interruption of the NACC sentence by sending defendant to prison was not authorized. The court modified the judgment of the trial court by deferring imprisonment of defendant on the felony charge until he had completed the sentence imposed by the city court to the custody and control of the NACC. The court rejected defendant's claim that N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 208 was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the trial court and, as modified, affirmed.

 

855 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pratt v. Tofany


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 854; 326 N.Y.S.2d 257; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3290



October 18, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant motor vehicle commissioner appealed from the judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), in favor of plaintiff teen driver, declaring a portion of N.Y. Veh. and Traf. Law § 501(b)(1) unconstitutional insofar as it provided that junior operators' licenses did not entitle the licensees thereunder to operate a motor vehicle in Nassau County and denied the commissioner's motion to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: The teen driver challenged the restriction imposed upon his junior operator's license prohibiting him from driving within Nassau County. This restriction was authorized by N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 501(b)(1) which provided that such a license did not entitle a licensee to operate a motor vehicle in a city having a population of one million or more or in the county of Nassau, except, for the purpose of driving to and from a state approved cooperative work-study educational program. The trial court found the statute unconstitutional as a violation of equal protection. On appeal, the court reversed and dismissed, holding that there was a reasonable for the statute. Of the counties of the state outside of New York City, Nassau County was the most populous, had the most automobile registrations and the most accidents and, significantly, and a high population density. These factors constituted a reasonable basis upon which the Legislature, acting pursuant to its responsibilities in the critical area of highway safety, could have determined that driving in Nassau County was more hazardous and required greater skill, care and maturity than in any other county of the state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the teen driver in his action challenging the constitutionality of a motor vehicle statute.

 

856 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rab v. Colon


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 813; 324 N.Y.S.2d 809; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3333



October 7, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injury victim appealed from the order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) denying his motion for special preference pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3403(a)(4) in his personal injury action against defendant alleged tortfeasor.


OVERVIEW: When plaintiff was 75 years old, he filed a personal injury action against defendant. Plaintiff filed a motion for special preference, which the supreme court denied. Thereafter, plaintiff filed another motion for special preference pursuant to Rule 3403(a)(4), which was also denied. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that plaintiff was not entitled to special preference solely because of his age and that the supreme court had the inherent power to control its calendar and determine the order in which it disposed of cases. Further, Rule 3403(a)(4) did not give plaintiff the right to have his civil court action heard in a supreme court forum, and it only applied to cases that were properly on the supreme court calendar.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied the victim's motion for special preference. The court granted the victim leave to renew the application in the civil court if he chose to transfer his case to that court.

Vitale v. Cristenfeld


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 775; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3476



September 7, 1971 

 

858 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Paramount Film Distrib. Corp. v. State


Claim No. 45976


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


37 A.D.2d 226; 324 N.Y.S.2d 363; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3506



July 27, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law and the facts, so as to increase the award from $ 29,297 to $ 128,322.50, plus appropriate interest, and, as so modified, affirmed; order affirmed, with one bill of costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant-respondent, a licensing fee claimant, sought review of a judgment of the Court of Claims (New York) limiting its award of the movie licensing fees to the amount paid by the claimant, and excluding the fees paid by the claimant's parent corporation. Respondent-appellant filed a cross-appeal from an order of the Court of Claims (New York), which vacated a subpoena duces tecum.


OVERVIEW: The court previously affirmed a denial of a motion to dismiss the claimant's action for failure to state a cause of action and lateness upon finding that the New York Court of Appeals' declaration of the unconstitutionality of the movie licensing provisions of N.Y. Educ. Law § 120 et seq., was retroactive. Under this ruling, the license fees paid by the claimant were illegally collected and thus, refundable. In ruling on the cross-appeals, the court affirmed its adherence to the retroactive effect of the ruling to permit recovery by the claimant. Addressing the claimant's cross-appeal, the court awarded full recovery of the license fees, concluding that full recovery should not be denied on the basis that the majority of the licenses were granted in the name of the parent corporation. The court observed that the recording of the license fees in the parent corporation was contrary to a clause in a contract between the parent corporation and the claimant, a wholly owned subsidiary, requiring that all licenses be issued in the claimant's name. Finally, the court found no merit in the State's argument that the subpoena duces tecum was not properly vacated.


OUTCOME: The court modified the court of claim's judgment to increase the award to the total fees paid and, as modified, affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Theatres and shows -- motion pictures -- licenses -- determination in Matter of Trans-Lux Distr. Corp. v. Board of Regents of Univ. of State of N. Y. (16 N Y 2d 710) declaring movie licensing provisions of Education Law (§ 120 et seq.) unconstitutional should be applied retroactively to permit recovery of license fees paid by appellant-respondent -- full recovery of license fees should not be denied on basis that majority of licenses were granted in name of parent corporation contrary to clause in contract between parent corporation and appellant-respondent, wholly owned subsidiary, that all licenses would be issued in appellant-respondent's name.

1. The determination in Matter of Trans-Lux Distr.  [***2] Corp. v. Board of Regents of Univ. of State of N. Y. (16 N Y 2d 710) declaring the movie licensing provisions of the Education Law (§ 120 et seq.) unconstitutional should be applied retroactively to permit recovery of license fees paid by appellant-respondent.

2. The trial court awarded appellant-respondent only $ 29,297 of the total of $ 128,322.50 in fees paid because appellant-respondent and its parent corporation ignored a clause in their contract that all licenses be issued in appellant-respondent's name and instead took out a vast majority of the licenses in the name of the parent corporation. However, $ 128,322.50 in total fees were paid by appellant-respondent alone. Full recovery should not be denied on the basis solely of whether the licenses were granted in the name of the parent corporation or appellant-respondent, its wholly owned subsidiary.

Golden v. Planning Board of Ramapo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


37 A.D.2d 236; 324 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3510



July 23, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Special Term, dated November 13, 1970, reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law, without costs; section 46-13.1 of the Zoning Ordinance of the Town of Ramapo is declared unconstitutional and invalid; and petitioners' application for preliminary plat approval is remanded to respondent for consideration on the merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owner and developer, sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Special Term (New York), which dismissed appellants' petition for an order to annul a determination by respondent planning board concerning appellants' application for preliminary plat approval of a residential subdivision.


OVERVIEW: Appellants applied for a preliminary permit with the planning board for approval of a subdivision plat. The planning board denied the application and subsequently amended the zoning ordinance to include explicit standards for the issuance of a special permit. Appellants challenged the dismissal of their complaint that was filed for an order to annul the denial of their application for preliminary plat approval by the planning board. Appellants questioned the constitutional validity of an amendment to the zoning ordinance on the ground that the planning board exceeded its powers. The court reversed the lower court judgment and remanded for consideration of the case on the merits. The court held that the time controls placed on land use by the amendment were not legislatively authorized. The court concluded that the requirement of the special permit, along with the prerequisites that had to be satisfied before the permit could be obtained, were an usurpation of power by the planning board to regulate its population growth and such power had not been delegated to it.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court dismissal of appellants' complaint and remanded for consideration on the merits.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- zoning ordinance requiring special permit from Town Board in order to use subdivisions in residential zones for residential purposes is declared invalid -- since Town Board has appeared, court treats proceeding under CPLR article 78 as action for declaratory  [***2] judgment ( CPLR 103, subd. [c]).

1. A town zoning ordinance amendment provides that the owners or agents of any subdivision in any residential zone must obtain from the Town Board a special permit to use the subdivision for any residential purposes. The amendment further provides that the board shall not issue any such special permit unless the subdivision has at least 15 points computed on a sliding scale of development values based on (a) whether the following municipal facilities are or will be available to the subdivision: public sewers, public drainage, roads, firehouses, and parks or recreation or school sites, and (b) whether the town's 18-year plan to make such capital improvements throughout the town has reached the particular subdivision involved. Such a wide-ranging legislative scheme exceeds the zoning powers which the State has delegated to any individual town or suburb (cf. Town Law, §§ 261, 263). Nor is this ordinance saved by a provision, which the town has enacted as a local law, permitting a residential developer to apply for a reduction in assessed valuation resulting from such temporary restriction on the use of his land. Only the State Legislature can [***3] delegate powers to grant tax reductions (cf. N. Y. Const., art. XVI). This zoning ordinance amendment is declared invalid.

2. Although a proceeding under CPLR article 78 is not the proper vehicle to test the constitutionality of a legislative enactment, the Town Board has consented to appear and subject itself to this court's jurisdiction, and so the court treats this proceeding as an action for a declaratory judgment (cf. CPLR 103, subd. [c]).

Rockland County Builders Ass'n v. McAlevey


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 738; 324 N.Y.S.2d 190; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3515



July 23, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, builder's association and property owners (builder's association), appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Rockland County (New York), which granted summary judgment for defendants, town and its officials (town), in its action against the town to declare that Ramapo, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § 46-13.1 was unconstitutional after the town denied approval for a preliminary subdivision plat.


OVERVIEW: The town denied approval for a preliminary subdivision plat for a failure to obtain a special permit required under § 46-13.1. The builder's association filed an action against the town, seeking to declare § 46-13.1 unconstitutional. The trial court denied the motion for summary judgment filed by the builder's association but granted the town's motion for summary judgment, effectively dismissing the complaint. The builder's association appealed. The court found that the builder's association had standing to challenge the ordinance because the town had already denied approval for a preliminary subdivision plat and applying for a special permit would have been futile. The court found no justification for compelling the builder's association to go through the motions of applying for a special permit before attacking the constitutionality of a zoning ordinance, such as § 46-13.1. The court found that § 46-13.1 was unconstitutional on its face.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, which granted summary judgment for the town in the action by the builder's association to declare the zoning ordinance unconstitutional.

 

861 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Corning v. Donohue


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


37 A.D.2d 213; 323 N.Y.S.2d 206; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3519



July 15, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law and the facts, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a county board of elections and a state attorney general, challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which granted the petition of respondent mayor for an order of prohibition restraining the board of elections from placing the names of candidates for the board of education on the ballot. The trial court declared N.Y. Educ. Law § 2553(9) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The mayor asserted that § 2553(9) violated N.Y. Const. art. III, § 17, which prohibited the adoption by the legislature of a private or local bill relating to the opening and conducting of elections or designating places of voting. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court held as follows: 1) N.Y. Educ. Law art. 52 applied to the city because a decrease in population to fewer than 125,000 did not ipso facto remove city from the regulating scope of art. 52 and implementing legislation would have been required to have accomplished that result; 2) pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 1, the legislature had the authority to provide a system of education for the city; and 3) N.Y. Educ. Law § 2553(9) was general in nature and properly legislated the conditions in the city, furthering the over-all state purpose of education.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- appointed or elected boards of education -- chapter 462 of Laws of 1970 made Albany Board of Education elective, first election to be held at November, 1971 general election; chapter 492 of Laws of 1970 provided that, if city previously with population of 125,000 or more falls below 125,000 by 1970 census, it shall continue to be governed by article 52 of Education Law governing six large cities, until July 1,  [***2] 1971; decrease in population of Albany did not remove it from governing scope of article 52 -- chapter 462 does not conflict with section 17 of article III of Constitution forbidding passage of local law dealing with conducting of elections -- having candidates for same offices running on nonpartisan basis is not discriminatory.

1. Article 52 of the Education Law applies to the city school districts of Albany and five other cities with populations in excess of 125,000 people. Article 51 applies to cities of less than 125,000 inhabitants. By chapter 462 of the Laws of 1970, the Albany Board of Education was made elective subject to a referendum which was held and the citizens approved an elective board. Chapter 462 provided that the first election of members of the Albany Board of Education be held at the November, 1971 general election. Chapter 492 of the Laws of 1970 provided that the school district of a city with a population of over 125,000 by the 1960 census and a population of less than 125,000 as a result of the 1970 census, be governed by the provisions of article 52 of the Education Law until July 1, 1971. A decrease in population by the 1970 census to under 125,000 [***3] did not remove Albany from the regulating scope of article 52. Implementing legislation will be required to accomplish that result. The Legislature, by its prior enactments and by chapters 462 and 492 has expressed its clear intent to continue Albany within the purview of article 52. Chapter 492 provides that the law shall terminate as of July 1, 1971 and does not provide that thereafter article 51 shall apply to the City of Albany.

2. Section 17 of article III of the Constitution provides that the Legislature shall not pass a private or local bill relating to the "opening and conducting of elections or designating places of voting". Section 1 of article XI provides that the Legislature "shall provide for the maintenance and support of a system of free common schools". Chapter 462 of the Laws of 1970 does not conflict with section 17 of article III. The statute is not local in nature. There is reasonable basis for the establishment by the Legislature of both elected and appointed boards of education in different areas of the State. The Legislature is presumed to have been cognizant of any local conditions. Chapter 462 is general in nature and properly legislates the conditions [***4] in Albany, furthering the over-all State purpose of education.

3. Having candidates for the same office or group of offices running on a nonpartisan basis is not discriminatory.

New York v. Wyman


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 700; 322 N.Y.S.2d 957; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3576



July 1, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commissioner of Social Services (New York) issued a directive limiting Medicaid reimbursements for abortion to those that were medically indicated. Respondents, City of New York (New York) and Medicaid recipients, appealed to the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which annulled the directive. The Commissioner sought review.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner appealed a trial court order that annulled his directive limiting Medicaid reimbursement for abortions to those that were medically indicated and directed the Commissioner to continue to provide reimbursement for abortions authorized pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 125.05. On appeal, the court affirmed the order. Although disagreeing with the trial court's conclusion that the Commissioner's action violated the due process and equal protection provisions of the United States Constitution, the court found that the directive was contrary to the legislative intent and public policy expressed by § 125.05, which liberalized the state's abortion law, and that the directive should have been annulled as ultra vires. In addition, the court noted that the Commissioner had failed to comply with 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396a(d), which made the approval of the United States Secretary of Health, Education and Welfare a condition precedent to any modification of a state plan that reduced the scope or extent of medical assistance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order annulling the Commissioner's directive.

 

863 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Yevoli v. Cristenfeld


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


37 A.D.2d 153; 322 N.Y.S.2d 750; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3614



June 30, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Democratic County Committee and intervenor-defendant Republican County Committee (committees) sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which held that the committees' rules that no person could receive their designations unless they signed an agreement to refuse the nomination of any other party or independent body were invalid.


OVERVIEW: The committees both adopted rules that no person could receive their designations or nomination for political office, other than judicial, unless they signed an agreement to refuse the designation or nomination of any other party or independent body. Several members of one committee brought a proceeding for a judgment declaring its rule invalid and the other committee intervened in the proceeding and consented that the validity of its rule be adjudicated therein. The court affirmed the trial court's decision holding the rules invalid. The court determined that N.Y. Elec. Law § 15(2) required that a party's committee's rules be consistent with the Election law. Section § 248 contemplated multi-party nominations of a single candidate. In addition, the committees' rules contravened the basic philosophy of the democratic system, which forbid such undue impairment of the franchise. Voters were entitled to have equal, easy, and unrestricted opportunities to declare their choice for each office. Because it was unconstitutional for the legislature to prohibit a multi-party candidacy, it was certainly unconstitutional for the committees to seek that same result by the device of rules.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision holding held that the committees' rules that no person could receive their designations unless they signed an agreement to refuse the nomination of any other party or independent body were invalid.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- inhibition of cross indorsements -- Special Term properly treated proceeding to declare rule of Democratic County Committee inhibiting cross indorsement of candidates void, as one for declaratory judgment -- rule, which provided that no person would receive designation of county committee for office unless he signed agreement to refuse designation of any other party, contravenes general policy of Election Law and is void.

1. Special Term properly concluded that an article 78 CPLR proceeding was not appropriate to declare a rule of a Democratic County Committee that no person would receive its designation or nomination for political office unless he signed an agreement to refuse the designation or nomination of any other party or independent body, to be invalid, and that the proceeding, under CPLR 103, should [***2] be treated as an action for declaratory judgment.

2. Subdivision 4 of section 137 of the Election Law authorizes a party committee to permit the nomination of one not a member of that party and section 248 contemplates multi-party nominations of a single candidate. Since the general policy of the Election Law is to permit multi-party nominations, and the rule under attack forbids such cross-nominations, it is inconsistent with the Election Law and such rule is unconstitutional and void.

 

864 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dickens v. Ernesto


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


37 A.D.2d 102; 322 N.Y.S.2d 581; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3684



June 25, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, appellants, potential adoptive parents, sought an order declaring unconstitutional the religious affiliation requirements in adoption proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellants filed an application as adoptive parents in the department of social services. Upon being interviewed by the assigned caseworker, they were asked a question regarding their religious affiliation. Appellants stated that they had no intention of joining any kind of organized religion. Based solely on this lack of an expressed religious affiliation their application to become adopting parents was denied. Thereafter, appellants brought an art. 78 proceeding, seeking an order declaring unconstitutional the religious affiliation requirements in adoption proceedings. The court denied the request. The court found that the practice of the department when interviewing the natural mother was to inquire as to her religious preference for her child. The court noted that, while the laws relating to the custody of children were secular, in the exercise of such power the State could not ignore the religious preferences of the parents. The State must recognize the right of the parent to guide one's child intellectually and religiously as being a substantial part of the freedom of the parent protected by the Fourteenth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court and denied appellants' request to enter an order declaring unconstitutional the religious affiliation requirements in adoption proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- religion -- Social Services Law ( § 373), Family Court Act ( § 116), Domestic Relations Law ( § 113) and New York Constitution (art. VI, § 32), which provide that child placed for adoption shall be placed with persons of same religious persuasion as child, are valid -- State has remained neutral, neither advancing nor inhibiting parents' choice of religion or nonreligion -- moreover, under subsequent amendments (L. 1970, ch. 494) such provisions need not be applied mandatorily.

1. Section 373 of the Social Services Law provides that, whenever a child is committed to any agency or released, surrendered, or boarded out, such shall be, when practicable, to an agency or person of the same religious faith as the child. Section 116 of the Family Court Act makes a similar provision when a child is remanded or committed by the Family Court, as does section [***2] 113 of the Domestic Relations Law when an authorized agency gives custody under court order for adoption of a minor in its lawful custody. The three statutes conform with the provisions of the New York Constitution (art. VI, § 32) which provides that: "When any court having jurisdiction over a child shall commit it or remand it to an institution or agency or place it in the custody of any person by parole, placing out, adoption or guardianship, the child shall be committed or remanded or placed, when practicable, in an institution or agency governed by persons, or in the custody of a person, of the same religious persuasion as the child." The provisions of the recited statutes are a valid exercise of the power of the State as parens patriae in regulating the manner in which the custody of children should be determined. These statutes protect that free exercise of religious choice made by the parents of all children whose custody is regulated by the provisions of any of these statutes. The First Amendment does not require the State to be the adversary of religion, but merely that the State be neutral in its relations with groups of religious believers and nonbelievers. In enacting [***3] these statutes the State has remained neutral, neither advancing nor inhibiting the parents' choice of religion or nonreligion but leaving it, when practicable, as it is.

2. Further, subdivision (g) of section 116 of the Family Court Act, and subdivision 7 of section 373 of the Social Services Law amended the respective statutes in 1970 (L. 1970, ch. 494) to eliminate any requirement that the religious matching provisions be applied mandatorily. Both contain identical provisions that the previous subdivisions in the respective statutes "shall, so far as consistent with the best interests of the child, and where practicable, be applied so as to give effect to the religious wishes of the natural mother * * * or parents". These portions of the amended statutes place the primary emphasis on the temporal best interests of the child, subordinating the religious preference of the natural parents. The legislative intent evidenced by the language used in these statutes clearly was to forestall any First Amendment attack against them.

3. Since the primary effect of these statutory enactments neither advances nor inhibits religion, and places primary emphasis on the temporal best interests [***4] of the child, they are not violative of the First Amendment to the United States Constitution.
