Kryger v. Board of Education


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


37 A.D.2d 622; 323 N.Y.S.2d 777; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3777



June 23, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Board of Education of the New York School District of the City of New York and the Acting Chancellor challenged injunctive relief and a declaration of summary judgment entered in favor of respondent president, on Behalf of the Community School Board No. 26, Borough of Queens by the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York).


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to the Community School Board passing a resolution enabling parents of students who completed the sixth grade in June 1970 at certain schools to exercise the option of having their children attend different junior high schools, the acting chancellor issued an order to "cease improper conduct" and superseded the Community School Board with respect to the assignment of pupils. The order was based upon the ground that the Community School Board's conduct would contribute to making J. H. S. 109 a segregated school. Reversing the injunction issued against appellants as well as the summary judgment entered in favor of the Community School Board, the court held that N.Y. Educ. Law § 2590-e, limited the powers of the Community School Board to those matters relating to its district and that it was inconceivable that the Legislature intended that one Community School Board could have unilaterally made a determination which would have materially affected another Community School District.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and granted summary judgment in favor of appellants.

 

866 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Amico v. Erie County Legislature


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


36 A.D.2d 415; 321 N.Y.S.2d 134; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3978



May 20, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order unanimously reversed on the law without costs and summary judgment granted in favor of defendants declaring Erie County Local Law No. 2, 1969, valid and constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county legislature (county) appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Erie County (New York) in favor of plaintiff sheriff, in the sheriff's suit challenging a local law placing all members of the sheriff's department under competitive civil service and claiming that a permissive referendum was required before the law was effective under N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(h)(2) and N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 34(4).


OVERVIEW: The county passed a law amending its charter to place all employees of the sheriff's department in the competitive civil service. The sheriff filed an action claiming that the law was unconstitutional and illegal. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment. The trial court granted summary judgment to the sheriff, finding that the sheriff's department employees were exempt from civil service under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 41(b)(1). The county appealed. The court reversed and entered summary judgment in favor of the county, declaring the local law valid and constitutional. The court held that deputy sheriffs were not exempted from the competitive civil service by § 41(b)(1). N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 9 was inapplicable because of the specific provisions in N.Y. County Law §§ 651, 652. The local law applied to only those employees whose appointments were legal when made. The law did not apply to personal agents of the sheriff who held positions confidential to him and who were not in the service of the county. Reclassification of employees under the New York Civil Service Law was not subject to referendum under N.Y. Const. art IX, § 1(2)(h) or N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 34(4).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to the sheriff, entered summary judgment for the county, and held that the challenged local law was valid and constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- reclassification of Sheriff's personnel -- Local Law No. 2 of 1969 of County of Erie which places deputies and employees in criminal division of Sheriff's office in competitive class of civil service and provides that those with one year in position shall be covered without examination is constitutional -- Civil Service Law ( § 41, subd. 1, par. [b]) which exempts from civil service "deputies of principal executive officers" authorized to act for principals does not apply to Deputy Sheriff -- all employees in criminal division are subject to Civil Service Law and regulations -- inapplicability  [***2] of local law to civil division personnel does not render local law vague -- local law does not violate Constitution because no examination is required of employees with one year in office -- reclassification is not subject to referendum.

1. Local Law No. 2 of 1969 of the County of Erie amends the Erie County Charter to place the deputies and employees in the criminal division of the Sheriff's office in the competitive class of the civil service and provides that those persons in the criminal division who have served for one year shall be covered in their positions without examination. Since the Erie County Legislature has not been shown by plaintiff to have usurped a prohibited power, such local law is constitutional and valid.

2. Paragraph (b) of subdivision 1 of section 41 of the Civil Service Law which exempts from the civil service "deputies of principal executive officers authorized by law to act generally for and in place of their principals" does not apply to a Deputy Sheriff since he is not authorized by law to act generally for and in place of his principal officer. Only the undersheriff has such authorization. Moreover, all employees of the Sheriff's Department, whether [***3] stenographer, laborer or Deputy Sheriff are designated as "deputies".

3. All employees of the Sheriff's Department, other than the undersheriff, whose duties are related solely to criminal functions and law enforcement, are subject to the regulations of the Civil Service Law and, like other law enforcement officers, are subject to classification in the competitive class.

4. The inapplicability of the local law to civil division personnel does not render the local law vague and unclear. The personnel commissioner of Erie County has already determined which deputies actually perform only criminal functions.

5. The local law does not violate section 6 of article V of the Constitution because no examination is required of employees in the criminal division who have worked there for one year prior to the effective date of the local law. In effect, the County Legislature has made the determination that such employees are qualified by experience and that it is not practicable to require them to take an examination. An incumbent may retain his position without taking an examination where his position is reclassified from exempt to competitive, provided that his appointment was legal [***4] when made. The personnel commissioner must determine the legality of appointments and place in the competitive class only those whose appointments were legal when made.

6. Reclassifications under the Civil Service Law are not subject to referendum by the people.

 

867 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dworkin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


36 A.D.2d 430; 321 N.Y.S.2d 263; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3980



May 20, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from an order of the Niagara County Court (New York), which granted defendants' motion to suppress evidence of marijuana seized by customs officials at a border crossing.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested while entering the United States after a search of their car by customs officers resulted in the discovery of marijuana. The lower court judge concluded that probable cause for the search did not exist, and that the search was not energized by suspicion on the part of the officers, but by the fact they saw anti-draft literature in the back of the automobile. The lower court thus found that the search and seizure violated defendants' rights under both the First and Fourth Amendments. The court reversed. The court concluded that (1) the customs officer was performing a routine, lawful border search for which neither probable cause nor suspicion was required, (2) that there was in fact grounds for suspicion in the presence and observation of antidraft literature in the vehicle, (3) that defendants did not meet their burden to an improper motive, (4) that, even if the inspection resulted from the observation of the literature, this was reasonable and proper based on the officer's duty to exclude writings prohibited by 19 U.S.C.S. § 1305, and (5) that the search and seizure were lawful and without violation of defendants' constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order suppressing evidence of marijuana seized from a defendant's car by customs officials at a border crossing.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- automobile in which defendants entered United States was subjected to line search at border and, when immigration officer observed antidraft literature on back shelf, he referred automobile to customs inspector who asked defendant owner to open trunk in which was found marijuana; motion to suppress such evidence should have been denied -- defendants failed to sustain burden of establishing that search and seizure were unlawful -- search was reasonable even in absence of suspicion -- there was reasonable basis for search in presence of antidraft literature prohibited under United States Code (tit. 19, § 1305) -- customs authorities do not waive right to search because all vehicles are not searched.

1. The automobile in which defendants entered the United States from Canada was subjected to a line search by an immigration officer and when he saw antidraft literature on the rear shelf, referred the automobile to a customs inspector [***2] who asked defendant owner to open the trunk of the vehicle in which was found marijuana. An order granting defendants' motion to suppress this evidence is reversed and the motion is denied.

2. Defendants have failed to sustain their burden of establishing that the search and seizure were unlawful.

3. The customs inspector had the right to search defendant owner's vehicle as he entered the United States, and such search was reasonable even though the inspector had no suspicion that drugs were in the vehicle when he initiated the search. If suspicion was required the border officials became suspicious when they observed antidraft literature, which gave reasonable ground for apprehension that contraband was being transported into the country.

4. Even if the immigration officer's selection of defendants' vehicle for a routine secondary customs inspection resulted from the observation of the literature, there was a reasonable and proper basis for such determination -- viz., that the antidraft material and vehicle might be examined to aid in the performance of the duty to exclude writings prohibited by the United States Code (tit. 19, § 1305) -- literature advocating forcible resistance [***3] to any law of the United States is contraband.

5. That some vehicles are searched and others are not did not invalidate the search. Customs authorities do not waive the right to search persons or vehicles crossing the border by not searching each one.

 

868 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hidley v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


36 A.D.2d 387; 320 N.Y.S.2d 957; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4110



May 12, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, without costs, and judgment granted in favor of defendants in accordance with the opinion herein. Appeal from order entered April 29, 1971 dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a governor and other state officials, challenged the judgment and order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which declared 1971 N.Y. Laws 50, 53, 54 unconstitutional in violation of N.Y. Const. art. VII, enjoined defendants from terminating the employment of plaintiff state employees and other state employees, and directed reinstatement with back pay of those state employees previously dismissed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs alleged that the budget and appropriation bills submitted and enacted by defendants contained lump sum appropriations in violation of N.Y. Const. art. VII, §§ 2, 3, 4. They further alleged that they had not been afforded due process and had been denied equal protection of the laws and that defendants had reassigned the duties of dismissed personnel in contravention of N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a cause of action. The trial court treated defendant's motion as a summary judgment motion and granted the relief demanded in the complaint. The court found that the New York Constitution did not mandate the submission and enactment of a line-item budget in the form contended by plaintiffs. While the contested appropriations bills did not list individual employees and salaries, or materials and supplies, it could not be said that they were not broken down into items sufficient to show what money was to be expended and for what purpose. Nor had there been any showing that the legislature was not furnished sufficient information or that the legislature improperly made substitutions to the appropriation bills.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and entered judgment in favor of defendants declaring that there had been no violation of the New York Constitution.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- State budget -- State budget for 1971, although containing lump sum appropriations rather than line items, does not violate Constitution (art. VII) -- People v. Tremaine (281 N. Y. 1) distinguished.

The State Purposes Budget, the Local Assistance [***2] Budget and the Capital Construction Budget, as submitted by the Governor and passed by the Legislature in 1971 although containing lump sum appropriations rather than itemized appropriations, are not unconstitutional in violation of sections 2, 3 and 4 of article VII of the New York State Constitution. The Constitution, either expressly or as construed in People v. Tremaine (281 N. Y. 1), does not mandate the submission and enactment of a line-item budget. In construing the word "items", only those details need be given which are necessary or appropriate to show where and for what the money is to be spent. The details which would be considered practicable and workable at the time of the Tremaine decision, when the executive departments employed less than a third of the present work force and the total State budget was less than $ 410 million, are not the same 30 years later when the Legislature is called upon to exercise its authority over appropriations in excess of $ 7 billion. While the 1971-72 budget and appropriation bills do not, in most instances, list individual employees and salaries, or materials and supplies, they are broken down into items sufficient to [***3] show what money is to be expended and for what purpose. Plaintiffs have not fulfilled their burden of demonstrating unconstitutionality, every intendment being in favor of the validity of the enactment. Upon this record, as a matter of law, the Executive and Legislative branches sufficiently complied with the purpose and letter of the Constitution.

 

869 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


P. R. W. Auto Service, Ltd. v. Board of Zoning Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


36 A.D.2d 956; 321 N.Y.S.2d 818; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4136



May 10, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law and in the interests of justice, without costs; in accordance, the first decretal paragraph of the order is stricken; cross motion by the Board of Zoning Appeals of the Town of Smithtown to dismiss the petition denied insofar as said board is concerned; and matter remitted to said Board of Zoning Appeals for further proceedings not inconsistent with the views set forth herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner auto shop sought to order respondent Board of Zoning Appeals (board) to hear the merits of the shop's application for a use variance without a disclosure affidavit from respondent mortgagee. The board appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which held that the shop had pleaded a cause of action against the board for an arbitrary refusal to waive the requirement and directed the board to answer the petition.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the shop failed to state a cause of action against the board for an arbitrary refusal to waive the requirement of the mortgagee's disclosure affidavit. The court held that absent authority in Smithtown, N.Y., Code of the Town § 54-14B(1) for such a waiver, the board's refusal to waive the jurisdictional requirement was not arbitrary. Further, the board could not be directed to waive compliance with the statutory requirement. The shop was entitled to the opportunity to supply the information called for in the missing disclosure affidavit by using the board's powers and jurisdiction pursuant to § 267(1) to compel the attendance of witnesses and to take testimony at a hearing on the shop's request for the variance. The court held that while the ordinance required the submission of the disclosure affidavits with the application, the ordinance did not mandate strict adherence to the letter so as to require the shop to obtain the execution of the requisite affidavit from someone not within the shop's control and to preclude satisfactory proof of the facts in some manner other than a formal affidavit where the shop was unable to procure the affidavit.


OUTCOME: The court held that the shop failed to state a cause of action against the board for an arbitrary refusal to waive the requirement of the mortgagee's disclosure affidavit, but permitted the shop to present evidence at the variance hearing by using the board's powers to compel witnesses.

 

870 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Robinson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


36 A.D.2d 375; 320 N.Y.S.2d 665; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4158



May 3, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law and the facts, and motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from an order of the Suffolk County Court (New York), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence, a loaded revolver, found during an inventory search of the impounded vehicle he was driving when he was arrested.


OVERVIEW: The vehicle defendant was driving when he was stopped was found to have been unregistered, uninsured, and no evidence of an inspection was available. After the vehicle was impounded, the police found a loaded revolver pursuant to a routine inventory search of the impounded vehicle. Defendant was charged with possession of the revolver, and he was granted a motion to suppress the revolver. The state appealed, and the court reversed concluding that the warrantless search of the defendant's impounded vehicle was reasonable. The court found that defendant's vehicle was searched solely for the purpose of compiling an inventory of its contents and was not an exploratory search for evidence. The court concluded that the police would have been remiss in their duty if they had not impounded the vehicle and removed it form the public highway because it was unregistered, uninsured, and did not have an inspection sticker.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order of suppressing the revolver found while the police were conducting an inventory search on the vehicle defendant was driving when he was arrested.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- weapon found in search of impounded vehicle made for purpose of inventorying contents of vehicle, may not be suppressed; search was not for evidence and did not violate defendant's constitutional rights against unreasonable search and seizure.

A weapon, found in the search of a vehicle which had been lawfully impounded, for the purpose of inventorying its contents (in accordance with a police regulation) to safeguard them for their rightful owner and to protect the police against dishonest claims of appropriation of the vehicle's contents, may not be suppressed. The "search" here was not one in the accepted legal sense for evidence but an involuntary examination reasonably and properly conducted. What was found on such an examination did not violate defendant's constitutional privilege against unreasonable search and seizure and should not have been suppressed.

 

871 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. A.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


36 A.D.2d 859; 321 N.Y.S.2d 747; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4214



April 26, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed on the law, and application denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution challenged the order of the County Court, Westchester County (New York), which, in a coram nobis proceeding, granted defendant's application to vacate a judgment rendered after a nonjury trial adjudging him to be a youthful offender and ordered a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed an appeal from his adjudication as a youthful offender. While his appeal was pending, the court ruled that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 913-g was unconstitutional insofar as it required an accused to waive a jury trial in order to qualify for youthful offender treatment. The court affirmed the youthful offender adjudication. Defendant filed a coram nobis application to vacate the judgment on the ground that he was improperly deprived of a jury trial. The trial court granted defendant's application. The court reversed the trial court's order. Coram nobis could not be used as a vehicle for an additional appeal and was not available where the alleged error appeared on the face of the record. The fact that defendant waived jury trial to qualify for youthful offender treatment was apparent on the face of the record. The fact that such a waiver could not constitutionally be compelled had been established before defendant executed it and before he was tried. The fact that § 913-g was unconstitutional in that respect had been established before defendant's appeal was submitted. Yet, neither at his trial nor on appeal did he contend he had been denied his right to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and denied defendant's application for coram nobis relief from his adjudication as a youthful offender.

 

872 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Meyers v. New York State Div. of Housing & Community Renewal


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


36 A.D.2d 166; 319 N.Y.S.2d 522; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4489



March 15, 1971 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, by striking from the first decretal paragraph thereof so much as denied the cross motion of defendants-intervenors and by substituting therefor a provision granting defendants-intervenors and defendant Village of Rockville Centre summary judgment declaring that subdivision 2 of section 12 of the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law is valid, that the courts may not interfere with the action of the Board of Trustees of the Village of Rockville Centre here in question and that the order of decontrol issued by the State Division of Housing and Community Renewal here in question is valid; and the order, insofar as appealed from, is affirmed as so modified, with a separate bill of $ 10 costs to appellants appearing separately and filing separate briefs, against plaintiffs-respondents.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenants filed suit in the Supreme Court at Special Term in Nassau County (New York) against defendant village seeking a judgment declaring § 12(2) of the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law to be unconstitutional and a rent decontrol order to be unlawful. The supreme court held that the law was constitutional and denied the parties' cross-motions for summary judgment. The village and defendant-intervenor landlords appealed.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the challenged law, the village board adopted a resolution stating that a public hearing to consider whether decontrol of rent for village housing was warranted had been held after due notice and that after deliberation the board found that decontrol was warranted. The court affirmed the trial court's finding that the law was constitutional because the state had an interest in housing accommodations, and the authority to decontrol rent was lawfully delegated to the village, which had better knowledge of local housing conditions. The village followed the standard set forth in the enabling legislation, which was that it had to first determine that the decontrol was warranted. The court ruled that the grant of power was specific and that thus it would not review the board's finding that decontrol was warranted. The court modified the trial court's order by striking the order that denied the village's cross-motion for summary judgment. The court substituted an order stating that the law was valid, that courts could not interfere with the village board's action, and that the order of decontrol was valid. As modified, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order to grant the village's motion for summary judgment on the validity of its resolution to decontrol rent. The order was also modified to state that the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law was valid. As modified, the trial court's order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- decontrol of housing -- Emergency  [***2] Housing Rent Control Law (§ 12, subd. 2), permitting village to adopt resolution requesting State Division of Housing and Community Renewal to decontrol housing accommodations after hearing and upon finding that decontrol is warranted, is not unconstitutional -- delegation of power to local unit of government is constitutional -- local board in adopting resolution followed statute -- standards need not accompany delegation of power -- exercise of discretion by municipality not reviewable.

1. In 1962 the Legislature amended the rent control statute to empower the governing body of a village to adopt a resolution requesting the State Division of Housing and Community Renewal to decontrol housing accommodations within the village after a public hearing on notice and upon a finding that decontrol is warranted (Emergency Housing Rent Control Law, § 12, subd. 2). In 1967, after the public hearing required by the statute, the Board of Trustees of the defendant village adopted the resolution authorized by the amendment of 1962. Subdivision 2 of section 12 of the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law is not unconstitutional.

2. The Legislature, after the enactment of rent control in 1950 [***3] (L. 1950, ch. 250), has gradually shifted the responsibility for the determination of the necessity to continue rent control to the municipalities in the State. The delegation of power to the local unit of government is constitutional.

3. While the resolution of the Village Board professed to do no more than to reflect that a public hearing had been held after due notice and that the board found that decontrol was warranted, the board followed the statute.

4. The only standard applicable to the municipalities is that the local governing body find that decontrol is "warranted", without any other qualification.

5. The contention that standards must accompany the delegation of power cannot be sustained.

6. Where the grant of power is specially designated, the exercise of discretion by the municipality under the grant is ordinarily not reviewable. The Legislature might have removed the restriction on housing accommodations which it imposed without judicial interposition; so, too, where it allowed local units of government to make the same decision in light of local conditions, the apportionment of its power did not change the essential character of sovereignty.

7. The cautionary [***4] words "[if] warranted" in conferring the power of decontrol did not subject the legislative judgment to judicial scrutiny.

People v. Purdy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


36 A.D.2d 697; 319 N.Y.S.2d 579; 1971 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4568



March 4, 1971 

 

874 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gold v. Lomenzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


35 A.D.2d 1054; 316 N.Y.S.2d 830; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3035



December 29, 1970, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review from an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which dismissed petitioner's application, in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, to vacate an order of respondents, the Secretary of State and others, made after a hearing, which suspended petitioner's real estate broker's license.


OVERVIEW: The Secretary held a hearing and suspended petitioner's real estate broker's license, pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 441-c. Petitioner had "demonstrated untrustworthiness" because of excessive and improper amounts charged for locating apartments. It was found that the fees charged were unrelated to services rendered and greatly in excess of those set forth in the real estate board guidelines. Petitioner's license was then revoked by the Secretary. Petitioner filed an application in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to vacate the Secretary's order, which was dismissed. On review, the court held that the language of the statute, "for cause" and similarly broad statutory standards had been upheld, to justify denial of license privileges against the charge of vagueness. Further, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague because it pointed to the untrustworthiness of a real estate broker, the courts construed it with limitations, and the acts upon which this suspension was based were proscribed by a criminal statute, N.Y. Penal Law § 180.55, which gave reasonable notice of what must be done and what must be avoided.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of petitioner's application to vacate an order of the Secretary suspending petitioner's real estate broker's license, and held further that revocation of petitioner's license rendered moot the appeal from dismissal of his petition to review the orders suspending such license.

 

875 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Plancher v. Plancher


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


35 A.D.2d 417; 317 N.Y.S.2d 140; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3039



December 28, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Order modified by (1) striking from the first ordering paragraph the words "in all respects" and adding thereto, immediately after the word "granted", the following: "as to items 1 to 7, inclusive, of the interrogatories and denied as to items 8 to 18, inclusive, thereof"; (2) striking from the second ordering paragraph the following: "only to the extent of" and inserting in said paragraph the words "and the sixth" immediately after "the first 4" and also inserting therein the words "and 'XLIV.' and 'XLV.'" immediately after "'XXXIX.', inclusive"; (3) striking from the third ordering [***2] paragraph all reference to the sixth affirmative defense and also the words "in all respects" and inserting therein, immediately after the word "denied", the following: "as to the second counterclaim and granted as to the first counterclaim"; and (4) striking out the last ordering paragraph. As so modified, order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband and respondent wife cross-appealed the decision of the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York) that granted a motion by the husband to dismiss four affirmative defenses in the wife's answer, granted the wife's motion for a protective order, denied the husband's cross motion to dismiss another affirmative defense and two counterclaims in the wife's answer, and granted the wife leave to add as a party a certain named individual.


OVERVIEW: The husband and the wife cross-appealed the decision of the lower court that granted a motion by the husband to dismiss four affirmative defenses in the wife's answer, granted the wife's motion for a protective order, denied the husband's cross motion to dismiss another affirmative defense and two counterclaims in the wife's answer, and granted the wife leave to add as a party a certain named individual. The court modified the lower court decision by dismissing the sixth defense and the first counterclaim of the wife's answer, deleting the last ordering paragraph, and directing the wife to answer some of the husband's interrogatories. The court found it was not improper to order the wife to disclose her financial condition.


OUTCOME: The court modified the decision of the lower court that granted a motion by the husband to dismiss four affirmative defenses in the wife's answer, granted the wife's motion for a protective order, denied the husband's cross motion to dismiss another affirmative defense and two counterclaims in the wife's answer, and granted the wife leave to add as a party a certain named individual.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- divorce -- wife's rights as tenant by entirety were not unconstitutionally infringed by provision (Domestic Relations Law, § 170, subd. [5]) for divorce based on two-year separation of parties after separation decree; no need for judgment declaring parties to be married and that husband's Mexican divorce was invalid, as conceded by both parties; husband's securing such worthless Mexican divorce does not bar him from equitable relief sought; woman whom he married after Mexican divorce but whom he later divorced was not necessary as party to his present action against wife for New York divorce; husband was entitled to pretrial disclosure of wife's financial condition.

1. Defendant wife's rights as a tenant by the entirety to the parties' former marital home were created by their marriage and rested on the common-law [***3] fiction of the unity of husband and wife. When a divorce occurs and ends the marital status essential to the existence of such tenancy the parties become tenants in common. The State controls rights included within the marital status and may modify the grounds on which a marriage may be terminated. Accordingly, it may not be held that the wife's rights as a tenant by the entirety were of such vested status as to be unconstitutionally infringed by the provision (Domestic Relations Law, § 170, subd. [5]) for divorce based on the separation of the parties for more than two years following a decree of separation. Defenses alleging such unconstitutionality were properly stricken from defendant wife's answer in plaintiff husband's action for a divorce under such statute.

2. The wife's additional defense that the husband was not entitled to partition sought in this action should also have been stricken. By obtaining a concededly invalid Mexican divorce, he did not forfeit any claims to equitable relief. The prior separation decree between the parties contained no provisions against either's obtaining a divorce and his applying for and receiving the worthless Mexican decree cannot [***4] bar relief under subdivision (5) of section 170. The court, in determining partition of the property may take into account any expenditures made by the wife to protect the parties' interests therein.

3. Defendant wife's counterclaim for judgment declaring that the Mexican divorce was invalid and that she is plaintiff's lawful wife should likewise have been dismissed. No controversy exists between the parties as to either prayer for relief, and plaintiff's complaint which acknowledges that defendant is legally married to him thereby concedes the invalidity of the Mexican divorce.

4. Concomitantly the necessity of the presence as a party in the action of a woman whom plaintiff married after obtaining the Mexican divorce but whom he thereafter divorced also falls and a provision granting defendant leave to bring her in as a party should be deleted.

5. Inasmuch as defendant has not squarely met plaintiff's claim of compliance with the separation decree, it is likely that a divorce will be granted and that, as incident thereto, new alimony may be provided. The parties' financial circumstances are vital components in resolving that question. No distinction can be made between disclosure [***5] of the husband's finances, ordinarily directed in the absence of serious resistance to the right to a decree or of special circumstances indicating impropriety in such disclosure, and disclosure of the wife's finances. Defendant wife has submitted no countervailing circumstances rendering improper the disclosure of her financial condition sought by plaintiff and such disclosure plainly serves the objective of the parties' adequate preparation for trial and the orderly and expeditious conduct thereof. Accordingly, defendant wife should be directed to answer specified items of the plaintiff's interrogatories.

 

876 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lattanzio


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


35 A.D.2d 313; 316 N.Y.S.2d 163; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3252



December 7, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the County Court of Schenectady County (New York), which convicted defendant, upon a jury verdict, of the crime of resisting arrest in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 205.30. The jury found that the arrest under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 167 for disorderly conduct was authorized by N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(1), (2), (3), (5), and that defendant intentionally attempted to prevent an officer from effecting the arrest.


OVERVIEW: A motorcycle police officer found a car near the middle of a street, an open beer on the seat, the keys in the ignition, and vehicles behind it were blowing their horns. The officer drove the car toward the curb then defendant opened a car door and shouted obscenities, claiming the officer was stealing his car. Defendant continued his obscenities and the officer arrested him for disorderly conduct. At the time of the arrest, defendant struck the officer in the face and tried choking him. Although the arrest resisted was required to be an authorized one in order to warrant a conviction under N.Y. Penal Law § 205.30, N.Y. Penal Law § 35.27 (1968) provided that a person could not have used physical force to resist an arrest when it would have reasonably appeared that the person was a peace officer. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that defendant's exception to the jury instructions regarding the constitutionality of § 35.27 was groundless where § 35.27 protected not only the physical safety of the peace officer but the average citizen as well, providing a strong presumption of constitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which, upon a jury verdict, convicted defendant of the crime of resisting arrest.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- resisting arrest -- defendant properly convicted of crime of resisting arrest (Penal Law, § 205.30) -- section 35.27 of Penal Law which provides that person may not use physical force to resist arrest, whether authorized or unauthorized, which is being effected or attempted by peace officer when it reasonably appeared that latter was peace officer, not unconstitutional.

1. Defendant was convicted of the crime of resisting arrest in violation of section 205.30 of the Penal Law. The jury was justified in finding that the arrest of defendant for disorderly conduct was authorized and that defendant intentionally attempted to prevent a policeman from effecting it.

2. Section 35.27 of the Penal Law (L. 1968, ch. 73, eff. March 21, 1968) provides that a person may not use physical force to resist an arrest, whether authorized or unauthorized, which is being effected or attempted by a peace officer when it [***2] would reasonably appear that the latter is a peace officer. The statute protects not only the physical safety of the peace officer but the average citizen as well, in that it discourages physical combat and reduces the risk of injury. The prompt remedial redress against illegal arrest that is available through the courts negates undue oppression. Section 35.27 of the Penal Law as charged is not unconstitutional.

Lawson v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


35 A.D.2d 878; 315 N.Y.S.2d 877; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3441; 78 L.R.R.M. 2628



November 23, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teachers sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court of New York, which dismissed a petition pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for review of a respondent board of education's determination that the teachers had engaged in a strike in violation of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210.


OVERVIEW: The teachers engaged in a strike against their employer. The board notified the teachers that they had violated N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210, which prohibited strikes by public employees, and that one day's salary would be withheld as a sanction. The teachers protested that the notice did not comply with N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210(2)(e). The court disagreed, finding that the notice, although inartfully phrased, was sufficiently clear and unambiguous to be considered in compliance with the statute. The court noted that § 210 provided for administrative review of the determination that an employee had violated § 210 and that the teachers had not sought administrative review. It affirmed the dismissal of the teachers' petition, holding that the teachers' failure to seek administrative review foreclosed any consideration as to whether or not there was any strike and whether or not the individual teachers given notice participated therein. It also rejected the teachers' contention that it was unconstitutional to prohibit public employee strikes or to impose administrative sanctions in the form of salary reductions for such strikes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of dismissal.

 

878 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Presbytery of Albany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


35 A.D.2d 252; 315 N.Y.S.2d 428; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3509



November 12, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an individual church formerly associated with the Presbytery of Albany, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Fulton County (New York), which denied the church's motion to vacate its prior order dissolving the church and vesting its property and assets in the presbytery.


OVERVIEW: The church, founded in 1828, incorporated in 1831, and reincorporated in 1966, was governed by the presbytery. After a doctrinal dispute, the church tried to withdraw from the presbytery's ecclesiastic organization. The presbytery then dissolved the church under canon law and claimed its assets. The church disputed the presbytery's right to the assets claiming that it was not subject to the presbytery's jurisdiction. When the supreme court held otherwise and denied a motion to vacate its judgment, the church appealed. The court affirmed. It held that the church had consistently recognized the presbytery's authority under the hierarchical scheme. The court also found no merit in a claim that the church was not subject to N.Y. Relig. Corp. Law § 17-b because it predated the law. The court held that the church's 1966 reincorporation was accomplished only by compliance with N.Y. Relig. Corp. Law § 63, which allowed incorporation of such a church only in connection with the presbytery's ecclesiastic organization, and that by reincorporating thereunder, it subjected itself to all of the related law as well as the presbytery's authority, which was properly enforced under 17-b thereof.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the supreme court denying the church's motion to vacate the supreme court's order dissolving the church and vesting its property and assets in the presbytery.


HEADNOTES

Religious corporations and associations -- disposition of church property -- property of appellant church, upon its being dissolved by ecclesiastical ruling body, was subject to disposition under section 17-b of Religious Corporations Law; appellant was not "associate" member; its contention that section 17-b was not applicable to it as "associate" member is rejected -- even though property was held in its name, section 17-b of Religious Corporations Law insofar as it affects its property is not unconstitutional.

1. Appellant church after incorporation became affiliated with the United Presbyterian Church of North America, which merged with the Presbyterian Church in the United States of America in 1958 to form the United Presbyterian Church in the United States of America.  [***2] On September 14, 1969 appellant attempted to withdraw from the United Church and thereupon respondent dissolved appellant. Section 17-b of the Religious Corporations Law provides that, whenever the presbytery having jurisdiction dissolves a church, the court has jurisdiction to grant an order vesting all property of the dissolved church in the presbytery of jurisdiction. No authorization exists in the constitution or regulations of the United Presbyterian Church for a church or its minister to be an "associate" member thereof, and therefore not subject to section 17-b as claimed by appellant. The Presbytery of Albany has power to govern the churches within its jurisdiction. Moreover, appellant consistently recognized the authority of the Presbytery of Albany and submitted to its jurisdiction pursuant to the hierarchial scheme.

2. Appellant was reincorporated in 1966 under the Religious Corporations Law as the Second United Presbyterian Church of Johnstown. Section 63 of the Religious Corporations Law provides for incorporation of Presbyterian churches only "in connection with the United Presbyterian church in the United States of America". Since the only "connection" recognized [***3] by the United Presbyterian Church between it and a local church is one whereby such local church is subject to the authority of the hierarchy, submission to such authority was a condition of incorporation. Moreover, by its reincorporation in 1966, appellant became subject to the provisions of the Religious Corporations Law as they existed at that date and therefore became subject to the jurisdiction of the hierarchy in matters affecting, among others, the administration of church property. That the local church holds legal title to its property does not free it from the obligation to administer it in accordance with ecclesiastical rule. The decision of the Presbytery that all assets be forfeited by appellant church, being a property decision of a hierarchial polity based upon ecclesiastical law may be constitutionally enforced. Accordingly, appellant's contention that its assets were held in the name of the local congregation and that section 17-b of the Religious Corporations Law is unconstitutional because it adversely affects property rights vested prior to its enactment is rejected.

DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed, with costs to the Workmen's Compensation Board.

People v. Roberts


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

881 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


McCaffrey v. Couper


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


35 A.D.2d 129; 314 N.Y.S.2d 597; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3828



October 1, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, on the law, without costs and without disbursements, defendants' motion for summary judgment granted, plaintiffs' cross motion denied, and the Clerk is directed to enter judgment for defendants without costs and without disbursements, declaring that article 149 of the Education Law, as amended, is constitutional in its application to plaintiffs and that the plaintiffs have no right, constitutional or otherwise, to be licensed as or to continue the practice as public accountants under the partnership or assumed trade name of "Fiduciary Associates".


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Regents of the University of the State of New York challenged an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, New York County (New York), that denied their motion for summary judgment and granted plaintiff accountants' cross motion for summary judgment declaring that the prohibition of N.Y. Educ. Law art. 149 against the practice of public accountancy under an assumed or trade name was unconstitutional as applied to the accountants.


OVERVIEW: The accountants claimed that art. 149 violated both the United States and New York Constitutions and that they were entitled to continue their public accountancy practice under a trade name. The court reversed the summary judgment that the trial court had granted in their favor. The police power allowed the state to regulate the practice of accountancy, and the power of reasonable regulation included the power to prohibit the practice of accountancy under assumed or trade names in order to insure the maintenance of high ethical standards in the profession. The prohibition did not deprive the accountants of vested rights by preventing them from using the name under which they had previously carried on their practice. Grandfathering provisions in N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 7407(4), 7408(2), that gave some public accounting corporations the right to continue to use trade or assumed names did not unlawfully discriminate against the accountants because the classification had a reasonable basis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment, denied the accountants' cross motion for summary judgment, granted the board's motion, and directed the entry of judgment declaring that art. 149 was constitutional and that the accountants had no right to be licensed as or to continue the practice as public accountants under a partnership or assumed trade name.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- regulation of accountants -- State, under its power of regulation, was authorized  [***2] to prohibit practice of accountancy under assumed or trade names; defendants are entitled to judgment declaring article 149 of Education Law constitutional and declaring that plaintiffs cannot practice public accountancy under name "Fiduciary Associates" -- statute does not unlawfully discriminate against plaintiffs in "grandfather" provisions.

1. The State, within the power of reasonable regulation, was authorized to prohibit individuals and partnerships from practicing as public accountants under assumed or trade names. Plaintiffs were not deprived of vested rights by the prohibition against their use of the trade name previously utilized for their practice prior to the enactment of licensing requirements, nor denied equal protection of the laws. The imposition of a new requirement for the continued practice of a profession carried on without the need of such requirements does not violate the Constitution. Accordingly, it was error to grant plaintiffs judgment declaring that article 149 of the Education Law, in its application to plaintiffs, violated the Constitutions and declaring that they are entitled to continue their practice of public accountancy under the name "Fiduciary [***3] Associates" and defendants are granted judgment declaring that article 149 is constitutional and that plaintiffs have no right to be licensed or to continue to practice under said name.

2. The statute, in the "grandfather" sections, permitting the licensing of public accounting corporations, if chartered and active prior to July 1, 1959, to continue (Education Law, § 7408, subd. 2) and the licensing of partnerships under the names of former copartners (Education Law, § 7407, subd. 4), does not unlawfully discriminate against plaintiffs. The legislative classification rests on a reasonable basis.

883 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bloom v. Mayor of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


35 A.D.2d 92; 312 N.Y.S.2d 912; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3980



July 20, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed insofar as appealed from, with $ 10 costs and disbursements jointly against appellants appearing separately and filing separate briefs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers filed suit seeking a declaration that the tax levy imposed by defendants, the city, city officials and state board, was invalid. The Kings County Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), denied the city, city officials and state board's motion to dismiss, and they appealed the order.


OVERVIEW: The city adopted a tax levy based on equalization rates set by the state board. The taxpayers challenged the tax levy and the statute authorizing refixation of the equalization rates. The court affirmed the denial of the city, city officials, and state board's motion to dismiss the taxpayers' complaint. The court held that the taxpayers had standing to sue and stated a valid cause of action. The taxpayers alleged illegal official acts that would produce public injury. The suit was properly brought against the state board, the city and the city officials together because the taxpayers claimed the parties collaborated to implement an unconstitutional and invalid tax plan. A declaratory judgment action was the proper method to challenge the city, city officials and state board's actions as the legality and construction of the tax statute, constitutional issues, and the propriety of official acts was at issue, and declaratory relief would provide a full remedy to the taxpayers.


OUTCOME: The order denying the city, city officials, and state board's motion to dismiss was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- capacity to sue -- plaintiffs, taxpayers in City of New York, have capacity to sue for judgment declaring that tax levy of City of New York is invalid -- State Board of Equalization and Assessment established special equalization rates under chapter 1069 of Laws of 1968 (Real Property Tax Law, art. 12-A) lower than those previously established with result that city would be enabled to impose higher tax levy -- plaintiffs directly affected by new rates -- they allege illegal official acts which will produce public injury -- State body or officer, charged with duty to enforce constitutional provision, is not immune from challenge that they are functioning beyond authority -- city and State officials properly joined in action -- declaratory judgment action is proper and not incompatible with action under section 51 of General Municipal  [***2] Law.

1. Plaintiffs, taxpayers in the City of New York, seek judgment declaring that the tax levy of defendant City of New York, adopted in 1968, is invalid. In June, 1968 the State Board of Equalization and Assessment established special equalization rates for the City of New York acting under chapter 1069 of the Laws of 1968 (Real Property Tax Law, art. 12-A) which permits the State board, upon application of the city, to establish the equalization rate for the current and preceding four years on the basis of "estimated market values" as the measure of full valuation (Real Property Tax Law, § 1250). The Constitution, on the other hand (N. Y. Const., art. VIII, §§ 4, 10) speaks of equalization rates fixed on the basis of "full valuation", without any reference to "estimated market values". The Constitution does, however, command that the Legislature shall prescribe the manner by which the ratio shall be determined. Because the new rates were lower than those previously established the city would be enabled to increase its spending power and impose a higher tax levy. Plaintiffs, as taxpayers, have capacity to sue. They are directly affected by the new equalization rates which [***3] result in a higher tax levy and a higher tax bill to plaintiffs. Whether the statute is unconstitutional, or whether, if it is found that it is constitutional, the public officers concerned acted hastily and without proper foundation in the process of making new determinations under the statute, are both issues in the case. Plaintiffs alleged illegal official acts which will produce public injury.

2. While a taxpayer cannot resist the unconstitutional expenditure of State funds in the absence of a showing of a personal or direct injury to himself, a State body or officer, charged with the duty to enforce a constitutional provision, is not immune from a challenge that it or he is functioning beyond authority. The complaint herein alleges that the State board has exceeded the constitutional power of the equalization of assessments delegated to it by the Legislature. ( Matter of O'Brien v. Assessor of Town of Mamaroneck, 20 N Y 2d 587, and Matter of Posner v. Rockefeller, 26 N Y 2d 970, distinguished.)

3. Claims of constitutional and statutory violations are aimed by the plaintiffs at both State and municipal officials. Plaintiffs allege that without the collaboration [***4] of both State and city officials, the invalid plan could not have been implemented. Accordingly, the city and State officials were properly joined in the action.

4. An action for a declaratory judgment is not necessarily incompatible with an action under section 51 of the General Municipal Law. On the contrary, a declaratory judgment action is preferred where the legality and construction of statutes, constitutional infirmities and the propriety of official acts are the points of inquiry and a decision will afford assistance to the municipality and its citizens.

5. Where a cause for complaint has been stated, the form of the action or proceeding can be molded by the court even though usually an article 78 CPLR proceeding is invoked. The action for a declaratory judgment provides a full remedy.

 

884 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Halpern v. Lomenzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


35 A.D.2d 41; 312 N.Y.S.2d 756; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4101



July 8, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with one bill of costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Secretary of State of the State of New York, State Racing Commission, state racing association, jockey organization, and members, challenged an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which denied their motions to dismiss the complaint of respondents, owners, trainers, and racers of thoroughbred race horses and members of horsemen's benevolent protective association, and for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Respondents had standing to bring the action. They had a legal right to race horses in New York and a legal interest in seeing that appellants did not impair the right. The fact that respondents had administrative remedies or might bring a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding was not controlling because respondents were challenging the constitutionality of a statute or administrative act and they claimed that an official was acting in violation of a statute to deprive them of valuable rights. Respondents' assertion that appellants were according preferred treatment at racing meetings to members of the state racing association with a resulting lowering in purses for respondents clearly raised a justiciable controversy. Moreover,a declaratory judgment action was available to test the constitutionality of the application of a statute to a set of facts and respondents' allegations clearly asserted that appellants were abusing the power granted to them. The trial court properly held that a constitutional issue was present and that triable issues of fact relating to that issue were present that precluded summary disposition. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision, which denied appellants' motions to dismiss respondents' complaint and for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Declaratory judgments -- when appropriate -- respondents, owners, trainers and racers of thoroughbred race horses in New York and members of Horsemen's Benevolent Protective Association, have standing to bring action against State Racing Commission, New York Racing Association and Jockey Club, as well as individual members of those organizations, for judgment declaring that appointments of New York racing officials and exercise of power by Jockey Club are illegal and for injunction barring officials from acting in their capacities, conducting racing meetings and making further appointments -- justiciable controversy presented.

1. Respondents, owners, trainers and racers of thoroughbred race horses in New York and members of the Horsemen's Benevolent Protective Associations, Inc. [HBPA] have standing to bring an action on behalf of themselves and all others similarly situated against the State Racing Commission [SRC], New [***2] York Racing Association, Inc. [NYRA] and the Jockey Club, as well as various individual members of these organizations, for judgment declaring that the appointments of New York racing officials are illegal, having been made under an unconstitutional delegation of power by the SRC; that the exercise of power by the Jockey Club is similarly illegal; and for an injunction barring any of the officials from acting in that capacity, conducting racing meetings, and making further appointments. Respondents have a legal right to race horses in New York and a legal interest in seeing that this right is not impaired by appellants and they need not break the rules and regulations of the NYRA and Jockey Club, thereby subjecting themselves to punitive sanctions, in order to obtain a legal declaration to their rights.

2. Nor, since the HBPA is a nonprofit corporation, was it precluded from maintaining this suit because of its failure to file a certificate of doing business.

3. The fact that respondents have administrative remedies or may bring an article 78 proceeding to challenge any action of the NYRA or the Jockey Club is not controlling where, as here, the respondents are challenging the [***3] constitutionality of a statute or administrative act, or where they claim that an official is acting in violation of a statute to deprive them of valuable rights.

4. Similarly, respondents have shown the existence of a justiciable controversy. They assert that appellants are according preferred treatment at racing meetings to members of the NYRA with a resulting lowering in purses for the respondents. Moreover, even assuming that the challenged provisions of the racing law are constitutional, a declaratory judgment action is available to test the constitutionality of the application of a statute to a set of facts.

5. The fact that the Attorney-General has brought an antitrust action against the HBPA in connection with a boycott of certain race tracks does not subject respondents to imposition of the doctrine of unclean hands. There has been no disposition of that action as yet, and there is no reason to assume that its result will be unfavorable to the respondents.

S. E. Nichols Herkimer Corp. v. Herkimer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


34 A.D.2d 371; 312 N.Y.S.2d 22; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4199; 63 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P52,369



June 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed on the law and facts, without costs, and plaintiff's motion for summary judgment declaring ordinance invalid granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Herkimer County (New York), which denied a motion by plaintiff for summary judgment and ordered that summary judgment be granted to defendants declaring the ordinance of the Village of Herkimer to be constitutional and valid.


OVERVIEW: In this declaratory judgment action, plaintiff sought to have an ordinance, which made it unlawful, with some few exceptions, to sell any article on Memorial Day and Independence Day, declared unconstitutional, illegal and invalid. Special Term denied plaintiff's summary judgment motion and granted summary judgment to respondent, holding the ordinance to be constitutional, legal, valid and effective. The ordinance prohibited commercial and business activities and provided that it was "unlawful" to sell or offer any article for sale during the hours of 9:00 a.m. to 6:00 p.m., which are generally accepted business hours, on the two holidays. The critical issue was whether the ordinance is essentially a regulatory or prohibitory statute. The court found it prohibitory in scope and therefore held that it exceeded the powers granted to the village. In view of the court's determination that by reason of its prohibitory nature the ordinance was invalid, the court did not reach the challenge to its constitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granted plaintiff's motion for summary judgment declaring the ordinance invalid.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- ordinance -- prohibition of business activities -- ordinance prohibiting commercial and business activities on Memorial Day and Independence Day exceeds powers granted to village by General Municipal Law ( § 86).

An ordinance which prohibited commercial and business activities on Memorial Day and Independence Day exceeds the powers granted to a village by General Municipal Law ( § 86). A provision prohibiting sale of articles for a reasonable period would be valid in the interest of avoiding conflict with appropriate ceremonies or formal observance of the national holidays, but the restrictive period should not be [***2] so unduly long as to prohibit and suppress rather than to regulate.

Anile v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department

887 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Hannon v. Ryan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


34 A.D.2d 393; 312 N.Y.S.2d 706; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4203



June 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Erie County (New York), dismissing a writ of habeas corpus. The inmate argued that his due process rights were violated by the revocation of the writ because the jury that convicted him of conspiracy was prejudiced and the indictment was inadequate.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was sentenced to the penitentiary after being convicted of conspiracy. The inmate filed a notice of appeal, and he was released on bail. When he failed to perfect his appeal, the People moved to dismiss. The court granted the inmate's cross motion to enlarge the time for argument of the appeal. The court heard and rejected the inmate's preliminary argument that the substitution of an alternate juror after submission to the jury deprived him of a constitutional right. The inmate was granted a second enlargement but continued a pattern of delay in perfecting his appeal. The court granted the People's motion to dismiss. The inmate began to serve his sentence, but he was released by the issuance of a writ of habeas corpus. The writ was revoked twice because of the inmate's delay. The inmate appealed, and the court affirmed. The court held that habeas corpus was not an available remedy because the inmate's case did not satisfy the practicality and necessity test. In addition, the court held that the jury was not prejudiced and that the indictment was adequate. The inmate's claims of due process violations based on the totality of the circumstances were meritless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the inmate's writ of habeas corpus and remanding the inmate to the custody of the sheriff.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- appeal -- habeas corpus -- relator's appeal to Appellate Division from his conviction of conspiracy was dismissed because of his failing to argue his appeal due to his deliberate dilatory tactics -- dismissal was affirmed by Court of Appeals -- under circumstances habeas corpus is not available remedy -- in any event no merit to any arguments made by relator.

1. Relator had been found guilty at an Extraordinary Special and Trial Term of the Supreme Court of conspiracy in violation of section 580 (subds. 1, 4, 6) of the Penal Law. Because of his actions in failing to argue his appeal, due entirely to his apparently deliberate dilatory tactics, his appeal from the judgment of conviction to the Appellate Division was dismissed and that order of dismissal was affirmed by the Court of Appeals. Under the circumstances, habeas corpus is not an available remedy. It is not available as a substitute for the normal process of appeal. While departure from the orderly proceedings,  [***2] such as appeal, may be permitted when dictated by reason of practicality and necessity, relator's case does not satisfy the practicality and necessity test.

2. In any event there is no merit to any of the arguments made by relator. The statutory provision for the exclusion from the Grand Jury and trial jury panels of persons "who owned $ 250.00 or less in property value" ( Judiciary Law, § 662, subd. 3, repealed by L. 1967, ch. 49, § 3) -- a requirement not enforced in this case -- the exclusion of persons who had scruples against the death penalty and the exclusion of individuals convicted of misdemeanors involving moral turpitude did not violate any of relator's constitutional rights.

3. Concerning the argument that the prosecution's investigation of prospective jurors, both Grand Jury and trial jury, had a coercive effect upon the jurors in violation of relator's rights under the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments, relator knew of this investigation when the trial jury was chosen and made no challenge to it. He therefore waived his opportunity to challenge.

4. Subdivision 6 of section 232 of the Code of Criminal Procedure requires that the court, on challenge, in its discretion [***3] be satisfied that a grand juror can act impartially or without prejudice. This procedure has been approved by the courts and six of the jurors, aware of the investigation, testified that it in no way influenced their verdict.

5. There was no error in charging violations of subdivisions 1, 4 and 6 of section 580 of the Penal Law in one count. The indictment does not charge three different crimes but charges different means by which one crime, conspiracy, may have been committed.

6. The language of subdivision 6 of section 580 is sufficient to give notice of the type of conduct declared illegal. It is not violative of relator's constitutional rights.

7. There was no due process violation based upon the alleged totality of circumstances.

8. This case was tried before an Extraordinary Term of Supreme Court, the existence of which was maintained and continued to handle postconviction matters. However, instead of submitting the habeas corpus petition to the Extraordinary Term, relator applied for his writ to a Justice of the Supreme Court, Special Term. After the Justice signed the writ and released relator on bail, a second Justice determined that there should be a hearing and [***4] continued bail, a third Justice held the hearing, dismissed the writ, and remanded appellant to the custody of the Sheriff, and a fourth Justice granted a certificate of reasonable doubt and bail pending the appeal from the denial of the writ of habeas corpus. While the jurisdiction of Extraordinary Term is not exclusive, it would have been more orderly and efficient for this matter to have been referred to the Extraordinary Term or, failing that, to a single Justice of the Supreme Court, rather than to four Justices of co-ordinate jurisdiction.

9. The judgment dismissing appellant's writ of habeas corpus and remanding appellant to the custody of the Erie County Sheriff should be affirmed.

 

888 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re County of Nassau


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


34 A.D.2d 412; 312 N.Y.S.2d 66; 1970 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4290



June 29, 1970 



DISPOSITION: Final decree reversed, on the law, and proceeding remanded to the Special Term for further proof and a new determination in accordance with the opinion rendered herewith, with costs to abide the event.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged the decree of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which set a price for the taking of their land based on the value of the land as residential property in respondent County of Nassau's condemnation.


OVERVIEW: The property owners used their land as a junkyard, which was a nonconforming use. The county condemned the land. The property owners claimed that the highest and best use of the land was as the nonconforming use. The trial court determined the value of the land based on the county's appraisal of the highest and best use as residential, which resulted in a much lower value than the property owners' appraisal. On appeal, the court reversed. The court concluded that the trial court needed to weigh the effect of the zoning on the property because the restriction both lowered the value, since the use had to continued, and raised the value, since no other properties could be used as a junkyard. The court found that where the restricted use resulted in a higher property value, that restricted use had to be considered the highest and best use of the property. The court recognized that the nonconforming use could be terminated by legislation but in that case the property owners would be allowed a period of time to amortize the investment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded so that the trial court could include in the record the full scope of the factors combining to form an evaluation of the nonconforming use.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- valuation -- junkyards -- county condemned land used as junkyards since 1951 as nonconforming use -- Special Term erred in treating junkyard as business not compensable in eminent domain and adding premium to value of land for residential purposes because of presence of junkyard -- court should take into account that nonconforming use possessed value arising out of restriction which made use nonconforming -- there was no evidence as to extent to which probable termination of use would affect value of land -- proceeding remanded for further proof and new determination.

1. The County of Nassau condemned claimants' land which was located in an industrial zone and used as a junkyard, first as a conforming use, but since 1951, when the zoning ordinance was amended to prohibit the maintenance of junkyards [***2] in an industrial district, as a nonconforming use. The claimants' appraiser testified that the highest and best use of the property was as a junkyard, and that the property was worth $ 631,000. The county's appraiser valued the property at $ 114,400, on the assumption of a residential use as the highest and best use and without giving any effect to the nonconforming character of the land as a junkyard. Special Term held that the land must be valued as though it were ready for use as a development for single-family homes and awarded a premium of an additional 20%. The theory of the Special Term, in treating the junkyard as a business not compensable in eminent domain, and adding a premium to the value of the land for residential purposes because of the presence of the junkyard, was not the appropriate method of evaluating the claimants' land.

2. Because of the zoning ordinance, claimants' pre-existing use of their land as a junkyard could not be halted, but only regulated, and at the same time competing junkyards could not thereafter be established. The one effect tended to lower the value; the second to increase the value. Hence, in determining the value of the land the somewhat [***3] contradictory influences of the zoning restriction were necessarily to be weighed by the Special Term. Special Term was bound to take into account that the nonconforming use possessed a value arising out of the very restriction which made the use nonconforming.

3. Despite its character as a vested right protected from instant abolition by a subsequently enacted ordinance, the claimants' nonconforming use could be terminated by proper municipal legislation. But the legislation could not be arbitrary or unreasonable in its requirements; the prescribed plan of termination must afford a reasonable period of time to the owner to amortize his investment -- a period of time related to the value of the investment, the possible relocation of the use, and the cost of the relocation, among other factors. Here, no proof was offered to demonstrate that the municipal authorities contemplated the passage of an ordinance to end junkyards; or even proof of what period of amortization would satisfy the test of constitutional validity. There was no evidence as to the extent to which a probable termination of the use would affect the value of the land.

4. The decree of the Special Term should be [***4] reversed and the proceeding remanded to the Special Term for further proof and a new determination.
