Irwin v. Board of Regents


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


33 A.D.2d 581; 304 N.Y.S.2d 319; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3104



October 20, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law and the facts, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of regents (board) challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which granted respondent accountant's application, in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, to vacate an order of the Commissioner of Education revoking his certificate to practice as a certified public accountant.


OVERVIEW: The accountant was convicted in federal court of furnishing a gratuity to a federal employee for or because of an official act performed or to be performed by a federal employee. He was subsequently charged by the Department of Education with two specifications, first, with having been convicted of a crime under N.Y. Educ. Law § 7406(1)(c), and second, of having been guilty of "unprofessional conduct" in violation of N.Y. Educ. Law § 7406(1)(b). Following a hearing before the subcommittee of the Public Accounting Committee on Grievances of the Department of Education, the Board of Regents ordered the Commissioner of Education to revoke the accountant's license. The accountant alleged that the subcommittee's denial deprived him of the opportunity to show that his conviction under the federal statute was not a crime within the scope of the Education Law. On appeal, the court reversed the relief from the certificate to practice revocation, and dismissed the accountant's petition. The court held that a subpoena duces tecum could in no way support this claim. As the board's adjudication under § 7406(1)(c) was otherwise uncontested, its determination was proper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granting the accountant's application, on the law and the facts, and dismissed the accountant's petition to vacate an order of the Commissioner of Education revoking his certificate to practice as a certified public accountant.

 

914 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fargnoli v. Murphy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


33 A.D.2d 594; 304 N.Y.S.2d 504; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3128



October 20, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which confirmed a determination from appellee New York State Tax Commission regarding the taxpayers' income tax returns for three years.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers timely filed their state income tax returns for the three years. But a review four years later by the Commissioner of Internal Revenue changed their method of reporting and thereby expanded the taxpayers' gross income and tax liability for the three years. The taxpayers filed a notice of change of income and assessments based upon the increased income were thereafter made. The taxpayers then petitioner for a redetermination of the deficiency and a refund of the income tax. On appeal, the taxpayers argued that N.Y. Tax Laws § 373, which expanded the statute of limitations applicable to deficiency assessments, was unconstitutional by virtue of divesting the taxpayers of a vested interest. The court held that the mere extension of a statute of limitations did not violate any fundamental vested right. Similarly the court determined that the last clause of § 373(4) did not render that section unconstitutionally vague. Finally, as construed, § 373(4) actually benefited the taxpayers by limiting the state to a one-year period within which to recompute their income in the case of a reported change on a properly filed tax return.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment.

 

915 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schuyler v. South Mall Constructors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


32 A.D.2d 454; 303 N.Y.S.2d 901; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3226



September 19, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment granted in favor of plaintiffs declaring that the provision contained in chapter 1 of the Laws of 1969, vesting authority in the Commissioner of General Services to negotiate a contract or contracts for the superstructure construction of the State Library and Museum at the Albany South Mall, is valid and not in contravention of the Constitution of the State of New York and, accordingly, that the agreement between the parties of August 18, 1969 is valid and binding, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, Commissioner of General Services of the State of New York (commissioner), city, and county, brought an action against defendant constructors under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3222 to determine whether a provision in the Deficiency Budget, 1969 N.Y. Laws 1, authorizing the commissioner to negotiate a contract for general construction of a library and museum superstructure was violative of the New York Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The first issue was whether the enactment contravened N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 6. The predecessor to § 6 was N.Y. Const. art. III, § 22 (1894) whose purpose was to eliminate the legislative practice of tacking on to budget bills propositions, which had nothing to do with money matters. The negotiation provision did not violate the spirit or purpose of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 6. The bill specifically appropriated over $ 136,000,000 for the construction of state buildings and other public improvements. Because the negotiation provision concerned an item, which could be constructed with funds from the appropriation, the provision related specifically to some particular appropriation in the bill, even though the particular appropriation to which it related was not precisely itemized in the general appropriation bill. The negotiation provision pertaining to the new state library and museum was not a local law within the meaning of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 15. Because the provision did not violate the constitution, the court sustained the validity of the statutory provision pursuant to which the present contract was negotiated and directed that judgment be granted for plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment in favor of plaintiffs declaring that the provision contained in 1969 N.Y. Laws 1, vesting authority in the commissioner to negotiate a contract or contracts for the superstructure construction of the state library and museum, was valid and not in contravention of the Constitution of the State of New York, and that the agreement between the parties was valid and binding.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- appropriation bills -- provision in deficiency budget which authorizes Commissioner of General Services to negotiate contract for general construction of library and museum superstructure is constitutional -- negotiation provision relates specifically to some particular appropriation in bill within meaning of State Constitution -- negotiation provision is not local law within meaning of Constitution.

1. The provision in the deficiency budget (L. 1969, ch. 1) which authorizes the Commissioner of General Services to negotiate a contract for general construction of the library [***2] and museum superstructure located at the Albany South Mall Project is constitutional.

2. Section 6 of article VII of the New York State Constitution provides that "No provision shall be embraced in any appropriation bill submitted by the governor or in such supplemental appropriation bill unless it relates specifically to some particular appropriation in the bill, and any such provision shall be limited in its operation to such appropriation." The bill specifically appropriates over $ 136,000,000 for the construction of State buildings and other public improvements, including the erection of the building in question. Since the negotiation provision concerns an item which may be constructed with funds from the appropriation, the provision relates specifically to some particular appropriation in the bill.

3. The negotiation provision is not a local law within the meaning of section 15 of article III of the State Constitution.

 

916 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Figari v. New York Tel. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 434; 303 N.Y.S.2d 245; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3265



July 25, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law and the facts, with costs, and petition granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, representatives of a company, appealed an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York) that dismissed a petition in a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for a judgment compelling respondent telephone company to restore telephone service to petitioners.


OVERVIEW: A corporation disseminated anti-Communist, anti-socialist, and pro-American information through the use of telephone recordings. The representatives brought a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to compel the telephone company to restore their telephone service which had been terminated for their failure to comply with a tariff. The tariff required an individual subscriber to state his name and address on a recording. The appellate court dismissed holding that administrative remedies had not been exhausted. On appeal, the court treated the petition as one for mandamus. The court found the speech regulated was not defamatory and did not tend to provoke immediate retaliation. Thus, the tariff was unconstitutional because it deterred freedom of speech and no substantial governmental interest existed to justify such deterrence. The court rejected the telephone company's argument that even if the tariff was unconstitutional, restoration of service was not required. The court found that the telephone company was bound to furnish service to all who paid the proper charges and obeyed reasonable regulations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment on the law and the facts, with costs, and granted the representatives' petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- recorded telephone messages -- telephone company adopted tariff which requires subscriber disseminating recorded messages when telephone number is called to state name and address on recording; Public Service Commission determined that tariff did not violate Public Service Law and that it could not pass on constitutionality; no administrative remedies yet to be exhausted; court should have assumed jurisdiction of constitutional question -- Public Service Commission is not necessary party in proceeding to compel telephone company to restore service to petitioners which was discontinued for failure to comply with tariff -- appeal is properly before Appellate Division rather than Court of Appeals -- proceeding is treated as one in mandamus -- tariff is unconstitutional as infringement on freedom of speech  [***2] -- service must be restored -- individual petitioners do not lack standing because they requested transfer of service to corporation supplying messages.

1. Respondent telephone company adopted a tariff which requires a subscriber disseminating recorded messages on a telephone when the number is called to state his name and address on the recording. The Public Service Commission determined that the tariff did not violate the Public Service Law and that it could not pass on the constitutionality of the tariff. There were no administrative remedies yet to be exhausted before the courts acquired jurisdiction over this mandamus proceeding to compel respondent to restore service to petitioners who failed to comply with the tariff. The only new question other than constitutionality on which the commission could express itself is whether the tariff is unjustly discriminatory as applied to petitioners. Where an attack upon the discriminatory application of a rule promulgated by a public utility is made, courts may exercise jurisdiction and mandamus is a proper remedy. Moreover, initial resort to the courts is proper where only questions of law are involved and the issue of the constitutionality [***3] of a tariff presents such a question. Special Term had jurisdiction to consider the constitutional question and should have taken jurisdiction of the claim of discrimination.

2. The Public Service Commission is not a necessary party to the instant proceeding. Discretion as to whether service is warranted lies solely with respondent. The commission's consent or advice concerning the restoration of service is not essential and it has expressly declined to pass upon the constitutional issue.

3. The appeal is properly before the Appellate Division and not the Court of Appeals because the order is nonfinal, questions other than constitutionality have been raised, and the constitutionality of a rule of a public utility rather than a State statute is involved.

4. It is not necessary to characterize the proceeding, since it was error to dismiss the petition even if petitioners have chosen the wrong remedy, as long as a cause of action is stated. The case is taken as found and the proceeding is treated as one in the nature of mandamus.

5. The tariff is unconstitutional in violation of the freedom of speech and association guarantees contained in the First Amendment and the comparable [***4] provision in sections 8 and 9 of article I of the New York State Constitution. The recorded messages are entitled to the protection of the Constitution even if they exaggerate, vilify prominent persons and are false so long as they are not defamatory and do not incite to riot or tend to provoke immediate retaliation. No compelling State interest warrants the identification requirement. The State has no interest in uncovering authors of defamatory matter so as to aid potential plaintiffs.

6. Service must be restored. The telephone company would be guilty of unjust discrimination if it could terminate service to a subscriber without valid reason and supply service to other subscribers similarly situated.

7. Individual petitioners, who requested a transfer of service to the corporation which supplied the recorded messages, do not lack standing. Their service was terminated prior to the transfer for noncompliance with the tariff.

 

917 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Soltis v. Samenga


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 1064; 304 N.Y.S.2d 493; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3273



July 25, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which denied and dismissed his application to compel respondent district court judge for Nassau County to transfer the hearing to determine whether petitioner was a narcotic addict under N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law art. 9 from the district court to either the supreme court or the county court.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner argued that he was entitled to a 12-member jury for the hearing to determine whether he was a narcotic addict. In the district court, petitioner would only be able to have a six-member jury. Upon review, the court determined that petitioner was not denied the equal protection of the law in violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV because a hearing in the district court to determine whether he was a narcotic addict was not required to be before a 12-member jury, a six-member jury was sufficient. The district court had jurisdiction over both civil and criminal cases. The court held that the district court had jurisdiction to empanel six-member juries to hear criminal narcotic addition cases under N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law §§ 207 and 208. Such trials would not be violative of petitioner's right to equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's judgment.

 

918 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Thomson Industries, Inc. v. Port Washington North


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 1072; 304 N.Y.S.2d 83; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3280



July 25, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court at Special Term, Nassau County (New York) entered judgment for defendant municipality in an action commenced by plaintiff manufacturer seeking to declare that a zoning ordinance enacted by the municipality and N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 249 did not prohibit the operation of the manufacturer's helicopter and the use of its 15-acre parcel as a helicopter landing site. The manufacturer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed the manufacturer's complaint so far as it sought a declaration as to the application of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 249 upon its proposed helicopter operations. Section 249 provided that a private airport might not be established except by authorization of the municipality in which it would be operated under certain limits and that such limits might be waived by the State Commissioner of Transpiration upon application by the municipality. The court affirmed, as modified, by ordering that § 249 prohibited the use of the manufacturer's property as a helicopter landing site unless authorization was granted by the governing body of the municipality in accordance with the provision. The court stated that (1) the zoning ordinance prohibited the activities which the manufacturer's helicopter operations would necessarily have entailed; and (2) the ordinance represented a fair and reasonable use of the police and zoning power by the municipality under the circumstances presented by the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, as modified, the trial court's judgment for the municipality. The court held that a certain statute prohibited the use of the manufacturer's property as a helicopter landing site unless authorization was granted by the governing body of the municipality in accordance with the statute.

People v. K.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 1049; 305 N.Y.S.2d 975; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3306



July 21, 1969 *


* Not published with other decisions of July, 1969, 32 A D 2d 921. [Rep.

 

920 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Meltsner v. Follette


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 389; 302 N.Y.S.2d 624; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3323



July 14, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law and the facts, without costs, and writ sustained to the extent that respondent is directed to afford the relator treatment consistent with his sentence or, if such treatment not be readily available at Green Haven Prison, to transfer the relator to a correctional institution where such treatment is available or to release him.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator challenged a judgment from the Supreme Court in Dutchess County (New York), which dismissed his writ of habeas corpus arguing that he was not receiving the rehabilitative treatment consistent with his sentence.


OVERVIEW: The inmate pleaded guilty to attempted grand larceny and was sentenced to a reformatory. Thereafter, he was transferred to the state hospital as insane and ultimately he was confined to prison. The inmate filed a writ of habeas corpus in which he challenged the constitutionality of his reformatory sentence. The writ was denied and the inmate appealed. Had the inmate been sentenced under N.Y. Penal Law §§ 261, 1297, he would have already gained release. However, he was sentenced pursuant to N.Y. Correction Law § 288, which provided reformatory sentences for males between ages of 21 and 30 years. The court reversed the judgment. Even though reformatory sentences were constitutional, a reformatory term inmate could not be confined for a period greater than the maximum to which a prison term inmate convicted of the same crime was subject, unless the basis for distinguishing between the two persons continued to exist. The inmate was not receiving the benefits of rehabilitation facilities and his treatment was indistinguishable from those with prison terms. Hence, his equal protection rights were violated by such a confinement.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing the writ of habeas corpus was reversed and the writ was sustained to the extent that the inmate had to be afforded treatment consistent with his sentence.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence to reformatory -- basis for distinguishing between felons under 30 sentenced to reformatory where rehabilitation is available and those over 30, sentenced to prison where term is shorter, is rational and sentence to reformatory is not unconstitutional -- since relator, sentenced to reformatory for longer period, by virtue of transfer to another institution does not receive rehabilitation treatment, his writ of habeas corpus is sustained -- neither discharge nor transfer to reformatory is required by sustaining writ; he must receive treatment regardless of nature of institution.

1. Relator, then 22 years old, was convicted of a felony and sentenced [***2] under sections 2185 and 2195 of the former Penal Law and section 288 of the Correction Law, applicable to males between 21 and 30, to a reformatory without duration of sentence being fixed, although the maximum sentence for the same felony if made under sections 261 and 1297 of the former Penal Law would have been 2 1/2 years and relator would now have been entitled to release. Such reformatory sentence does not violate the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution. The ability to benefit from, and the availability of, rehabilitation facilities and treatment form a rational basis for distinguishing between persons convicted of the same offense, and the legislative determination that a distinction be made between those persons older than 30 years and those 30 years and younger is not arbitrary.

2. Whereas the provision for reformatory sentences is constitutional as is section 6-a of the Correction Law which empowers the Commissioner of Correction to transfer inmates from one institution to another, the statutes may not, in conjunction, result in the confinement of a reformatory term inmate for a longer period unless the basis for distinguishing between the two persons [***3] continues to exist. Since here relator is not receiving the benefits of rehabilitation by virtue of an administrative transfer, his writ is sustained.

3. Sustaining the writ does not require relator's discharge nor transfer to a reformatory. Regardless of the nature of the institution in which relator is confined, he must be afforded treatment consistent with his sentence. If none is available at the institution where he is now confined, he must be transferred to an institution where such treatment is available, or released.

 

921 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Medina


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 969; 303 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3360



July 14, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law and the facts, and case remanded to the County Court for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed in this memorandum.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the County Court, Suffolk County (New York), which certified him, as a narcotic addict, to the care and custody of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission for an indefinite period not to exceed 36 months from the date of the order.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a plea of guilty to criminal possession of a dangerous drug. A medical examination indicated that he was an addict within the definition of N.Y. Mental Hyg. § 201(2). Defendant denied that he was an addict, and a hearing was held at which it was determined that he was an addict. The court reversed the judgment, holding that the facts were insufficient to establish that defendant was a narcotic addict under § 201(2). The judgment was based upon the mere discovery of hypodermic needle scars on defendant's arms, coupled with his admission of prior addiction. If defendant could not have been found to be an addict within the first classification of § 201(2), the alternative test of imminent danger of becoming dependent upon narcotics could have been utilized. However, before the second classification in § 201(2) could be utilized, expert testimony as to the subject person's mental condition was required. Because the proof was insufficient to support a finding that defendant was a narcotic addict, the court held that he had to be resentenced on the conviction of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree, and not as a narcotic addict.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and remanded the case to the trial court for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed in the court's memorandum.

 

922 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Smith v. Deegan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 940; 303 N.Y.S.2d 789; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3421



July 7, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which dismissed his application for a writ of habeas corpus. Defendant contended that N.Y. Correct. Law § 218 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's parole was revoked after a hearing wherein defendant was denied counsel. Defendant contended that the denial of counsel was a violation of due process. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err when it denied the writ. Parole was a matter of grace and not a right, so the parole hearing did not involve constitutional due process, and neither the assistance of counsel nor a hearing was constitutionally mandated. Nothing in § 218 or the nature of the parole revocation procedure required an adversary proceeding. The hearing was neither a trial nor a judicial proceeding within the meaning of the federal or state Constitutions. It was an administrative proceeding, not judicially reviewable, where a purely discretionary determination was made. The Board's decision was not subject to review because the Board violated no positive statutory duty.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

923 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bon Air Estates, Inc. v. Suffern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 921; 302 N.Y.S.2d 304; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3467



July 2, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law and the facts, and new trial granted, with costs to abide the event.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court of Rockland County (New York), which found that Suffern, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § 55-2, as interpreted by the building inspector, was constitutional and dismissed the property owner's complaint against respondent village regarding the constitutionality of the ordinance. The village filed a cross-appeal for relief on its counterclaims.


OVERVIEW: The property owner was advised that it would have to get a separate building permit for each building group in the apartment complex it was building. Previously, one permit was required, but the building inspector interpreted § 55-2 to mean that separate permits were required. The property owner filed an action to have § 55-2 declared unconstitutional. The village filed a counterclaim for money damages. The trial court dismissed the complaint. Both parties sought review of the judgment. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and remanded the case for a new trial, holding that the proper interpretation was that a separate permit was required for each building because each one had to be inspected, and the expense was multiplied in proportion to the number buildings; however, because the property owner showed that the fees exceeded the village's costs by 200 percent, the proof was sufficient to overcome the presumption of the ordinance's validity. The court held that the trial court had to determine if there were additional costs of other agencies or if the ordinance was intended for use as an income-producing device and that the counterclaim could be addressed at the new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that found the village ordinance constitutional and dismissed the property owner's suit against the village, and the court granted a new trial.

 

924 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gleason v. Gleason


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


32 A.D.2d 402; 302 N.Y.S.2d 857; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3507



July 1, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Order in the first above-entitled action entered March 13, 1969, reversed, on the law, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to the appellant, and defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint granted; the Clerk is directed to enter judgment in favor of the defendant dismissing the complaint with costs.

Order in the second above-entitled action entered on March 31, 1969, and judgment entered thereon, affirmed, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to the respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, defendant wife in one case sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which denied the wife's motion to dismiss plaintiff husband's action for a divorce based upon N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(5). In the second appeal, plaintiff husband sought review of a judgment, which granted defendant wife's motion to dismiss the husband's action for a divorce.


OVERVIEW: The wives in two separate cases obtained orders of separation from the husbands prior to adoption of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Laws § 170(5), which specified that a divorce was authorized when the husband and wife lived apart pursuant to a separation order for two years. After § 170(5) was adopted, both husbands filed actions for divorce on the grounds that they had continuously lived apart from the wives under the separation orders. The wives filed motions to dismiss the complaints on the grounds that § 170(5) was non-applicable to pre-existing judgments of separations. The supreme court granted one motion to dismiss and denied the other. The unsuccessful wife claimed that § 170(5) did not apply retroactively. The unsuccessful husband claimed that § 170(5) did apply retroactively. The court ruled that retroactive application of § 170(5) to pre-existing orders of separation would unconstitutionally destroy the rights of the parties who had obtained separation orders and would confer authority upon the courts to dissolve a marriage without the consent of a non-guilty party who had obtained the judgment of separation and without proof of any breach of the marital contract on his part.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the supreme court's order that denied the wife's motion to dismiss the husband's action for divorce in one case and affirmed the order in the second case that granted the wife's motion to dismiss the divorce action.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- divorce -- subdivision (5) of section 170 of Domestic  [***2] Relations Law authorizing divorce where parties lived apart pursuant to judgment of separation for two years after granting of judgment does not apply retroactively to judgments preceding enactment of statute.

Subdivision (5) of section 170 of the Domestic Relations Law, enacted by the Divorce Reform Law (L. 1966, ch. 254), which authorizes a divorce where husband and wife have lived apart pursuant to a judgment of separation for a period of two years after the granting of the judgment and plaintiff submits satisfactory proof that he has substantially performed all the terms of such judgment, does not apply retroactively to judgments of separation obtained prior to the enactment of the Divorce Reform Law.

 

925 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


American Institute for Imported Steel, Inc. v. County of Erie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


32 A.D.2d 231; 302 N.Y.S.2d 61; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3571



June 27, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, as to last four decretal paragraphs, unanimously reversed on the law and facts, with costs, declaration made that the resolution adopted on April 16, 1968 by the Erie County Legislature is invalid, and defendants enjoined from attempting to implement or enforce the provisions of the resolution; first decretal paragraph of judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an association of foreign steel importers and related parties, challenged a decision from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Erie County (New York), which denied all relief in the association's request to have a resolution adopted by the Erie County Legislature in April, 1968, which precluded the use of foreign steel, declared invalid and unconstitutional and that sought to stop respondent county from implementing its provisions.


OVERVIEW: A resolution was adopted by the Erie County Legislature in April, 1968, which mandated that contract documents for capital improvement projects of the county should provide that only steel made in mills located in the United States would be supplied in the performance of contracts and subcontracts. On appeal, the court held that the resolution was invalid because of its conflict with the competitive bidding requirement of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 103. The policy of New York was to assure that public works and public purchases were made with the prudent and economical use of public monies for the benefit of all the inhabitants of the state and to facilitate the acquisition of facilities and commodities of maximum quality at the lowest possible cost. Because of the resolution's fatal conflict with § 103, the court did not have to reach the constitutional issues raised.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and held that the resolution was invalid and that the county was enjoined from attempting to implement or enforce the provisions of the resolution.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- County Legislature -- validity of resolution -- resolution adopted by County Legislature providing that only steel made in mills located in United States could be supplied in performance of contracts was invalid -- resolution conflicted with competitive bidding requirement of section 103 of General Municipal Law.

1. A resolution [***2] adopted by the Erie County Legislature, which provided that contract documents for capital improvement projects of the county should provide that only steel made in mills located in the United States would be supplied in the performance of contracts and subcontracts, was in excess of the power of the Erie County Legislature and is invalid because of its conflict with the competitive bidding requirement of section 103 of the General Municipal Law, which states that "all contracts for public work * * * shall be awarded * * * to the lowest responsible bidder".

2. The court properly decreed that the proceeding was not only one brought pursuant to article 78 of CPLR but also an action for declaratory judgment.

 

926 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Krolick v. Lowery


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


32 A.D.2d 317; 302 N.Y.S.2d 109; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3600



June 26, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Determinations of the respondent confirmed, without costs and without disbursements, and the petitions dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court at Special Term in New York County (New York) transferred petitioner firemen's N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceedings to the court. The firemen sought annulment of a determination of respondent fire commissioner in which each was subjected to a fine and, in one instance, a two-day forfeiture of annual leave for refusal to obey orders of a superior and, in one instance, neglect of duty.


OVERVIEW: One fireman refused a superior's order to take a blood alcohol test after he fell from his seat in a fire apparatus responding to an alarm. He was not wearing a seat belt at the time. Another fireman, who smelled of alcohol and was a little unsteady on his feet, refused a superior's order to take a blood test after he lined up on the wrong side of the fire engine. Both refusals were based on the advice of union representatives that such orders violated their constitutional rights. The court confirmed the commissioner's determinations and held that: (1) there was substantial evidence to support the determinations; and (2) there was a reasonable basis for the doctor's order to submit to a blood test because reasonable grounds existed for believing the firemen were intoxicated. There was no compulsory extraction of blood so there was no illegal search and seizure. There was nothing of a testimonial nature used to deprive the firemen of life, liberty, or property because there was no blood sample results introduced in evidence. Furthermore, it was an administrative and not a criminal proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the commissioner's determinations disciplining the firemen for refusal to obey orders of a superior and neglect of duty. The court dismissed the petitions seeking annulment of those determinations.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- firemen -- disciplinary proceedings -- firemen of City of New York refused to obey orders of their superiors that they submit to blood test, superiors believing each to be intoxicated; charges were preferred against each for violation of regulation requiring obedience to orders; after hearing, each was found guilty; determinations are supported by evidence -- reasonable grounds existed for believing that each fireman was intoxicated -- since blood test was refused,  [***2] there was no unlawful search and seizure; petitioners were not deprived of life, liberty or property and not compelled to incriminate themselves; no criminal prosecution is involved -- when intoxication is reasonably suspected, blood sample test is proper -- human life at stake in performance of fireman's work and to extent of blood sample, firemen's individual rights must yield in interests of public safety.

1. Firemen in the employ of the City of New York refused to obey orders of their superiors that they submit to a blood test. The superiors claimed reasonable grounds for believing petitioners to have been intoxicated. Section 25.1.1 of the Regulations of the Fire Department provided that members shall obey all "orders, commands and instructions" promptly, implicitly and unqualifiedly. Charges were preferred against each fireman and each was found guilty after a full hearing of failure to obey. The first named petitioner was also charged with intoxication in violation of section 25.1.4 of the Regulations but he was acquitted of that charge. The second petitioner was also charged with, and found guilty of, neglect of duty in violation of section 11.1.11 which required firemen [***3] to use safety belts. The second petitioner had failed to use his safety belt and sustained injury by falling from the fire vehicle. The determinations made are supported by evidence of sufficient probative force. The evidence is conclusive that each disobeyed the order to permit the taking of a blood sample, and in the case of the second petitioner that he failed to use a safety belt.

2. The regulation forbidding firemen to indulge in or be under the influence of intoxicating liquors is reasonable, and as to each, reasonable grounds existed for a belief that each was intoxicated or under the influence of intoxicating beverages.

3. Since petitioners refused to permit a blood test, there was no illegal search and seizure in violation of the Fourth Amendment. Since there was no blood sample taken, there were no results to be introduced in evidence at the subsequent administrative hearing. There was thus nothing of a testimonial or communicative nature used to deprive petitioners of life, liberty or property and they were not compelled to incriminate themselves in violation of their claimed constitutional privilege. Moreover, the proceeding involved was an administrative one and [***4] was not a criminal prosecution, and the punishment, if petitioners were found guilty of the charges, was a reprimand, a limited fine, suspension for a limited time, demotion or dismissal.

4. When intoxication or being under the influence of an intoxicating beverage is reasonably suspected, a blood sample test is a proper measure reasonably calculated to prove or disprove the fact. If there be any self incrimination it can only occur when the results of such test are offered in evidence against the person charged. That did not occur here.

5. Human lives are or may be at stake in the performance of a fireman's work and to the extent that a blood test be reasonably and properly ordered without danger, his individual rights must yield in the interests of public safety.

People v. McRae


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


32 A.D.2d 772; 301 N.Y.S.2d 322; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3605

928 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Selig


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


32 A.D.2d 213; 302 N.Y.S.2d 94; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3597



June 26, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Respondent disbarred, effective July 28, 1969.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Association of the Bar of the City of New York, instituted disciplinary proceedings against respondent attorney based on charges that the attorney paid the Chairman of the New York State Liquor Authority on behalf of clients to procure favorable disposition of their matters before the Authority. The attorney argued that his grand jury testimony was inadmissible in the disciplinary proceedings because it was compelled.


OVERVIEW: The attorney argued that his testimony, given with immunity, should not have been used in the disciplinary proceeding because it violated his Fifth Amendment privilege against self-incrimination. On appeal, the court disagreed. There was no authority proscribing the use of compelled testimony resulting from the grant of immunity in a non-criminal action or proceeding and such evidence had been specifically ruled admissible in disciplinary proceedings. Further, the fact that the investigatory leads enabling the prosecutor to elicit the attorney's testimony derived from bugging devices did not support the attorney's argument because a constitutional objection to the use of intercepted conversations obtained through such devices was personal and limited to a party to the conversation or whose premises were involved; the attorney was not a party to any of the conversations in question nor were the devices planted on his premises. Finally, there was no merit to the attorney's claim that the payments made were extorted from him and were not bribes. The evidence was clear that the attorney was a willing participant in the scheme and there was no evidence of extenuating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the referee's report and held that the attorney should be disbarred.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- disciplinary proceedings -- respondent, attorney at law, claims his testimony before Grand Jury was forced and improperly admitted in evidence before Referee in disciplinary proceedings -- United States Supreme Court decisions do not proscribe use of compelled testimony in such proceeding -- previously, specifically held that such evidence was admissible -- People v. Berger (388 U. S. 41) does not avail respondent, since not to be applied retroactively -- respondent cannot object to use of intercepted conversations where he was not party to them -- respondent was willing participant to bribes -- no extenuating circumstances -- respondent disbarred.

1. In testimony before a New York County Grand Jury, respondent attorney at law admitted that,  [***2] on five separate occasions, he had paid sums of money to a law office associate of the then Chairman of the New York State Liquor Authority on behalf of clients to procure favorable disposition of their matters before the Authority. When first summoned before the Grand Jury, respondent refused to testify and was cited for and adjudged in contempt. He then asked permission to purge himself of the contempt charge and testify. He now claims his Grand Jury testimony, admitted in evidence before the Referee, was forced and compelled and is incompetent and inadmissible in this proceeding against him. The decisions in Malloy v. Hogan (378 U. S. 1), Spevack v. Klein (385 U. S. 511), and Garrity v. New Jersey (385 U. S. 493) do not proscribe the use of compelled testimony resulting from the grant of immunity in a proceeding other than a criminal action or proceeding. Moreover, it has been specifically held that such evidence is admissible in a disciplinary proceeding.

2. The fact that the leads enabling the District Attorney to elicit respondent's Grand Jury testimony in June, 1963 may have derived from "bugs" during 1962 does not avail respondent, even if it be [***3] assumed that the "bugging" was illegal. People v. Berger (388 U. S. 41), insofar as it declared unconstitutional the "bugging" statute, is not to be applied retroactively ( People v. Kaiser, 21 N Y 2d 86).

3. Respondent was not a party to any of the conversations overheard in the "bugged" offices. The objection on constitutional grounds to the use of intercepted conversations obtained through eavesdropping devices is personal and limited to a party to the conversation or whose premises are involved.

4. His participation in the unlawful payments reflected in the charges is not that of an innocent man forced to submit to extortion. The evidence is clear that respondent was a willing participant. There would appear to be no extenuating circumstances.

5. Respondent should be disbarred.

People v. Joslin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


32 A.D.2d 859; 301 N.Y.S.2d 212; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3620



June 24, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the County Court of Otsego County (New York), which denied defendant's motion to suppress and which convicted defendant of illegal possession of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was in a restaurant and the waitress informed the police that another patron had seen a gun on defendant's person. Defendant was subjected to a frisk, and the gun was recovered. Defendant was charged with illegal possession of a firearm and filed a motion to suppress. The motion was denied and defendant was convicted pursuant to his guilty plea. He sought review. The court affirmed. It found that while there was discrepancy over the issue of whether the police had asked defendant if he had a gun prior to searching defendant, the search was valid as a frisk, which permitted the officer to contact or pat the outer clothing of defendant to detect by the sense of touch if defendant carried a concealed weapon.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's weapon conviction and found that the trial court did not err when it denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained from a frisk of defendant.

 

930 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


J. Hungerford Smith Co. v. Ingraham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


32 A.D.2d 188; 301 N.Y.S.2d 266; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3695



June 18, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law and the facts, and petition dismissed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent commissioner of health (commissioner) challenged the decision of Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which denied its petition to dismiss petitioner company's N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 action. Through its N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, the company sought to challenge the commissioner's order which determined that the company had violated N.Y. Pub. Health Law 1223.


OVERVIEW: In its petition, the company alleged that N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 1220 and 1242(3) were unconstitutional. Moreover, the company argued that it was the victim of discriminatory enforcement and that the commissioner's cease and desist order was arbitrary, capricious, and unreasonable. The commissioner filed its motion to dismiss the company's petition on the ground that it was not timely filed because the company filed its petition more than 60 days after it issued its cease and desist order. Under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1244(1), the company only had 60 days to challenge the commissioner's decision. The trial court found that the company's motion was timely stating that the commissioner's order did not become final and binding until the deadline for compliance in the cease and desist order had passed. The court reversed the trial court's decision holding that the cease and desist order was final and binding on the cease and desist date set forth in the order. Moreover, the court pointed out that the company failed to file its petition within 60 days of the cease and desist date.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision, which denied the commissioner's motion to dismiss the company's petition, which challenged the commissioner's cease and desist order.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against body or officer -- limitations of time -- application to review order of the Commissioner of Health which had directed petitioner to cease and desist discharges of industrial wastes must be made within 60 days ( Public Health Law, § 1244, subd. 1; CPLR 217).

The petition in a proceeding commenced on January 19, 1968 under CPLR article 78 to review and annul an order of the Commissioner of Health directing petitioner to cease and desist discharges of industrial wastes on February 1, 1967 unless it took necessary corrective action by October 1, 1967, should be dismissed as untimely. The four-month Statute of Limitations for article 78 proceedings is not applicable if the law authorizing the proceeding provides a shorter time ( CPLR 217). Subdivision 1 of section 1244 of the Public Health Law states that applications to review orders of the Commissioner must be made within 60 days. The order [***2] sought to be reviewed became final on petitioner on February 1, 1967.

 

931 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schacht v. Schacht


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 201; 301 N.Y.S.2d 151; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3696



June 18, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant wife appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Queens County (New York), which had granted a divorce to plaintiff husband and awarded her $ 10 per week alimony. The wife contended that the trial court improperly applied N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(5) retroactively, that it improperly granted a divorce to the "guilty party," and that as thus construed, the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The wife obtained a decree of separation against the husband in 1966. Later in the year, the legislature enacted New York's first "no-fault" divorce law. Section 170(5) was enacted as a part of that Divorce Reform Law and added as a ground for divorce that the parties had lived apart, pursuant to a decree of separation, for two years after the granting of the decree. The husband brought his action for divorce in 1968, using § 170(5) as the grounds. An amendment to § 170(5) passed in 1968 clearly made the statute apply retroactively to separation decrees obtained before the effective date of the 1966 law. The court held that this amendment reflected the legislative intent that the statute should be applied retroactively. The statute made no distinction between "guilty" and "innocent" parties to a separation decree and thus was available to the husband. As thus construed, it was not unconstitutional. It did not take away any vested property right held by the wife. The court held that the alimony award was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- divorce -- subdivision (5) of section 170 of Domestic Relations Law, authorizing either spouse to obtain divorce where parties have lived apart for two years pursuant to separation decree, was intended to be and is retroactive and applicable where separation decree preceded its enactment -- "guilty" party in prior separation decree may sue -- statute is constitutional.

1. Subdivision (5) of section 170 of the Domestic Relations Law, authorizing either spouse to obtain a divorce where the parties have lived apart for two years pursuant to a separation decree, was intended to be and is retroactive and applicable where a separation decree preceded its enactment.

2. The "guilty" party in the prior separation decree may sue for a divorce as subdivision (5) expressly gives the right to either spouse.

3. The statute is constitutional when construed as retroactive and available to the [***2] "guilty" spouse.

 

932 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Transp. Authority v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


32 A.D.2d 197; 301 N.Y.S.2d 245; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3716



June 17, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Submit judgment in accordance with opinion Per Curiam.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff authority brought an original action to recover from defendant city the cost of operating its passenger train stations located within the city. The controversy was submitted upon an agreed statement of facts pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3222.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1277 made the city responsible for the total cost of the authority's "operation, maintenance and use" of passenger train stations located within the city. The city argued that three of the stations were not true passenger stations because passengers could only use the stations to change trains. The court held that these stations were included within the statutory definition of "passenger station." The city also argued that some of the expenses were for the operation of the railroad itself, as opposed to merely the operation of the stations. The court held that "operations" included whatever services were connected to the use of the stations by passengers, even though the services might have a connection with passengers' use of the railroad. Further, purely railroad activity, although conducted at a station, was not within the statutory intent. Thus, the city was responsible for the salaries of redcaps and baggagemen, for track cleaning and announcing of trains, and for the costs of a central telephone bureau allocated to the stations. It was entitled to a credit for concession rentals and redcap revenues.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the city was responsible for most of the disputed costs, but not for the salaries of train directors and interlockers, who moved trains from one track to another. Nor was it responsible for the cost of printing tickets. The court directed that a judgment be submitted in accordance with its opinion.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- railroads -- passenger stations -- division of costs -- detailed liability of City of New York under section 1277 of Public Authorities Law which makes total cost to Metropolitan Transportation Authority of "operation, maintenance and use" of each passenger station of any railroad facility within City of New York charge to be borne by city, stated.

1. Section 1277 of the Public Authorities Law makes the total cost to plaintiff of the "operation, maintenance and use" of each passenger station of any railroad facility within the City of New York a charge to be borne by the city. Under that statute defendant city is liable for the three stations -- Penn Station, Jamaica and Flatbush. This statute was enacted because all relief measures for the Long Island Rail Road had proved inadequate and it was intended to increase public assistance. The statutory words "passenger station" mean nothing less than stations used for passengers.

2. Defendant is liable for such items [***2] as redcaps, baggagemen, announcers, ticket sellers and stationmasters. "Operations" include the services of employees whose work is connected with the passengers' use of the stations, although necessarily related to the passengers' use of the railroad.

3. Defendant is not liable for train directors, interlockers and ticket printing done at the stations, such not being within station operation. The tickets are for actual transportation on the entire road and the work has no bearing on the operation of the station.

4. Defendant is liable for track cleaning.

5. A central telephone inquiry bureau serves the entire road, with an expense allocation according to the place of origin of the call. Under the circumstances, it is a proper charge for station operation.

6. Defendant is liable for certain expenses from January 20 to January 31, 1966, the increase in its liability under the statute over the contract in effect January 20 which expired January 31. Section 1277, enacted in May, 1966, is retroactive to January 20.

7. Defendant is entitled to concession rentals and redcap revenue. The Legislature intended that the actual burden of station operation costs rather than an accounting [***3] figure should be the measure of relief. When this burden is reduced by the operation of the station, the reduction should be given effect.

934 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Connolly v. Board of Assessors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 106; 300 N.Y.S.2d 192; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3931



May 19, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Order dated September 25, 1968 affirmed, with $ 20 costs and disbursements. Appeal from order dated June 21, 1968, dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of two orders of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Nassau County (New York), which denied her motion for summary judgment and, on reargument, granted partial summary judgment in favor of respondent board of assessors. The taxpayer contended that the board had adopted an illegal method for assessing her real property.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer owned three vacant lots. The board of assessors had for many years used a 1938 tax map to value property. In 1964 they determined to equalize the real property tax burden by assessing unimproved property at one-third of its current market value. Improved land would continue to be assessed at its 1938 value. The taxpayer contended that this method was illegal, first of all because it did not assess property at its "full value," as required by N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 306, and secondly because it treated unimproved property differently from improved property. The court held that the board of assessors could assess property at a uniform percentage of its full value. It further held that the decision to assess unimproved property at a percentage of its current value was a justifiable attempt to equalize the tax burden on the property owners in the county.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court entered on reargument and dismissed the appeal from the prior order as rendered moot by the reargument.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- assessments -- statutes (Real Property Tax Law, §§ 306, 502, subd. 3; County Government Law of Nassau County, § 603) do not prevent Board of Assessors from assessing improved property at 100% of 1938 land value map valuations and unimproved property at 33 1/3% of current market value -- factual question presented to be decided after trial as to whether assessments were properly made as to instant unimproved property.

1. Finding lack of uniformity in relationship of assessment to market value of unimproved property and improved property, respondent Board of Assessors in 1964 adopted a "Current Market Value Land Map" declaring that [***2] 33 1/3% represented the fair equalized ratio of assessed value to current market value of vacant and unimproved land in the county. This created two classes of property for assessment purposes: improved property and certain unimproved property at 1938 valuations, and all other unimproved property at one third of current market value. Section 306 of Real Property Tax Law requiring assessments to be at "full value" is not violated where assessment is expressed at a uniform percentage of full value.

2. The fact that statutes (Real Property Tax Law § 502, subd. 3; County Government Law of Nassau County, § 603) provide that land shall be assessed separately from improvements and the assessment stated in separate columns but that only the total assessment shall be subject to judicial review did not make it illegal for the Board of Assessors to determine an equalized assessment ratio based upon the total assessment figure for improved property. The Legislature has recognized that any separation of value for land and buildings is artificial and hypothetical. Moreover, the statutory provisions are merely directory.

3. The statutes do not prevent respondent from lawfully assessing [***3] improved property at 100% of 1938 land value map valuations and unimproved property at 33 1/3% of current market value. The Board of Assessors deemed a dual approach necessary in a justifiable attempt to equalize tax burden on property owners. Whether this was successful in regard to the instant unimproved property is a factual question to be decided after trial.

People v. Webster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


32 A.D.2d 557; 300 N.Y.S.2d 127; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4185



April 21, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, and case remitted to the court below for further proceedings as hereinafter indicated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York) that convicted him upon a guilty plea to attempted burglary in the third degree, and imposed a second-offender sentence of two and a half to four years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the trial court judgment convicting him upon a guilty plea to attempted burglary in the third degree, and imposing a second-offender sentence upon him. The court found that after his guilty plea, defendant was arraigned on a prior offense information. At that time he admitted identity, but challenged the prior conviction on the ground that it had been unconstitutionally obtained. Without a hearing and determination of the issue raised by that challenge, the sentencing court adjudged defendant a second felony offender and imposed a second-offender sentence, without prejudice to any future coram nobis application that defendant might choose to make addressed to the alleged invalidity of his prior conviction. The court held that this procedure violated N.Y. Penal Law § 1943. The court held that such error required reversal of the judgment. Therefore, the court reversed the trial court judgment and remitted the case remitted for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remitted the case for resentencing.

Bon-Air Estates, Inc. v. Building Inspector of Ramapo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


31 A.D.2d 502; 298 N.Y.S.2d 763; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4268



April 7, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed with $ 10 costs and disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant building inspector challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court, Special Term, Rockland County (New York), which held that Ramapo, N.Y., local laws no. 4, 5 (1967) were unconstitutional and directed the building inspector to issue a building permit and certificate of occupancy in respondents' N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 action challenging the validity of municipal legislation imposing certain obligations on builders of one and two family homes.


OVERVIEW: Respondents filed an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to review the validity of municipal legislation requiring the builders of one and two family homes to hold in escrow the down payment received on the sale of the home and deposit a particular sum on applying for a certificate of occupancy. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment finding the local laws unconstitutional and directing the building inspector to issue a building permit and certificate of occupancy. The court held that the local laws were unconstitutional and invalid on their face in that the laws were in excess of any delegated power reposed in a town by the state constitution and statutes. The court held that the local laws failed largely because they were drawn without inherent demonstration of the facts tending to support their reasonableness. The court further held that the laws were unconstitutional because they impaired the obligation of private contracts, contrary to the United States Constitution, and to that extent impermissibly impinged upon private property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment finding the local laws unconstitutional and directing the building inspector to issue a building permit and certificate of occupancy in respondents' action for a review of the validity of the local laws.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- power to adopt ordinance -- Town of Ramapo did not have authority to enact local laws (Local Laws, 1967, Nos. 4 and 5 of Town of Ramapo) which imposed upon builders of one- and two-family homes, obligations of holding in escrow, until transfer of title contract's down payment and depositing cash or undertaking upon applying for certificate of occupancy to insure good workmanship and compliance with building codes and local regulations -- local laws not sanctioned by police powers vested in town -- police power resposes in State, not in political subdivisions -- State Constitution and statutes (Town Law, § 130, subd. 15; N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 1; Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd. 1) did not authorize local laws here  [***2] involved -- local laws were unnecessary infringement on right of contract and discriminatory -- article 78 proceeding proper vehicle to challenge constitutional power of local legislative body.

1. The Town of Ramapo did not have authority to enact certain local laws (Local Laws, 1967, Nos. 4 and 5 of Town of Ramapo) which imposed upon builders of one-and two-family homes in Rampapo the obligations of (a) holding in escrow, until transfer of title to the purchaser, the contract down payment received on the sale of any such premises and (b) depositing cash or an undertaking in the amount of $ 1,000 upon applying for a certificate of occupancy, to insure good workmanship and compliance with the applicable building codes and local regulations.

2. The local laws were not sanctioned by general police powers vested in the town under common-law doctrine. The residual police power reposes in the State, not in any of its political subdivisions. A municipality can exercise police power only when it has specifically or impliedly received a delegation of such power from the State. These local laws are not validated by any express or implied grant of general police power to the municipality.

 [***3] 3. The State Constitution and the statutes (Town Law, § 130, subd. 15; N. Y. Const., art IX, § 1; Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd. 1) do not authorize the local laws here involved.

4. The provision which purports to indemnify purchasers from builders of one-and two-family houses against damage from improper construction by the escrow deposit of the contract down payment and the posting of cash or a bond with the application for a certificate of occupancy is an unnecessary infringement on the right of contract between individuals.

5. It is also discriminatory since restricted to builders of one- and two-family homes on land held primarily for resale, while exempting from their operation other builders, either of multiple dwellings or commercial properties.

6. Whatever may be the power of a municipality to regulate a particular business, occupation or trade, it does not include the power to abolish a lawful trade.

7. The local laws were drawn without inherent demonstration of the facts tending to support their reasonableness.

8. An article 78 proceeding is a proper vehicle to challenge the constitutional power of the local legislative body.

 

938 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mills


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


31 A.D.2d 433; 298 N.Y.S.2d 549; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4333



March 27, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the County Court of Monroe County (New York) that, following the jury trial, convicted him of assault in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of assault in the second degree after violently and sexually attacking the complainant. The victim asserted before the grand jury that she was raped, however, there was no physical evidence supporting the claim. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment, first holding that N.Y. Penal Law § 2013 did not require corroboration of a consummated rape in order to convict defendant of assault. Therefore, the county court did not err when it refused to charge the jury that the lack of corroboration of the victim's testimony regarding a consummated rape mandated dismissal of the indictment. The court also ruled that the county court erred when it admitted into evidence defendant's blood-stained pants and a pair of shoes. The police seized the items after the warrantless entry int defendant's residence without first announcing their authority and purpose, in violation of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 178 and the Fourth and Fourteenth Amendments. However, the court concluded that the improper admissions constituted harmless error. There was no reasonable possibility that the evidence illegally seized and improperly admitted contributed to he conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- assault in second degree -- defendant properly convicted of assault in second degree -- section 2013 of Penal Law requiring corroboration of testimony of female not applicable -- no testimony of consummated rape -- question whether testimony was corroborated before Grand Jury not before court -- property of defendant taken unconstitutionally -- search was unreasonable -- admission of evidence was not reversible error since it added nothing to People's case.

1. Defendant was properly convicted of assault in the second degree.

2. Section 2013 of the Penal Law requiring corroboration of the testimony of the female is not applicable. At no time during the trial did complainant testify to a consummated rape, and she repeatedly asserted that her condition at the time of the assault was such that she could not say that there had been a consummated rape. The trial court did not err in failing to instruct the jury on the need for corroboration of the female's [***2] testimony.

3. The issue whether complainant's testimony as to rape was corroborated before the Grand Jury is not before the court. No motion was made to inspect the Grand Jury minutes and dismiss the indictment. Whatever defendant may now assert to be a deficiency of proof before the Grand Jury, he has lost his right to challenge the indictment by not moving before judgment.

4. Certain property seized from defendant was taken unconstitutionally. The search in which the evidence was taken was unreasonable. The detectives' entry into and search of defendant's room was not authorized by a search warrant, nor had defendant consented to it since he did not know that the police officers were present when he permitted a person he knew to enter the room. No notice of the officers' office and purpose was given before the entry.

5. The admission, however, of the evidence is not reversible error. The evidence did not add anything to the People's case that might have contributed to the conviction.
