Plaza Management Co. v. City Rent Agency


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


31 A.D.2d 347; 298 N.Y.S.2d 162; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4407



March 13, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Orders entered August 15, 1968, modified, on the law with $ 50 costs and disbursements in each action to the appellant, to include a declaration that the amendment of the rent law embraced in Local Law No. 41 of the Laws of 1967 is constitutional, and the Clerk is directed to enter judgment declaring that the amendment of the rent law embraced in Local Law No. 41 of the Laws of 1967 is constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner brought an action in the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York) against defendant city rent agency for a declaration that the city rent law, particularly as amended pursuant to Local Law No. 41 of the City of New York of 1967, was unconstitutional. The supreme court denied the rent agency's motion to dismiss because the complaint presented factual questions, and the rent agency appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner had received permission in the past to adjust the figures that were used to calculate his net return on his building. The adjustments were subject to certain cut-off dates, but eight years after he bought the building, the city changed a cut-off date. The owner's request for a further rent increase was then denied. In considering his appeal, the court concluded that there were no fact questions remaining for trial and that the motion to dismiss should have been granted. With regard to the legislation's constitutionality, the court ruled that relief against the hardship cause by rent controls was only mandated when a deprivation occurred. A deprivation was not merely a reduction in income but had to amount to a taking because there was no constitutional guarantee of the preservation of a return on any property. There were also limits on any relief given, and to have the figures adjusted as the owner had been doing, the purchase had to be within a reasonably close time to the application for an adjustment. That the owner was successful in previous applications did not give him a vested interest in future success. The court concluded that the legislation was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the supreme court's order denying the agency's motion to dismiss the complaint was modified on the law to include a declaration that the amendment to the rent law was constitutional. The court also directed the entry of judgment to that effect.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- amendment of City Rent and Rehabilitation Law in 1967 which had effect, for purpose of computing increase in rents for fair return, of using assessed valuation rather than higher sale price is not unconstitutional on theory that there was no necessity for it -- Legislature could require that sale, so that its price can be used, be close to date of application for increase in rents -- defendant's motions to dismiss complaints challenging constitutionality of amendment are denied and it is granted declaration that amendment is constitutional.

1. In each action, plaintiff [***2] purchased its property after certain cut-off dates specified in the City Rent and Rehabilitation Law and was able to obtain rent increase adjustments to enable it to obtain fair returns on its property, and in each instance it was able to use the higher purchase price, rather than the lower assessed valuation, for computing the fair return. By Local Law No. 41 of the City of New York for 1967, the cut-off date was changed to February 1, 1961. Since plaintiffs had purchased their properties prior to such cut-off date, the computation of a fair return was based on the assessed value. The local law is not unconstitutional on the theory that there was no necessity for it because existing law covered the situation.

2. The Legislature has a constitutional right to impose a requirement that the sale, to justify use of the sale price in fixing a fair return, be reasonably close in time to the application for an increase, and can itself fix the period that is reasonable instead of leaving it to the administrative authority, the courts or anyone else. That there have been applications at times where the sale was reasonably close to the cut-off date does not operate to give plaintiffs a [***3] vested interest in the cut-off date.

3. The essential facts -- that the earlier statutes allowed the purchase price to be used; that it was so used; that the present statute prevents such use; and that a return based on the assessed value under the existing assessment will be less than one based on the purchase price -- are not in dispute. Defendant is entitled to denials of its motion to dismiss the complaints, and to declarations that the amendment embraced in Local Law No. 41 is constitutional.

 

940 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


McCartney v. Austin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


31 A.D.2d 370; 298 N.Y.S.2d 26; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4443



March 10, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a child and his parent, challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Broome County (New York) upon an order of the court at special term that granted a motion for summary judgment filed by defendants, a board of education and a school district, dismissing plaintiffs' action for a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2164 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought a declaratory judgment that § 2164, which provided that no child could have been admitted to school without a certificate of his immunization against poliomyelitis, smallpox and measles, was unconstitutional. Plaintiffs' additional and alternative argument advanced was that if the exemption accorded by N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2164(8) to children whose parents were bona fide members of a religious organization whose teachings were contrary to the practices required by N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2164 had not applied to them, it was discriminatory and otherwise constitutionally invalid. On appeal, the court affirmed summary judgment in favor of defendants. It held that statutes of such nature, and § 2164 in particular, were within the police power and were thus constitutional. The court found that plaintiffs had repeatedly claimed the benefit of the exemption and that they had negated the application of § 2164 by their affidavit that indicated that their religious faith had not condemned nor condoned medical immunization as required by § 2164. The court concluded that plaintiffs' opposition had not been predicated upon religious grounds within the intendment of § 2164.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, without costs, in favor of defendants in plaintiffs' action for a declaratory judgment that a public health law was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- immunization -- section 2164 of Public Health Law, which provides that no child shall be admitted to school unless immunized against poliomyelitis, smallpox and measles, is constitutional -- exemption of members of religious organizations not applicable -- petitioner is Roman Catholic and that faith does not prohibit inoculation.

1. Section 2164 of the Public Health Law, which provides that no child shall be admitted to school without a certificate of his immunization against poliomyelitis, smallpox and measles is within the police power and is constitutional.

2. Subdivision 8 of the section, which grants an exemption "to children whose parent, parents, or guardian are bona fide members of a recognized religious organization whose teachings are contrary to the practices herein required" is [***2] not applicable. Petitioner is a Roman Catholic and that faith does not have any proscriptions against inoculation.

3. If the exemption is discriminatory and otherwise constitutionally invalid under the establishment of religion clause (U. S. Const., 1st Amdt.) and that guaranteeing the free exercise of religion (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 3), the exemption provision would be found separable from the other provisions of the section, consistently with the saving clause contained in section 5000 of the act, and the argument as to its constitutional invalidity would be academic.

 

941 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Posner v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


31 A.D.2d 352; 297 N.Y.S.2d 867; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4441



March 10, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant optometrists sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which dismissed their petition seeking to compel respondents, the Governor of the State of New York and a task force, to promulgate an optometric fee schedule retroactively.


OVERVIEW: The Governor promulgated a maximum medical fee schedule for each item of service to be performed by a physician for a patient who qualified for Medicaid benefits. The fee schedule was made retroactive. No comparable fee schedule for eye services performed by the optometrist was promulgated at that time. The optometrists brought an action seeking to require that when the fee schedule applying to their services was promulgated, it would also be made retroactive. The trial court dismissed the action for failure to state a cause of action. The optometrists appealed, contending that a failure to make their fee schedule retroactive, as had been done with the physicians' fee schedule, violated the equal protection and due process requirements of the United States and New York Constitutions. In affirming, the court held that the guarantee of equal protection was applicable only where the classification and discrimination were entirely arbitrary and devoid of a reasonable basis. The court found valid distinctions between the various professions that dispensed services. The court concluded that the state could validly distinguish between ophthalmologists and optometrists.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- "Medicaid" -- retroactive application -- failure to make "Medicaid" fee schedule for optometrists retroactive, where fee schedule for physicians had been made retroactive, did not violate equal protection and due process requirements of Federal and State Constitutions.

Following the enactment of Federal and State legislation establishing the "Medicaid" program, the Governor promulgated a maximum medical fee schedule, retroactive to July 1, 1966, for physicians performing services for a patient who qualified for "Medicaid" benefits. In a proceeding seeking to require that, when promulgated, a comparable fee schedule for eye services performed by petitioners, optometrists, also would be made retroactive to July 1, 1966, the application was properly dismissed. The failure to make petitioners' fee schedule retroactive is not violative of the equal protection and due process requirements of the Federal and State [***2] Constitutions. The State may validly distinguish between physicians and nonphysicians, and, more specifically, between ophthalmologists and optometrists. The legislation involved is not itself discriminatory nor does it treat any involved unequally. The complaint is rather with the administrative process. It is evident that, once the rates for petitioners' services had been arrived at, there were present financial and administrative considerations upon which retroactivity could validly have been rejected. The decision to make the rates prospective cannot be said to be arbitrary and discriminatory.

Kaplan v. Kaplan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


31 A.D.2d 247; 297 N.Y.S.2d 881; 1969 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4737



January 20, 1969 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Kings County, dated June 4, 1968, affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant wife sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which denied the wife's motion to dismiss plaintiff husband's cause of action that sought a judgment of absolute divorce on the ground of the wife's continuous abandonment of the husband for a period of approximately six years preceding the institution of the action.


OVERVIEW: The husband sought to maintain an action for absolute divorce based on abandonment of the husband by the wife for a period of more than two years prior to the commencement of the action but less than two years from September 1, 1967, the effective date of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170. In affirming the order insofar as it was appealed from, the court found that the two-year continuous period of abandonment as a ground for divorce pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(2) should be computed to include a period of abandonment prior to September 1, 1967.N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(2) was retroactive. It was the general rule that amendatory statutes were to be construed as prospective only, unless there was a clear expression of the legislative purpose to justify a retroactive application. The question of whether a statute was prospective or retroactive was to be determined by ascertaining the legislative intent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order insofar as it was appealed from.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- divorce -- abandonment -- subdivision (2) of section 170 of Domestic Relations Law, which became effective on September 1, 1967 and permits plaintiff to maintain action for absolute divorce on ground of abandonment of plaintiff by defendant for period of more than two years, was intended to be given retroactive effect.

Subdivision (2) of section 170 of the Domestic Relations Law, which became effective on September 1, 1967 and permits a plaintiff to maintain an action for absolute divorce on the ground of abandonment of plaintiff by defendant for a period of more than two years, was intended to be given retroactive effect. The two-year continuous period of abandonment may be computed to include a period of abandonment prior to September 1, 1967.

945 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York State Osteopathic Soc. v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


31 A.D.2d 111; 295 N.Y.S.2d 536; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2672



December 13, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor osteopathic practitioners, who received their certifications from a California college and whose New York professional licenses carried the inscription "D.O., M.D.," appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which granted the application of petitioners, spokesperson and osteopathic professional organization, to revoke the "M.D." inscriptions on intervenors' licenses.


OVERVIEW: After finishing certification in California, intervenors were entitled to the inscription "D.O." on their professional licenses. Later, California legislation changed the certification of the college, and required that in-state practitioners already licensed chose either the "M.D." or "D.O." classification on their licenses. Intervenors had until that time inscribed only "D.O." on their New York licenses. Intervenors requested and were granted leave to amend their licenses to include both designations. The petitioners filed a complaint that sought revocation of the dual designations and an order that required intervenors to tender their licenses to have the "M.D." designations deleted. On appeal of the judgment in favor of the petitioners, the court rejected intervenors' arguments that the petitioners lacked standing because they had an interest in the regulation of the osteopathic profession. New York public policy vitiated against allowing intervenors to claim dual designations when they had not received any training or education that entitled them to any designation but "D.O." The dual designations contravened the purpose of N.Y. Educ. Law § 6512(3).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the petitioners, holding that intervenors were not entitled to have the dual designations of "M.D." and "D.O." inscribed on their professional licenses. The court affirmed the trial court's order that required intervenors to tender their licenses for the deletion of the "M.D." inscription.


HEADNOTES

Physicians and surgeons -- osteopaths -- physicians who had earned degrees of osteopathy in college in California which subsequently permitted them to change "D.O." degrees to "M.D." degrees not entitled to have New York licenses bearing "D.O." inscriptions changed to add "M.D." -- State recognizes distinction between those trained in osteopathic medicine and allopathic medicine (Education Law, § 6512, subd. 3) -- physicians' qualifications were in osteopathy (cf. Education Law, § 6509) -- petitioner does not lack standing to bring proceeding and is not barred by laches -- not necessary to join all holders of "D.O., M.D." licenses.

1. Intervenors [***2] are physicians practicing in New York State who graduated from a college in California prior to 1962, where they had pursued a program of study and instruction in osteopathic medicine, authorizing them to earn only the degree of Doctor of Osteopathy. Upon passing medical examinations in New York State they were issued licenses containing the inscription "D.O." (Education Law, § 6512, subd. 3). In 1962 the California college was converted into a nonosteopathic medical school and intervenors were permitted to submit their California licenses and have their "D.O." degrees changed to "M.D.". Thereafter they submitted their New York licenses bearing "D.O." inscriptions, along with their newly acquired "M.D." diplomas to appellants and requested that an "M.D." inscription be added to their licenses, and their names be entered in the registry of New York M.D.s. Their licenses were amended so that the applicant's name was followed by the inscriptions "D.O., M.D.". Special Term properly granted petitioner's application directing the Commissioner of Education and Board of Regents to cancel, revoke and rescind the "M.D." inscriptions on these licenses and to order recipients of such "M.D."  [***3] inscriptions to remit their licenses for such deletions.

2. Subdivision 3 of section 6512 of the Education Law states, in part, that: "3. Graduates of colleges of osteopathy registered by the New York state education department who pass the medical licensing examination of the state of New York shall be issued a license to practice medicine and surgery. A license so issued shall contain thereon, following the name of the licensee, the letters D.O." The public policy of this State is to recognize a distinction between those who have pursued a course of study and have been trained in osteopathic medicine and those who have been trained in allopathic medicine.

3. Section 6509 of the Education Law in part provides that: "On receiving from the board an official report that an applicant has successfully passed the examinations and is recommended for license, the department shall issue to him a license to practice according to the qualifications of the applicant." The "qualifications of the applicant" were predicated on the course of study and training in the field of osteopathy and each of the intervenors received his preliminary and professional education and training only in osteopathic [***4] medicine.

4. When intervenors' degrees were changed from "D.O." to "M.D." in California, there was no showing that they had pursued any further program of academic study nor were they required to take examinations in any of the fields of medicine; and when they submitted their M.D. degrees to appellants, no new, additional or different qualifications or training were considered.

5. Petitioner, having sought correction of the inscriptions for some time, most of which was consumed with negotiations and conferences with appellants, is not barred by laches.

6. The proceeding will not be dismissed because of the nonjoinder of all New York State holders of "D.O., M.D." licenses. It was not necessary to proceed in the instant proceeding in the nature of mandamus against anyone except that body or those bodies whose determination is sought to be reviewed. In any event, the question is raised for the first time on appeal.

7. Petitioner, the official spokesman for the osteopathic profession in New York, does not lack standing to bring this proceeding.

8. Intervenors were not entitled to the additional inscription under the full faith and credit clause of the Constitution.

9. Distinctions [***5] made by the Education Law among differing medical practitioners have been held to be proper under the due process clause of the Constitution.

Knatler v. M & M Affiliates, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


31 A.D.2d 722; 297 N.Y.S.2d 195; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2784

947 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Finn's Liquor Shop, Inc. v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


31 A.D.2d 15; 294 N.Y.S.2d 592; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2950



November 7, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Determination unanimously annulled, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to the petitioner.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner liquor store sought review of a determination of respondent State Liquor Authority (New York) (SLA), which suspended the store's liquor license for 10 days, directed that a demand be made on the store and its surety, and directed a letter of warning.


OVERVIEW: The store was charged with a violation of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 100(5) for selling liquor on credit and for refusing to answer question in an interview conducted by the SLA. The court annulled the SLA's determination. It held that there was no evidence to sustain the charge against the sore of sales on credit because the supporting evidence was obtained in violation of the store's constitutional rights. There was no warrant for the search and seizure, and the store had standing to object to the search because evidence was obtained from the coat of an officer of the store that was left for safekeeping in an area not open to the general public. The court held that the officer's refusal to answer questions in the SLA interview was not a violation of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 53.1(o) because the knowledge used by the SLA was gained as a result of an illegal search.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the determination of the SLA to suspend the store's liquor license.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- licenses -- unlawful search and seizure -- search by investigator for State Liquor Authority at petitioner corporation's liquor store of coat belonging to officer, director and stockholder of corporation was unlawful -- petitioner corporation has standing to object to procedure -- as to charge of refusal to answer questions in interview, excluding fruit of search would render interrogation unnecessary -- determination annulled.

1. Petitioner, a corporation, was charged with selling liquor on credit ( Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 100, subd. 5). An investigator for the State Liquor Authority testified that he and another investigator visited petitioner's liquor store and that he was told by the manager of the premises that he could inspect the premises.  [***2] The investigator went into the rear room of the premises, saw there a coat belonging to an officer, director and stockholder of petitioner, and searched the pocket of the coat. This was an unreasonable search. The permission of the manager to inspect the premises could not and did not confer a right to search the personal property of another lawfully and properly on the premises.

2. Petitioner, a corporation, has standing to object to the procedure. The coat with its contents was in the custody and safekeeping of petitioner in a portion of the premises not open to the general public. Because of the search petitioner is or will be compelled to suspend its business and forfeit $ 1,000. The evidence should have been excluded.

3. The second charge was a refusal to answer questions in an interview in violation of a rule of the State Liquor Authority. As to this charge, the privilege against self incrimination is personal and its use available to natural persons only. However, the knowledge used by the State Liquor Authority was gained as the result of an illegal search, and excluding the fruit of the search would have rendered pointless and unnecessary the subsequent interrogation.  [***3] Nor was there any waiver for timely objection was made to the use of the material found.

4. The determination appealed from should be annulled.

 

948 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cox v. Katz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


30 A.D.2d 432; 293 N.Y.S.2d 829; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3282



September 19, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Order entered on August 29, 1968, unanimously affirmed, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to the respondents.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city voters appealed the order of the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which denied their request for a temporary injunction against defendants, a city clerk and a commissioner of elections. The supreme court also dismissed the voters' complaint. Intervenor attorney general was also a party to the action.


OVERVIEW: As part of the plan for the creation of a new civil city court system, a specified number of new judges were created by the legislature. Pursuant to the timing of the passage of 1968 N.Y. Laws ch. 987, the new judges were to be nominated after the spring primary season had passed. Thus, under N.Y. Elec. Law § 131(7), the new judges were to be nominated by political parties instead of being selected in primaries. The city clerk was about ready to certify the election process for the new judgeships. The city voters claimed that the nominating process hindered the ability of minority groups to nominate or elect judges belonging to their group and that the barrier to minority power violated N.Y. Const. art. I, § 1 and the equal protection clauses of the New York and United States Constitutions. Upon appeal, the lower court's decision was affirmed. The court held that neither § 1 nor the equal protection rights of the voters were violated by 1968 N.Y. Laws ch. 987. The court ruled that that there was no constitutional requirement that judges be allocated solely on the basis of population.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Civil Court of City of New York -- provision in New York State Constitution for choosing of Judges of Civil Court of City of New York "by the electors of the counties included within the city of New York from districts within such counties established by law" is construed to enable Legislature to make provision, in its discretion, for election and allocation of said Judges -- implementing statutes providing that vacancy in said office was to be filled, in case of former Justice of Municipal Court or his successor, by electors of former Municipal Court district and, in case of former Justice of City Court or his successor, by electors of county are constitutional -- 1968 statute, signed and effective after date of spring primary election, creating 25 additional judgeships for Civil Court, mandating that these additional Judges be elected  [***2] at November, 1968 general election, to take office January 1, 1969, is constitutional -- in light of circumstances, nominations for these additional offices properly made by political parties rather than by electors.

1. Effective September 1, 1962, the New York State Constitution (art. VI) was amended to enable the Legislature to establish a single court of city-wide civil jurisdiction in New York City and provision was made for the abolition of the City Court and the Municipal Court, the transfer of the Justices thereof to the city-wide court and the choosing of the Judges of the city-wide court "by the electors of the counties included within the city of New York from districts within such counties established by law" (art VI, § 15, subd. a). The Legislature thereafter enacted implementing statutes which established the Civil Court of the City of New York, abolished the Municipal and City Courts, whose Justices had been elected, in the case of the Municipal Courts, from Municipal Court districts within each county in the city and, in the case of the City Courts, from each of the counties within the city, constituted said Justices Judges of the Civil Court, and provided that [***3] a vacancy in the office of Judge of the Civil Court was to be filled, in the case of a former Justice of the Municipal Court or his successor, by the electors of the former Municipal Court district and, in the case of a former Justice of the City Court or his successor, by the electors of the county. The latter statutes do not violate the above-quoted provision of section 15 (subd. a) of article VI of the New York State Constitution, which is construed to enable the Legislature to make provision, in its discretion, for the election and allocation of the Judges of the Civil Court, nor do they infringe the right of franchise or violate the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution.

2. In 1968 (L. 1968, ch. 987), the Legislature created 25 additional judgeships for the Civil Court, mandating that these additional Judges be elected at the November, 1968 general election, to take office January 1, 1969. This statute was signed and became effective on June 22, 1968, after the spring primary election was held. It does not infringe the right of franchise, which may be regulated by the Legislature within reasonable limitations, or the equal protection clause of the United [***4] States Constitution. In the light of the circumstances, the nominations for these additional judicial offices may properly be made by the political parties rather than by the electors of the city.

 

949 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Towler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


30 A.D.2d 876; 293 N.Y.S.2d 7; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3339



July 22, 1968 



DISPOSITION: The findings of fact below are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which refused him youthful offender treatment and convicted him of robbery in the first degree, grand larceny in the first degree, and assault in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was offered youthful offender treatment on condition that he plead guilty. Maintaining his innocence, he refused to plead guilty. Defendant was tried and convicted as an adult. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment. The alternatives presented to defendant deprived him of the right to stand trial on the charge of being a youthful offender. An adjudication as a youthful offender would have allowed defendant the following advantages: he would not have been denominated a criminal, he would not have been disqualified from public office or public employment, he would not have forfeited any right or privilege, he would not have been disqualified from receiving any license granted by public authority, and the determination would not have been deemed a conviction and would have had no effect upon future multiple offender treatment. Due to the improperly imposed condition, defendant, by maintaining his innocence, was forced to expose himself to the far more serious consequences of a judgment of conviction in order to exercise his constitutional right to trial. The court directed that defendant be afforded youthful offender treatment in any subsequent proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, remanded the cause, and ordered that defendant be afforded youthful offender treatment.

 

950 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Larsen Baking Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


30 A.D.2d 400; 292 N.Y.S.2d 145; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3326



July 22, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County, entered January 11, 1967, in favor of plaintiffs after a nonjury trial, reversed, on the law and the facts, without costs, and case remanded to the trial court for (1) determination of the proper sewer surcharges as to each plaintiff, taking into consideration as to Larsen a 40% reduction in the total volume of water intake and as to Ebinger a 40% reduction in the volume registered by its water meter No. 6-7189; and (2) entry of judgment (a) declaring that section 687-1.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York and the Rules and Regulations issued pursuant thereto by the Commissioner of Public Works of the City of New York are constitutional insofar as they pertain to sewer surcharges as applied to plaintiffs on the basis of the industry average, (b) directing that defendant (i) make such refunds to plaintiffs to which they may be entitled because of payments made by them in excess of the proper [***2] sewer surcharges and (ii) correct the record of liens for any unpaid amounts and (c) awarding costs and disbursements of the action to plaintiffs against defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which entered judgment in favor of respondent bakeries in a case for declaratory judgment concerning the computation of sewer surcharges assessed against the bakeries.


OVERVIEW: The bakeries initiated a case against the city and sought declaratory judgments that a local law and the rules and regulations of the Department of Public Works concerning the computation of sewer surcharges declared illegal. The lower court entered judgment in favor of the bakeries and the city appealed. The court reversed the lower court judgment and remanded for further proceedings because it was not unreasonable for the city to apply the industrial average of excess pollutant concentration in computing the amount of the bakeries' sewer surcharge. The bakeries offered no evidence to show that the cost of individual sampling chambers was unreasonable. The city did not require the bakeries to accept the industrial average and offered the bakeries the alternative of bearing the cost of individual sampling. The court concluded that the city correctly defined the scope of the industry and fairly tested a representative cross section in order to arrive at the proper industrial average. The court ordered that the lower court determine the proper sewer surcharges to be assessed to each bakery and to direct the city to refund any excess sewer surcharges paid by the bakeries.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court judgment entered in favor of the bakeries and remanded for a determination of the proper sewer surcharges to be assessed against each bakery.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- surcharge on sewer rents for excess water pollution -- section 687-1.0 of Administrative Code of City of New York, enacted by Local Law No. 2 of 1961, provides for surcharge on sewer rents for those throwing excess pollutants in sewage; by rule, Commissioner of Public Works was empowered to establish industry-wide average pollutant concentration of wastes discharged into sewer system and did establish average for sweetgoods bakers; in action for declaratory judgment, judgment for plaintiffs is reversed, and case remanded for entry of stated judgment for defendant -- plaintiffs offered no proof that cost of installing individual measurement devices was unreasonable -- rule was not subject to strict construction; plaintiffs were not compelled to accept industry-wide average -- bakery investigation begun in 1961 but excess surcharge was not imposed until 1965; no retroactive application was involved -- schedule of priorities for industries investigated did not involve denial of due process  [***3] -- procedure whereby industry average of pollutants was obtained was not arbitrary -- before surcharge billed to plaintiffs, they were entitled to reduction of 40% of water found to have been absorbed in baking process -- for water derived from wells, used for refrigeration and discharged into sewer, one plaintiff was properly denied allowance.

1. Section 687-1.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York (Local Laws, 1961, No. 2 of City of New York) provides for combating water pollution by requiring industries that contribute thereto to pay for the cost of treating sewage. A surcharge was established for pollutants in waste in excess of permissible levels in accordance with a formula. The sewer rent is based upon the water supply to a given property. With respect to bakeries, the Commissioner of Water Supply, Gas and Electricity determined that the average retention of water by bakeries is 40% and that a firm, by showing that it was a bakery, could reduce by that much its sewer rent and surcharge. The Commissioner of Public Works, by rule (§ 9.4, subd. [d]) provided that an industry-wide average pollutant concentration of the wastes discharged into the sewage system [***4] shall be established for each industry and applied to a plant where the sampling is impractical. For the sweetgoods bakers, including plaintiffs, a figure of 4,625 parts per million of excess pollutant concentration was obtained. Plaintiffs were offered the alternative of undergoing individual sampling of their sewage but they declined. In these consolidated actions for judgments declaring that Local Law No. 2 which enacted section 687-1.0 of the Administrative Code, and the rules pertaining to sewer surcharges is illegal, a judgment in favor of plaintiffs is reversed, and the case remanded for determination of the proper sewer surcharges to each plaintiff and for entry of a stated judgment in favor of defendant.

2. The Commissioner is empowered by the law to require the installation, by the regulated party and at its own expense, of equipment necessary for continuous or intermittent measurement. The regulated party, to invalidate such a law, must prove that the cost is so great as to be unreasonable. Plaintiffs offered no evidence to show that the nonrecurring costs of the required sampling chambers were so great as to be unreasonable.

3. The rule of strict construction of [***5] a criminal statute is not applicable to subdivision (d) of section 9.4 of the Rules and Regulations since the administrative agency proceeded under the civil provisions of the statute. Local Law No. 2 contemplated the adoption of such regulations. Continuous universal sampling required under a strict construction would vitiate the law. Moreover, plaintiffs were not compelled to accept the average if they bore the costs of an individual sampling.

4. The bakery investigation began in 1961 and continued to 1965. It was not unreasonable to apply the industrial average from the effective date of the law. The charges were not rendered retroactive because they were computed at a later date.

5. There was no denial of due process or of equal protection of the law in the establishment by the Commissioner of a schedule of priorities for testing based upon reasonable expectations as to which industries would be the heaviest polluters and proceeding thereon to investigate the wholesalers before the retailers. The classification was reasonable and was not intentionally discriminatory.

6. The procedures whereby the Commissioner arrived at the industry average for the sweetgoods wholesale [***6] bakers were not arbitrary.

7. In billing plaintiffs for the surcharge, they should not have been charged for the 40% of the water found to have been absorbed in the baking process, even though plaintiffs made no application therefor.

8. One plaintiff was properly denied an allowance for well water used for refrigeration and air conditioning and then discharged into the sewer. It is not the source of the water that matters but the medium of disposal. Defendant, in determining the average retention, made due allowance for the fact that water used for refrigeration does not enter into the baking process.

 

951 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


State Com. for Human Rights v. Kennelly


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


30 A.D.2d 310; 291 N.Y.S.2d 686; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3427



July 11, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Westchester County, dated June 20, 1967, affirmed, without costs. The time within which appellants must comply with the order is extended until three months after entry of the order hereon.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlords sought review of the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Westchester County (New York), which granted an application to enforce an order of the State Commission for Human Rights and denied the landlords' cross motion to annul the order.


OVERVIEW: The trial court affirmed the order of the state human rights commission, which found that landlords discriminated against a prospective tenant in violation of the Law Against Discrimination, N.Y. Exec. Law art. 15, § 296, subd. 5, para. a, by refusing to lease an apartment to her. The landlords sought review, contending that they were entitled to deny the tenant a lease because she was undesirable tenant, and that the statute was unconstitutional because it constituted a deprivation of their property rights. The court affirmed the order. It held that the evidence adequately supported the commission's findings that the landlords had discriminated against the tenant because she was a Negro and that they thereby violated the applicable provisions of the antidiscrimination statute. The court further held that the statute was a constitutional exercise of the state's police power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court enforcing the order of the commission.


HEADNOTES

Civil rights -- discrimination in housing -- evidence supports finding of State Commission for Human Rights that appellants discriminated in rental of apartment in three-apartment dwelling because complainant was Negro -- appellants' withdrawal of apartment from rental market for self-occupancy was done in bad faith after commission's findings -- directives of commission bear reasonable relation to discriminatory practices -- Law Against Discrimination is constitutional.

1. The evidence supports the findings of the State Commission for Human Rights that appellants discriminated [***2] against complainant in the rental of an apartment in a three-apartment dwelling because she was a Negro, thereby violating the Law Against Discrimination (Executive Law, art. 15). The refusal of the accommodations was not based on other standards of acceptability such as ability to pay rent, number of children in the family, etc.

2. Appellants' exercise of the right to withdraw the apartment from the rental market and to occupy it themselves was tainted with bad faith since it was exercised after the commission's findings. Any hardship resulting from the exercise of the right is self imposed. Nevertheless the time for compliance is extended until three months after entry of the order of affirmance herein.

3. The directives of the commission bear a reasonable relation to the discriminatory practices found to exist, are not burdensome or retaliatory, do not exceed the reasonable authority vested in the commission and are sustained.

4. The Law Against Discrimination insofar as it applies to the rental of private housing is constitutional.

 

952 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


300 West 154th Street Realty Co. v. Dep't of Bldgs.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


30 A.D.2d 351; 292 N.Y.S.2d 25; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3431



July 11, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs or disbursements to either party.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord brought an action against respondent, Department of Buildings of the City of New York, to declare unconstitutional the Emergency Repair Program authorizing repairs without the consent of the landlord and to collect rents for the payment of the repairs. The Supreme Court at Special Term in New York County (New York) entered judgment in favor of the department and dismissed the petition. The landlord appealed.


OVERVIEW: A tenant unsuccessfully attempted to get the landlord to fix a stopped up toilet that was causing an overflow of waste. She then went to the department, which corrected the problem. The department billed the landlord for the cost of the repair and the landlord brought the action. The landlord argued he did not know of the problem until he received the bill and that such problems were the responsibility of the tenant. The landlord argued his constitutional rights were violated because he had no opportunity to contest the necessity for the repair work. The court affirmed the dismissal of the petition and found the program was constitutional. The court found the condition constituted a health hazard and that summary abatement of such nuisances was a long established principle that was not abrogated by the state constitution. Pursuant to the police powers, the department had the authority to recover expenses incurred for matter essential to public health or safety. The court found no equal protection violation in the selection of one class of landlords for the program because the end sought was legitimate and the measures taken reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the landlord's petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- health hazards in multiple dwellings -- "Emergency Repair Program" which authorizes respondent Department of Buildings of City of New York to make repairs of conditions hazardous to health in multiple dwellings without landlord's consent and collect rents in payment is constitutional -- designation of respondent as agency to make repairs properly made by Board of Health and Mayor -- summary abatement of health hazard does not constitute forfeiture of owner's property -- provision imposing charge for repairs against owner and lien upon rent is constitutional.

1. The "Emergency Repair Program" which authorizes respondent Department of Buildings of the City of New York to make repairs with respect to health hazards in multiple dwellings [***2] without the permission and consent of landlord or lienors and to collect the rents in payment therefor should landlord fail to reimburse the city is constitutional.

2. Pursuant to chapter 22 of the Administrative Code, a resolution of the Board of Health dated January 29, 1965 and an executive order of the Mayor, respondent was designated by the Department of Health to effect repairs to conditions constituting a health hazard and nuisance in buildings. Nuisances may be abated by the Department of Health or any agency of the City of New York designated by it as its agent (Administrative Code, ch. 22, § 556-10.0) and the board could properly designate or approve the designation of respondent as the agency to abate the nuisance.

3. The summary abatement of a condition which constituted a health hazard cannot be considered as the imposition of punishment upon the owner, or as the forfeiture of the owner's right of property. The burden of showing unconstitutionality has not been met by petitioner.

4. The charge for correcting the condition is a joint and several charge against the owners, lessees or occupants of the apartment and the expenses are or may be a lien against rent (Administrative [***3] Code, § 564-22.0) or a lien upon land or buildings. Such a provision is constitutional. It is neither unreasonable nor discriminatory that reimbursement is sought first from the owner. Selectivity in enforcement founded upon a rational basis is not arbitrary.

 

953 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cedar v. Commissioner of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


30 A.D.2d 882; 291 N.Y.S.2d 719; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3495



July 3, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher challenged an order from the Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed the teacher's petition seeking review of a determination of respondent Commissioner of Education of the State of New York who refused to set aside the settlement agreement made between the teacher and his school board upon the teacher's claim of wrongful discharge.


OVERVIEW: The tenured teacher was notified that he was to be discharged. Before a hearing could be held, the teacher and the school board entered into an agreement whereby the teacher agreed to resign his position, the district agreed to pay him money, and he agreed to execute a general release. The teacher then sought to have the agreement set aside. The Commissioner determined the teacher was not entitled to have the agreement set aside or to be reinstated. The teacher appealed. The trial court confirmed the Commissioner's determination. The teacher appealed, contending the agreement was invalid as a waiver of tenure rights and as violative of the constitutional prohibition of gifts of public funds. The court disagreed, holding that: (1) a teacher, like any other employee, could resign; (2) for consideration the teacher waived his right to a hearing; (3) the money agreed to be paid constituted a valid compromise of a legal dispute, entered into at arm's length by adult parties represented by counsel; and (4) the school board had the right to negotiate a settlement in avoidance of expensive and uncertain litigation in the best interests of the taxpayers of the school district.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the teacher's petition for review of the Commissioner's determination.

 

954 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cohn v. Borchard Affiliations


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


30 A.D.2d 74; 289 N.Y.S.2d 771; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4015



May 14, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Order entered September 11, 1967, reversed on the law with $ 30 costs and disbursements to the appellant, and the motion dismissing the action for lack of prosecution granted with $ 10 costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied defendant's motion to dismiss plaintiff's action for lack of prosecution upon the ground that no 45-day notice had been served as required by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 provided flatly that the court could not dismiss an action for failure to prosecute unless a notice was served that prosecution was to be resumed and a note of issue served in 45 days. Defendant argued that the action should have been dismissed despite the requirements of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 because the statute was unconstitutional. The court reversed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to dismiss the action, holding that: (1) a court's power to control its own calendar and the disposition of business before it, including the power to dismiss for lack of prosecution, was inherent; (2) inherent powers could not be limited except by constitutional means; and (3) because the restrictive provisions of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 impaired and interfered with the trial court's inherent power to control its own calendars, the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and granted defendant's motion to dismiss the action for lack of prosecution.


HEADNOTES

Dismissal and nonsuit -- dismissal for delay -- CPLR 3216, which provides that court may not dismiss action for failure to prosecute until issue is joined and one year elapsed and notice served that prosecution will be resumed and note of issue served in 45 days, interferes with court's inherent power to control calendar and, to extent that it restricts dismissal for general delay, is unconstitutional.

 CPLR 3216 provides that the court, either on its own motion or that of a party, may not dismiss an action for failure to prosecute unless and until issue has been joined and one year has elapsed, and, further, a notice served that prosecution is to be resumed and a note of issue served in 45 days. The court's power to dismiss for lack of prosecution is inherent. The rule [***2] so curtails this inherent power that it is for all practical purposes abolished. Because the rule's restrictive provisions impair and interfere with the court's inherent power to control its own calendars, CPLR 3216, to the extent that it restricts dismissal for general delay, is unconstitutional.

 

955 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gormeley v. New York Daily News


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


30 A.D.2d 16; 289 N.Y.S.2d 506; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4115



April 29, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant challenged a decision from the Workmen's Compensation Board, which held that the claimant did not have an occupational loss of hearing within the meaning of art. 3-A of the Workmen's Compensation Law. The claimant had filed a claim for workmen's compensation benefits alleging an industrial loss of hearing.


OVERVIEW: A claimant retired after 44 years of employment for his employer. Two years later, he filed a claim for industrial loss of hearing under art. 3-A of the Workmen's Compensation Law. The Board found that he did not have a compensable loss of hearing because his hearing loss was outside of the frequency range used for conversational speech and that the criteria prescribed for the measurement and for the determination of hearing loss were highly salutary and eminently reasonable. On appeal, the court held that: (1) art. 3-A, which provided an adequate method for the measurement of the impairment or establishment of the relationship of an impairment with the employment, was not unconstitutional because there was a reasonable basis for the enactment thereof, which was neither arbitrary nor illegal; and (2) section 44-gg of the Law, which established that loss of hearing should be based upon conversational hearing, was not unconstitutional as applied to the claimant, and the fact that a person suffering loss of hearing from a traumatic injury was entitled to benefits where the same loss of hearing under art. 3-A of the Law was not compensable did not make art. 3-A unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's decision denying the claimant's application for workmen's compensation benefits for an industrial hearing loss.


HEADNOTES

Workmen's compensation -- occupational loss of hearing -- Article 3-A of Workmen's Compensation Law providing compensation for occupational loss of hearing resulting from noise is constitutional -- section 49-gg of article is constitutional as applied to claimant whose loss of hearing, board found, was outside range of conversational hearing and not compensable -- that compensation is given for loss of such hearing if resulting from trauma does not make law unconstitutional -- no unlawful delegation of legislative authority involved in section 49-gg which provides for reliance on report of Committee of Consultants.

1. Article 3-A of the Workmen's Compensation Law, entitled "Occupational Loss of Hearing", relating solely to loss of hearing resulting from employment where the employee is exposed to harmful noise, is constitutional. There was a reasonable basis for its enactment which was neither arbitrary nor illegal.

2. Section 49-gg of such article directs that expert recommendations be secured and authorizes the board to adopt reasonable rules prescribing [***2] standards for the measurement and determination of occupational loss of hearing. A Committee of Consultants made a report containing a formula for the determination of hearing loss. The formula set the tonal ranges for the hearing levels of conversational speech and the method of computing the percentage of loss within such ranges for the purpose of schedule losses. The board adopted the report and adopted rules which set forth such formula for the determination of hearing loss. Such section is not unconstitutional as applied to claimant who, the Workmen's Compensation Board found, did not have an occupational loss of hearing because under the formula, his loss of hearing was outside the frequency range used for conversational speech.

3. That a person suffering loss of hearing as a result of a traumatic injury is entitled to benefits where the same loss of hearing under article 3-A is not compensable does not make the law unconstitutional because of unfairness or discrimination.

4. There was no unlawful delegation of legislative authority in section 49-gg by the provision for the recommendations by experts, and pending the adoption of rules by the board, that the report of the [***3] Committee of Consultants should be the basis for determining standards and criteria of occupational loss of hearing.

 

956 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Capparelli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


29 A.D.2d 1000; 289 N.Y.S.2d 499; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4127



April 29, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the County Court of Madison County (New York), which convicted defendant of soliciting in a public place on behalf of another for immoral purposes; that was, prostitution. Defendant argued that N.Y. Penal Law § 1148 was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of soliciting in a public place on behalf of another for immoral purposes; that was, prostitution. Defendant argued that the words "solicit" and "immoral" did not adequately inform men subject to the penalties what acts it was their duty to avoid. The court affirmed. The court held that the question to be resolved was not whether the words "solicit" and "immoral" taken in the abstract were impermissibly vague and indefinite, but rather whether the verbiage of the whole statutory provision was sufficiently definite, clear, and positive to give unequivocal warning to citizens of the rule that was to be obeyed. The court reasoned that N.Y. Penal Law § 1148 provided that every person who lived wholly, or in part, on the earnings of prostitution committed by another, or who, in any public place, solicited on behalf of another for immoral purposes, was guilty of a misdemeanor. A person who lived with or was habitually in the company of a prostitute and had no visible means of support, should be presumed to be living on the earnings of prostitution. The court concluded that § 1148 was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

959 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Trustees of Sailors' Snug Harbor v. Platt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


29 A.D.2d 376; 288 N.Y.S.2d 314; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4391



March 21, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper) unanimously reversed, on the law and in the exercise of discretion, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to abide the event, and the matter remanded for the taking of further testimony.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landmark preservation commission sought review of an order from the Supreme Court for New York County (New York), which, in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, granted an application to annul the commission's determination that designated appellee trustees' property as landmarks and landmark sites.


OVERVIEW: The trustees' institution was a charitable organization whose main activity was to provide a home for retired seafaring men beyond the age of active duty. On a tract of land there were five buildings that were used as dormitories and those buildings were the subject of the commission's determination. On review the court reversed because the trial court was not supplied with sufficient facts. The court noted that there was evidence that the buildings were one of the two best examples of Greek revival architecture in the country. The court noted that the test in determining a taking of charity property was where maintenance of the landmark either physically or financially prevented or seriously interfered with carrying out the charitable purpose. The court had to determine whether the preservation of the buildings seriously interfered with the use of the property, whether the buildings were capable of conversion to a useful purpose without excessive cost, or whether the cost of maintaining them without use would have entailed serious expenditure.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously reversed the supreme court's judgment on the law and the matter was remanded for the taking of further testimony.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- preservation of landmarks -- State may constitutionally restrict use of property for aesthetic benefit of community -- petitioner charitable corporation's 19th century buildings for housing retired seamen have been declared special landmarks and petitioner may not tear them down and construct new buildings; under applicable statute (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 207-8.0) court may grant relief to charity where maintenance of landmark interferes with carrying on charitable purpose; matter remanded for determination  [***2] of stated factual questions.

1. The State has the right, where constitutionally guaranteed rights are not infringed, to place restrictions on the use to be made by an owner of his property for the cultural and aesthetic benefit of the community.

2. Petitioner, a charitable organization, operates a home for retired seamen in five buildings constructed in the 19th century. It intends to tear them down and construct modern buildings. The Landmarks Preservation Commission has determined that the old buildings are of a special character and therefore petitioner may not reconstruct or demolish them without obtaining the commission's permission. Chapter 8-A of the Administratives Code of the City of New York in section 207-8.0 provides guidelines as to what constitutes an undue burden on commercial property but in regard to property devoted to charitable uses the guidelines are limited to the instances where the institution desires to alienate the property by sale or lease. The statute is not thereby rendered unconstitutional but must be interpreted as giving power to the commission to provide relief in the situation covered by the statute but not restricting the court from so doing [***3] in others. The criterion for relief as to a charity would be where maintenance of the landmark either physically or financially prevents or seriously interferes with carrying out the charitable purpose. The answer thus depends on whether the preservation of the buildings seriously interferes with the use of the property, whether the buildings are capable of conversion to a useful purpose without excessive cost, or whether the cost of maintaining them without use would entail serious expenditure, all in the light of petitioner's purposes and resources. The matter is remanded for further consideration of these factual questions.

961 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Tonawanda v. Tonawanda Theater Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


29 A.D.2d 217; 287 N.Y.S.2d 273; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4656



February 15, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Order, insofar as appealed from, unanimously reversed and complaint dismissed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant theater operator sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Erie County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss plaintiff city's complaint. The city had alleged that there was a licensing ordinance and had requested that the theater owner be enjoined and restrained from operating in violation of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: It was conceded that the theater operator was conducting such a business, was not licensed to do so, and had never even applied for such a license. The theater operator had sought to dismiss the complaint against it for failure to state a claim. The motion was denied and the theater operator subsequently appealed. On review, the critical issue was whether the city's licensing ordinance, which required that all "drive-in theaters" conducted in the city by licensed by the mayor, was constitutional. There were no standards, policies, or rules set forth to control or guide the discretion of the mayor. The matter was left entirely to him, and he might license or not as he saw fit, for any reason or for no reason. In reversing, the court noted that similar legislative provisions repeatedly had been held to be unconstitutional delegations of legislative authority. The court further noted that a reasonable amount of discretion could be delegated to an administrative official, but not an unreasonable and wholly unrealistic and unlimited amount. Therefore, because the discretion vested in the mayor under the ordinance was unrestricted, the ordinance was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously reversed the order appealed from, with costs, and directed that the complaint be dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- licenses -- constitutional law -- ordinance of City of Tonawanda providing for licensing of drive-in theatres provided that licenses should be procured from Mayor and issued at his discretion -- no standards, policies or rules set forth to guide discretion of Mayor -- ordinance is unconstitutional.

An ordinance of the City of Tonawanda requires a license fee of $ 1 per speaker and prohibits a drive-in theatre from operating "unless duly licensed hereunder", and further provides that "All licenses herein provided for shall be procured from the Mayor of the City of Tonawanda, and issued at his discretion, and any such license may be revoked by said Mayor for cause in accordance with the Laws of this City, County, State and Country." There are no standards, policies or rules set forth to control or guide the discretion of the Mayor. The ordinance is unconstitutional and a motion to [***2] dismiss the complaint, which requests that defendant be enjoined and restrained from operating in violation of the ordinance, should be granted.

 

962 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Smith v. La Vallee


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


29 A.D.2d 248; 287 N.Y.S.2d 601; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4661



February 15, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed, application granted, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Cayuga County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion by Marsh, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term of Cayuga County (New York), which dismissed, without a hearing, an application for a writ of habeas corpus. Defendant was convicted of assault in the second degree with the intent to commit the felony sodomy and was sentenced to one day to life pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 243.


OVERVIEW: Previously, the court reversed an order of a county court denying defendant's application for a writ of error coram nobis, vacated the sentence, and remanded for resentencing. Defendant's resentence was affirmed. Defendant asserted that: (1) the resentence should be vacated for failure of compliance with N.Y. Penal Law § 2189-a; (2) that he had not been given, while serving his sentence, any therapeutic care or treatment or rehabilitation procedure of a psychiatric nature; (3) and that the imposition of the one day to life sentence based on hearsay without giving him an opportunity to be heard or to confront witnesses, violated constitutional requirements of due process. On appeal, the court found that while it was true that a prior writ was dismissed after a hearing, the issue presented on such application was not similar to the issues presented. The court held that no reason appeared for affording additional review of the resentencing procedures except as the issue of violation of constitutional requirements of due process was raised. The court noted that adequate psychological and psychiatric services were indispensable to the whole concept of "one day to life" sentences.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously reversed the order, granted the application, and remitted the matter to the supreme court for a full hearing dealing with the extent of the psychological and psychiatric treatment given defendant since the imposition of the sentence. The court also ordered that defendant should further be given a complete psychiatric examination by two duly qualified psychiatrists to determine whether it would be dangerous to release him.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- appeal process provided full opportunity for reviewing issue of compliance with section 2189-a of Penal Law -- failure of sentencing court to afford relator opportunity to be heard and to confront witnesses before imposing sentence was not denial of due process -- relator entitled to hearing as to extent of psychological and psychiatric treatment given him since incarceration and to examination to determine whether he can rejoin society.

1. Relator, who had been sentenced to one day to life, was remanded for resentencing and again sentenced to one day to life. This resentence was affirmed by the Appellate Division and permission to appeal to the Court of Appeals was denied. That appeal process provided a full opportunity for review of the issue of compliance with section 2189-a [***2] of the Penal Law at resentencing.

2. The failure of the sentencing court to afford defendant an opportunity to be heard and confront witnesses before imposing the one day to life sentence was not a denial of due process (cf. Williams v. New York, 337 U.S. 241).

3. In view of the allegations of the petition that relator has not been given any therapeutic care or treatment and that no rehabilitative procedures have been followed through his 15 years of incarceration, which relator was not given an opportunity to support at an evidentiary hearing, the matter should be remitted for a full hearing dealing with the extent of the psychological and psychiatric treatment given relator since the imposition of sentence upon him. He should further be given a complete psychiatric examination by two duly qualified psychiatrists to determine whether it would be dangerous to release him, and whether he is now capable of rejoining society.
