People v. Jefferson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


29 A.D.2d 681; 287 N.Y.S.2d 605; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4809



January 22, 1968 

 

965 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Calzadilla v. Dooley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


29 A.D.2d 152; 286 N.Y.S.2d 510; 1968 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4833; 1 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9850



January 18, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Order denying motion to dismiss petition unanimously reversed, motion granted and petition dismissed, without costs. Appeal from order determining venue dismissed as academic.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant New York State Athletic Commission challenged the denial of its motion to dismiss respondent wrestler's petition, which sought to annul the Commission's refusal to issue a professional wrestling license. The appeal was by permission of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), from trial court orders denying motions to dismiss the petition for insufficiency and to change venue.


OVERVIEW: The Commission denied the wrestler's application to wrestle on the basis that state law did not permit women to wrestle or be licensed. The trial court denied the Commission's motions to dismiss the wrestler's petition. The appellate court referred the matter to the court, which reversed the trial court's order denying the motion to dismiss the petition. The court found that the principal issue was whether there had been a valid delegation of legislative power to the Commission to determine whether the application by a female for a wrestling license would be consistent with the public interest, convenience, or necessity, and with the best interests of wrestling generally. The court found that the delegation of power to the Commission was valid. A great deal of latitude and discretion was to be accorded the Commission, which was responsible to the people of the State of New York for the maintenance of fair dealing, honesty, and clean sport in a field which had in the past been infested with undesirable elements. The Commission's rules were to be enforced unless shown to be clearly arbitrary. The rule that women could not obtain a wrestling license was not clearly arbitrary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying the motion to dismiss the petition. The motion was granted and the petition was dismissed. The court dismissed, as academic, the appeal from the order determining venue.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- women wrestlers -- delegation of authority to State Athletic Commission to control wrestling under reasonable standards is valid, and denial of license to woman wrestler under commission's rules was proper exercise of police power.

The State Athletic Commission was given "the sole direction, management, control and jurisdiction" over licenses to hold wrestling matches or exhibitions within New York State and over licenses to persons to participate in wrestling matches or exhibitions (L. 1953, ch. 137, amdg. L. 1920, ch. 912, § 6) in accordance with standards set forth (L. 1952, ch. 666, § 11, amdg. L. 1920,  [***2] ch. 912, § 12) that a license be granted in its judgment if the "character and general fitness of an applicant * * * are such that the participation of such applicant will be consistent with the public interest, convenience or necessity and with the best interest of * * * wrestling generally". Such standards are definite and reasonable and the delegation of power to the commission is valid. Accordingly, the denial of a professional wrestling license to a woman under a rule of the commission (19 NYCRR 205.15) that no women may compete in any wrestling contest or exhibition and be licensed as a wrestler was a reasonable exercise of the police power of the State.

 

966 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Narcotic Addiction Control Com. v. James


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


29 A.D.2d 72; 285 N.Y.S.2d 793; 1967 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2696



December 19, 1967 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment reversed, on the law and on the facts, and respondent-cross-appellant is to be returned to the custody of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission for such treatment as the statute provides. Settle order on notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Narcotics Addiction Control Commission (New York) challenged a decision of the Supreme Court (New York), which directed the Commission to release defendant, an alleged narcotic addict, upon the ground that N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 206 (1966) was unconstitutional. Defendant also appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court ordered defendant released, holding unconstitutional § 206, which provided that upon defendant's apprehension he could be brought immediately to the reception center for physical examination. The court determined that there was no prejudice to defendant by the provision that he be brought for medical examination prior to a hearing because such a hearing would have served no useful purpose unless there was an examination to determine whether he should have been further detained. The court noted that none of defendant's rights were lost because the assignment of counsel, after his medical examination, assured him every opportunity to assert any and all constitutional rights appropriate under the circumstances. The court concluded that the statements made by defendant were not of a nature that would have abridged his rights against self-incrimination. The court determined that while the legislature should have made provision for the immediate appearance of defendant before the court after apprehension, the failure to do so did not constitute an infirmity as to declare the § 206 to be unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and judgment of the trial court and directed that defendant be returned to the custody of the Commission for such treatment as the act provided.


HEADNOTES

Mental health -- drug addiction -- amendments to Mental Hygiene Law with respect to treatment and detention of narcotic addicts constituted valid and proper exercise of State's police power.

1. The 1966 amendments to the Mental Hygiene Law, with respect to the treatment and detention of narcotic addicts, were a valid and proper exercise of the State's police power.

2. Section 206 of the Mental Hygiene Law directs that the alleged addict, after apprehension, be taken for medical examination prior to his appearance in court for a hearing. This provision does not violate the constitutional rights of one [***2] so apprehended. The warrant authorizing apprehension of an alleged addict may be issued only after a Judge or Justice has examined the petition and determined that there are reasonable grounds to believe that the person on whose behalf the application is made is a narcotic addict. (Mental Hygiene Law, § 206, subd. 2, par. a.) The procedure authorized by the act is similar to that where an ordinary warrant of arrest is issued by a Judge.

3. Failure to advise him of his rights and to have counsel assigned to him did not in any way prejudice the alleged addict. The assignment of counsel, after his medical examination, assured him every opportunity to assert any and all of his constitutional rights that would be appropriate in the circumstances.

4. Furthermore, even if there were any defect in not assigning counsel prior to the alleged addict's medical examination, there was no justification to release him because, with the protection of counsel, and in accordance with the provisions of the law, he was found to be a narcotic addict, first by a court sitting alone, and again at a trial with a jury.

5. Statements made during the period of his examination, which were admitted in evidence [***3] during the subsequent hearings, were not of a nature that would abridge the alleged addict's rights against self incrimination.

6. Moreover, if there is to be a system of compulsory treatment, a medical examination must be had, whether before or after assignment of counsel, and no medical examination can be complete, or can serve any useful purpose unless the alleged addict responds to questions that are essential in order to make a proper diagnosis. Consequently, the taking of these statements, without advice of counsel, and the use of them during the hearings, do not prejudice or violate the rights of the alleged addict.

7. The evidence overwhelmingly established that he was an addict within the meaning of the law. Even if evidence, which it is claimed was unlawfully obtained, were eliminated, there is sufficient remaining to support the conclusion that he is an addict.

8. In no event should the act, which has a severability clause, fall in its entirety. It should be permitted to stand if, after excising the portion of the act deemed to be unconstitutional, the remainder carries out the objectives of the Legislature.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT

People v. Stansbury


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


28 A.D.2d 917; 282 N.Y.S.2d 949; 1967 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3564

969 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Benjamin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


28 A.D.2d 106; 281 N.Y.S.2d 405; 1967 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3593



July 6, 1967 



DISPOSITION: Order entered on November 28, 1966, reversed, on the law, defendant's motion for resentence granted, and the matter remanded for the purpose of resentencing defendant without regard to the findings of his prior court-martial conviction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied his motion for resentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant unsuccessfully sought to have set aside a sentence previously imposed on him wherein he was adjudged a second felony offender. The predicate of the finding was a general military court-martial, which had resulted in a conviction of defendant for assault with intent to commit murder. At the court-martial haring, defendant was represented by two young officers, both without any legal training at all. He was found guilty, dishonorably discharged, and confined to hard labor. He contended that the court-martial conviction was under circumstances which did not afford him a fair trial as contemplated by the Constitution of the United States, not having been represented by an attorney, and as such, it was an infirm support for a finding that he was a second felony offender, and therefore he should be resentenced as a first felony offender. The court held that the procedures pursuant to which defendant was convicted were not consonant with any modern or fair concept of due process. The retrospective nature of N.Y. Penal Law § 1943 required that the denial of resentence be reversed and that defendant be resentenced without regard to his prior court-martial conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, granted defendant's motion for resentence, and remanded the matter for the purpose of resentencing defendant without regard to the findings of his prior court-martial conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- subsequent offenders -- military court-martial conviction for crime, felony in New York, wherein defendant was not represented by attorney with legal training cannot serve as predicate for second offender punishment (Penal Law, § 1943).

A conviction of defendant for assault with intent to commit murder, a felony under New York law, by a General Military Court Martial in California in 1943 when defendant was 20 years of age, at which defendant was represented by two young officers without legal training cannot serve as a predicate for second felony offender punishment under section 1943 of the Penal Law. Defendant was denied a fair trial within section 1943, which has retrospective application, by not being represented by an attorney.  [***2] The exigencies of war, at the court martial, did not preclude assignment of counsel with legal training.

 

970 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Paramount Film Distributing Corp. v. State


Claim No. 45976


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


27 A.D.2d 420; 279 N.Y.S.2d 781; 1967 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4175



May 15, 1967 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Claims (New York) denied appellant state's motion to dismiss respondent licensee's claim for the refund of fees paid pursuant to the licensing provisions. The state challenged the order.


OVERVIEW: After the movie licensing provisions of N.Y. Educ. Law § 120 et seq. were held void as violating the Fourteenth Amendment, the claimant filed a claim for the refund of fees paid pursuant to those provisions. The state argued that the licensing fees were voluntarily paid without protest and thus were not recoverable. The court affirmed the denial of the state's motion to dismiss the claimant's action. The claimant would have been prevented from earning a livelihood unless it paid the fee, and thus paid the fee under duress and not voluntarily. The court also rejected the state's argument that the claimant's cause of action accrued when it made the payments. The claimant effectively had no tribunal in which to bring its action until the movie licensing provisions were held void. Until the provisions were held void, there was no competent tribunal in which the claimant could have asserted its claim for recovery of the fees. Therefore, the statute of limitations did not commence to run until the provisions were held void.


OUTCOME: The order that denied the state's motion to dismiss the licensee's claim for the refund of licensing fees was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Theatres and shows -- refund of movie license fees -- claimant paid license fees, without protest, pursuant to section 120 et seq. of Education Law, which was later declared void -- fees were recoverable since there was no statutory requirement for payment under protest, and fees were involuntarily paid under duress solely to stay in business -- subdivision 4 of section 10 of Court of Claims Act does not preclude recovery since claimant effectively had no tribunal to bring action until date statute was voided; Statute of Limitations for filing claim did not commence to run until that date -- Guaranty Trust Co. v. State of New York (299 N. Y. 295) distinguished.

1. Claimant paid license fees, without protest, pursuant to the movie licensing provisions of section 120, et seq., of the Education Law, which provisions were held void on June 10, 1965, as violative of the Fourteenth Amendment. On November 24, 1965, claimant filed a notice of claim with the Court of Claims and in December, 1965, filed a claim [***2] seeking refund of fees paid pursuant to the licensing provisions. The Court of Claims properly denied the State's motion to dismiss the claim on the ground that it fails to state a cause of action or alternatively to dismiss that portion of the claim which relates to payments made before May 24, 1965 on the ground that the Court of Claims lacks jurisdiction. There was no statutory requirement that payment be made under protest, and since the claimant had to pay the license fees to stay in business, and did so solely for that reason, the fees were involuntarily paid under duress and were recoverable.

2. Claimant is not precluded from recovery under subdivision 4 of section 10 of the Court of Claims Act because it did not file a notice of claim within six months of its last payment. The claimant effectively had no tribunal in which to bring the action prior to June 10, 1965, when the provisions were held unconstitutional. The six-month Statute of Limitations did not commence to run until that date.

3. The instant case is distinguishable from Guaranty Trust Co. v. State of New York (299 N. Y. 295) which held that the cause of action for the return of payments made pursuant [***3] to a void statute accrued at the time such payments are made. In Guaranty, suit could have been brought in the Court of Claims despite the ancillary presence of a constitutional issue, while here suit could not be initially brought in the Court of Claims because of the presence of a constitutional issue which was separate and distinct from the monetary recovery. The test is whether the constitutional issue is ancillary to an action for monetary recovery or vice versa. Here, claimant's only avenue of approach was to proceed first in the Supreme Court to have the licensing statute voided and then, if successful, to seek refund in the Court of Claims. This procedure would not provide relief as a practical matter since the statutory period for filing a claim would have elapsed in most instances. Thus, claimant effectively had no tribunal in which to bring its action until June 10, 1965.

 

971 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Iannucci v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


27 A.D.2d 346; 279 N.Y.S.2d 458; 1967 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4263



May 2, 1967 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law and the facts, so as to provide that the Board of Supervisors adopt and submit to Special Term a constitutionally valid plan of reapportionment, not later than 10 days from the date of entry of the order to be entered hereon, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Supervisors of the County of Washington challenged an order of the Supreme Court in Washington County (New York), which found in favor of respondents in their action against the Board and respondent State of New York and declared unconstitutional a permanent apportionment plan utilizing weighted voting.


OVERVIEW: Respondents challenged an apportionment plan based on weighted voting, and the lower court found the plan to be unconstitutional. The Board appealed, and the court modified but affirmed the lower court's order. The court stated that any weighted voting system was unconstitutional not by virtue of the amount of weight assigned but by virtue of the existence of any weighting whatsoever. Such systems did not comport with the constitutional requirement of one vote per person, the court indicated. The court stated that district representation was the only practical method to constitutionally accomplish reapportionment. District representation avoided the pitfalls of weighting, could conform to new federal census data, and minimized changes in district population, the court explained. The court ordered the Board to submit a valid reapportionment plan to it within 10 days of its opinion.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower court's order and, as modified, affirmed it without costs. The court ordered the Board to adopt a constitutionally valid reapportionment plan within 10 days of the court's opinion and to submit that plan to the court.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- weighted voting on county level does not meet requirement of "one person, one vote" and such voting is unconstitutional.

Weighted voting on the county level is unconstitutional and does not satisfy the mandate of "one person, one vote". The problem of over or under representation in the Board of Supervisors is caused not by the amount of weight involved in a particular ward or town, but by the very existence of the weighting system which creates new inequities in terms of power. Representatives of the larger districts, because of the weighting of their votes, necessarily have greater influence [***2] over the passage of legislation and the result is discrimination against the smaller districts. In all reapportionment proceedings on the county level, resort must be had to a district plan providing for electoral districts of equal population.

Lutheran Church in America v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


27 A.D.2d 237; 278 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1967 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4570



March 21, 1967 



DISPOSITION: Order entered on November 21, 1966, unanimously reversed, on the law, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to appellant, and defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant building owner challenged a decision of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed as time barred a complaint by the building owner against respondent Landmarks Preservation Commission.


OVERVIEW: The commission designated the building owner's building as a landmark. The building owner brought an action for a declaratory judgment, which alleged that the local law under which the building was designated a landmark, was unconstitutional. The trial court held that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217, which prescribed a four-month statute of limitations for art. 78 proceedings, was applicable and that the action, commenced approximately eight months after the designation of the building as a landmark, was time barred. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the art. 78 proceeding rules were not applicable because the building owner did not seek to review the factual determination made by the commission, where the statute of limitations might be applicable, but attacked the constitutional power of the administrative body to apply the landmarks law to it in the first instance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, which dismissed as time barred a complaint by the building owner against the commission.


HEADNOTES

Declaratory judgments -- when remedy appropriate -- plaintiff is entitled to bring declaratory judgment action after four-month period of limitation for article 78 CPLR proceeding to test constitutionality of Landmarks Preservation Law (Local Laws, 1965, No. 46 of City of New York), under which its building was declared landmark.

1. Plaintiff is entitled to bring a declaratory judgment action after the four-month period of limitation barring the bringing of an article 78 CPLR proceeding, to test the constitutionality of the Landmarks Preservation Law (Local Laws, 1965, No. 46 of City of New York) under which its building was declared a landmark.

2. Even though an article 78 proceeding is available, petitioner may resort to another remedy where it raises the issue of constitutionality.

3. The exercise of a power [***2] which offends against the Constitution may be attacked at any time.

4. No justiciable controversy could have arisen if plaintiff had brought its action prior to being designated a landmark. The administrative action was necessary to render the action viable but it was not converted thereby into an article 78 proceeding.

5. Plaintiff does not seek to review the factual determination, where the four-month statute might be applicable.

6. The sole issue tendered being a constitutional one involving a pure issue of law, the remedy of declaratory judgment is peculiarly applicable.

Board of Education v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


27 A.D.2d 69; 276 N.Y.S.2d 234; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2693



December 30, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order reversed, on the law, and complaint dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Commissioner of Education of the State of New York (commissioner) sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which declared N.Y. Educ. Law § 701 in violation of the state and federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff school boards sought a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Educ. Law § 701 was unconstitutional insofar as providing for the purchase of textbooks with public funds for free loan to pupils attending religious schools. The trial court declared § 701 in violation of the state and federal constitutions. The court reversed and determined that the school boards did not have standing to challenge a state statute restricting its governmental powers. The court found that the school boards were a public entity created by the state was subject to its direction and control under the aegis of the education department through the commissioner.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the declaration that the education law was in violation of the state and federal constitutions.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- loan of textbooks -- plaintiffs, school boards, have no status to bring action for judgment declaring unconstitutional statute permitting use of State funds to loan books to students in schools under control or direction of religious denomination.

Plaintiffs, school boards, have no standing or status to bring an action for judgment declaring that section 701 of the Education Law, which provides for the purchase of textbooks with public moneys for loan to students enrolled in grades 7 through 12 in public or private schools, is unconstitutional insofar as it permits use of such funds for the purchase of books to be loaned to students in schools under the control or direction of a religious denomination ( City of Buffalo v. State Bd.  [***2] of Equalization, 26 A D 2d 213).

 

974 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rock Hill Sewerage Disposal Corp. v. Thompson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


27 A.D.2d 626; 276 N.Y.S.2d 188; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2703



December 30, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Order reversed, on the law, and motion to dismiss complaint granted, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant town challenged an order of the trial court (New York) denying its motion to dismiss plaintiff sewerage corporation's complaint. The motion was made pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a). The corporation sought a declaratory judgment that the town's ordinance was unconstitutional and asked that the trial court strike the town's action from its records. In the alternative, the corporation asked the court to appoint a condemnation commission.


OVERVIEW: A special sewage district was formed in the town. Shortly thereafter the corporation was formed to service an area of the town in which a summer housing development was located. Only a fraction of the area had been developed. In 1960 the town board passed a resolution pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law §§ 451, 452 providing for an annual sewerage charge for each plat in the area and consenting to the formation of the corporation. In 1965, following the receipt of the petition, the town board passed another resolution that stated that when the 1960 resolution was passed it was the town's intent to only charge sewer rent to those building plots connected to and making use of the sewerage facilities. The corporation argued that the 1965 action was unconstitutional in that it deprived the corporation of property without due process of law. Reversing, the court held that the town's motion to dismiss should have been granted. The court held that the 1960 resolution was illegal and void if it was the intent of the ordinance to charge property owners not using the sewerage system. Hence the 1965 resolution could not have deprived the corporation of something that it never legally acquired.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, on the law, and granted the town's motion to dismiss the corporation's complaint, without costs.

 

975 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Shanker v. Regents of University


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


27 A.D.2d 84; 275 N.Y.S.2d 646; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2725



December 27, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff voter challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York) that denied his motion for summary judgment and granted defendant Board of Regent's motion to dismiss the complaint, which sought a declaration that the Board was unconstitutionally elected or appointed.


OVERVIEW: The voter filed an action, seeking summary judgment declaring that the Board was unconstitutionally elected or appointed, and declaring that N.Y. Educ. Law § 202 was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the voter's motion for summary judgment and granted the Board's motion to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the complaint failed to state a cause of action. Affirming, the court found that § 202 did not violate N.Y. Const. art. V, § 4 because the Board was excepted from the provision requiring appointment by the governor with the advice and consent of the senate. The voter's contention that the election of the Board failed to comply with the one man one vote rule mandated by the Equal Protection Clause was untenable because the members were not elected. The voter's vote was not diluted any more than any other voter because none were allowed to vote for members of the Board. There was no constitutional requirement on the legislature to place any geographical limitations on the selection of any members of the Board. The Board was not a general elective organ. The right to elect members was exclusively restricted to members of the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- Board of Regents -- New York State Constitution (art. V, § 4), which provides for appointment by Governor of heads of certain State departments is not applicable to Board of Regents -- individual plaintiff has no interest to protect in arguing that election of members of Board of Regents by New York State Legislature violates one man one vote rule ( Reynolds v. Sims, 377 U.S. 533) -- election by Legislature not contrary to Seaman v. Fedourich (16 N Y 2d 94).

1. Section 202 of the Education Law, which provides that the Board of Regents shall be composed of 15 members elected by the New York State Legislature, one of whom shall be chosen from each of the State's 11 Judicial Districts, with 4 chosen at large, does not violate section 4 of article V of the New York State Constitution which provides for the appointment by the Govrnor of the heads of certain State departments. This provision [***2] is not meant to apply to the Board of Regents, which was previously excepted in this section.

2. Plaintiff is not in a position to argue that, by reason of legislative and rule-making powers which have been granted to the Board of Regents and also by reason of the fact that one member of the board is chosen from each Judicial District, the Board of Regents violates the one man one vote rule established in Reynolds v. Sims (377 U.S. 533). In that case, the United States Supreme Court held that an individual's right to vote was protected under the Equal Protection Clause and that right is "unconstitutionally impaired when its weight is in a substantial fashion diluted when compared with votes of citizens living in other parts of the State." Plaintiff has no such interest to protect. He cannot now vote for any member nor does he contend he is entitled to such a right.

3. The Court of Appeals stated in Seaman v. Fedourich (16 N Y 2d 94), that "if the [Legislature] may exercise its legislative powers only in a body constituted on a population basis, any general elective municipal organ to which it delegates certain of its powers must * * * be subjected to the same constitutional [***3] requirement." The Board of Regents is not a general elective organ. The right to elect members is exclusively restricted to members of the Legislature.

4. The Court of Appeals in the Seaman decision further stated "that a person has a substantial right to be heard and to participate, through his elected representatives, in the business of government on an equal basis with all other individuals". The members of the Board of Regents are not the elected representatives of the plaintiff.

Insurance Co. of North America v. Senior


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


27 A.D.2d 24; 275 N.Y.S.2d 749; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2761



December 23, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant chairman challenged an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), granting the petition of respondent insurance carrier, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, annulling and setting aside two assessments made by appellant under N.Y. Work. Comp. Law §§ 15(8), para. h, 25-a, 151.


OVERVIEW: Respondent issued workmen's compensation policies paying workers pursuant to Workmen's Compensation Board awards. Under N.Y. Work. Comp. Law § 44, respondent received recoupment payments from other carriers and self-insurers representing the apportioned amounts of the liability of prior employers who were jointly responsible for the worker's disease. Appellant, as Chairman of the Workmen's Compensation Board of the State of New York, made assessments based on the total compensation payments respondent made, refusing to allow a deduction of the amount of the recoupment payments. The court held that the term "compensation" in the statute was not intended to include the same payments as the basis of liability for both the payee and payor in computing the assessments. Respondent as the last employer, was required to pay the entire award. Ultimate responsibility rested on all the employers involved in the ratio of the apportionment provided by § 44. Respondent was, therefore, entitled to a deduction of the recoupment payments received from prior employers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and judgment.


HEADNOTES

Workmen's compensation -- carriers -- assessments -- respondent, insurance carrier engaged in issuing workmen's compensation policies, is entitled to deduction of recoupment payments received from prior employers pursuant to section 44 of Workmen's Compensation Law in computation of assessments provided by sections 15 (subd. 8, par. [h]), 25-a and 151 of Workmen's Compensation Law.

Respondent is an insurance carrier engaged in the business of issuing workmen's compensation policies and, during the fiscal year ending March 31, 1964, paid the sum of $ 2,297,726.42 to workers pursuant to awards made by the Workmen's Compensation Board. Pursuant to the provisions of section 44 of the Workmen's Compensation Law respondent received recoupment payments during the fiscal year ending March 31, 1964 in the sum of $ 197,831.63 from other carriers and self-insurers representing the apportioned [***2] amounts of the liability of prior employers who were jointly responsible for the worker's disease. Respondent is entitled to a deduction of these recoupment payments received from prior employers pursuant to section 44 of the Workmen's Compensation Law in the computation of the assessments provided by sections 15 (subd. 8, par. [h]), 25-a and 151 of the Workmen's Compensation Law, which provide for the computation of the assessments on the "total compensation".

Home Gas Co. v. Banach


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


26 A.D.2d 758; 272 N.Y.S.2d 183; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3542



July 20, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Orders modified so as to provide that the defendants' counterclaims be severed, and that the issue of lawful operation, as well as those of public necessity and good faith, be determined at trial term, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant property owners sought review of a judgment of a trial court (New York), which granted the motion of plaintiff gas company to dismiss certain of property owners' separate defenses, some of which were counterclaims, directed trials of certain issues, and vacated the owners' demands for bills of particulars in the gas company's action for condemnation under N.Y. Condemn. Law art. 3-A of storage rights beneath the owners' lands.


OVERVIEW: The property owners' answers to the gas company's condemnation action raised issues of the gas company's lawful operation, public necessity, and good faith. The answers also alleged that N.Y. Condemnation Law art. 3-A, § 85 was unconstitutional. The owners argued that the section created a distinction between companies, which violated the equal protection provisions of the federal and state constitutions. The owners counterclaimed for conversion of underground gas and trespass to land. The court held on appeal that a discriminatory statute could be challenged only by the persons personally aggrieved. C.P.L.R. 603.Finally, the court held that dismissal of the bill of particulars was proper because the owners demands were not properly within the scope of a bill of particulars.

Wickham v. Trapani


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


26 A.D.2d 216; 272 N.Y.S.2d 6; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3559



July 15, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant apple grower sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court at Trial Term (New York), which favored plaintiff state agriculture and markets commissioner by declaring provisions of N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 25 to be constitutional and an apple marketing order of the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets promulgated thereunder to be legally valid in all respects.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the apple growing industry was subject to regulation by the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets (commissioner), and the use of the disjunctive "or" in N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 294(d)(2) made it clear that the commissioner was given discretion in the selection of the class to assess for advertising, promotion and publicity programs. Defendant regarded the assessment imposed by the commissioner as a tax and complained that because he derived no profit from his apple-raising endeavors, he should not be compelled to pay the assessment. The court determined, however, that the assessment was not a tax at all, but a fund-raising measure incidental to a valid regulation. The court held that the wisdom of the marketing order and the equitability of its application were administrative matters outside the scope its power to review in an action for a declaratory judgment, and it found that the apple growers assertions of illegality could not be sustained.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that declared that the assessment against apple growers promulgated by the commissioner was legal.


HEADNOTES

Agriculture -- marketing of agricultural products -- apple marketing order -- judgment declaring article 25 of Agriculture and Markets Law constitutional and apple marketing order thereunder valid is affirmed -- apple-growing industry is subject to State regulation and control -- means selected by Commissioner to provide publicity to create new markets for apples were not arbitrary -- statute gives Commissioner power to select class on which assessment should fall -- assessment is not tax -- notice of referendum to be held was sufficient in view of absence of list of apple growers -- public money was not involved since taxing power of State was not used -- payments to apple growers associations for services were not gift of State moneys -- no unlawful delegation of legislative power to apple growers -- plaintiff cannot claim that defendant should have brought timely proceeding under article  [***2] 78 CPLR to review apple marketing order -- wisdom of order and equitability of its application are administrative matters not subject to review.

1. A judgment declaring article 25 of the Agriculture and Markets Law constitutional and an apple marketing order of the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets issued thereunder to be valid is affirmed.

2. The apple-growing industry bears sufficiently on the public interest to be subject to State regulation and control.

3. The means selected by the Commissioner to provide advertising, promotion and publicity programs designed to create new markets for apples, to maintain existing ones, to carry on marketing research and to keep growers informed of various aspects of the industry -- all within the contemplation of the grant of power contained in paragraph (d) of subdivision (2) of section 294 of the Agriculture and Markets Law -- were not so unrelated to the conditions of "unreasonable and unnecessary economic waste of the agricultural wealth of this state", the existence of which the enactment of article 25 was designed to correct (Agriculture and Markets Law, § 292), as to be considered arbitrary and violative of due process.

4.  [***3] Paragraph (d) of subdivision (2) of section 294 of the Agriculture and Markets Law does not require all persons mentioned in the statute, i.e., producers, processors, distributors or handlers, to pay a part of the assessment to defray the cost and expenses of the administration of the marketing order. The Legislature's use of the disjunctive "or" makes clear that it was intended to leave to the discretion of the Commissioner the selection of the class upon which the assessment should fall.

5. The assessment imposed by the Commissioner here involved is not a tax but a fund-raising measure incidental to a valid regulation and this is true even though the assessment may be the "heart" of the regulatory order. The expenditure of the funds raised redounds primarily to the benefit of appellant and of all other apple producers from an anticipated expansion of markets for their product. That other classes involved in apple marketing may also be benefited is or could be found to be incidental.

6. The posted notices and the newspaper and radio publicity which the referendum received together with the instructions given to all County Agricultural Agents in the State to place the notice of [***4] the referendum in the hands of growers, which presumably were followed or at least not shown to have been disregarded, were, in the absence of an established list of persons engaged in apple growing which rendered the giving of personal notice a practical impossibility, reasonably calculated under all of the circumstances to apprise interested persons of the upcoming referendum and thus measured up to the quality which the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the Federal Constitution requires.

7. The declaration in the statute that the proceeds of the assessment are not to be considered State funds (Agriculture and Markets Law, § 294, subd. [8]) fortified by the fact that the assessments were not exacted by the use of the taxing power of the State negates the contention that the Commissioner was handling public money.

8. The payments to apple growers associations for services to be performed cannot be deemed a gift in any sense since they were made in reimbursement for services performed pursuant to subsisting contracts. The provisions of section 174 of the State Finance Law have no application to contracts of this character.

9. Article 25 does not delegate legislative [***5] power unlawfully to apple growers.

10. Since plaintiff sought the declaration that his marketing order was constitutional and legal, plaintiff cannot deprive defendant of his right to raise defenses against the statute and order by claiming that defendant should have raised them in a timely challenge to the marketing order under article 78 of the CPLR.

11. The wisdom of the marketing order and the equitability of its application are administrative matters without the scope of the court's power to review in an action for a declaratory judgment.

 

979 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Port Jefferson v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


26 A.D.2d 700; 272 N.Y.S.2d 617; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3579



July 12, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the Village of Port Jefferson and its treasurer, appealed a decision from the trial court (New York), which dismissed their action against defendants, the Board of Supervisors and the County Treasurer of the County of Suffolk. The action claimed that Suffolk County, N.Y. Charter § 210 and § 203 were invalid.


OVERVIEW: A town voted to join the County police district. At the time of the vote, the town's contained unincorporated areas. Two areas within the town then incorporated into a Village. The Village wanted to withdraw from the County police district in order to establish its own police department. The Village and its treasurer initiated an action, claiming §§ 201 and 203 were invalid. The trial court found that § 203 was invalid because it violated the one-man, one-vote principle, but that it did not invalidate the rest of the County Charter. The trial court then dismissed the action. In affirming the decision, the court found that the town's election to join the County police district was binding on the Village. By subsequently incorporating, the Village residents did not acquire the right to a second vote on whether or not to join the County police district. The court reasoned that a second vote would give the Village residents unequal and greater rights than the residents of any other town or village.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

980 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Madole v. Barnes


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


26 A.D.2d 681; 272 N.Y.S.2d 647; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3638



July 6, 1966 - Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Board of Supervisors of Orange County (New York) and others, challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court, Orange County (New York), which declared certain rules and regulations of the Board to be unconstitutional and directed a permit applied for by respondent applicant to issue upon the filing of a new application and payment of the required fee.


OVERVIEW: The Board had previously adopted a rule that certain rooms in the county court houses, when not used for court purposes, could be used for public meetings of political parties. The applicant, on behalf of the National Renaissance Party (NRP), applied for use of a court house, and the application was denied because the NRP was not a political party. The trial court granted the applicant's request to compel appellants to grant permission to use the court house. In reversing, the court found that the rule concerning political parties was unconstitutionally discriminatory. However, the application was not made by the NRP, nor did the petition allege that it was a political party. Even if it were, the permit would not have to be granted if the expression of the NRP's views would immediately and irreparably create injury to the public.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remitted the case to the trial court.

983 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Attoma v. State Dep't of Social Welfare


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


26 A.D.2d 12; 270 N.Y.S.2d 167; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4185



May 19, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed on the law and facts, without costs of this appeal to any party and petition dismissed, without costs. Certain findings of fact disapproved and reversed and new findings and conclusions made.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent social welfare department (department) appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Monroe County at a Trial Term (New York), which directed the department to process a filing by petitioner applicants for the operation of a nursing home.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the delay in processing the application was caused by certain other facts that none of the parties has emphasized and as to which the trial court made no findings. The land upon which the nursing home was to be constructed was zoned such that the proposed building violated the permissible use in such a zone. These impediments required the granting of a variance by the zoning board. After the first hearing the request was denied but was granted following a second hearing, the day before a new law, 1964 N.Y. Laws ch. 730, became effective. The court found that this procedural issue was the proximate cause of the delay and not any act of the applicants. The granting of a variance was an obvious condition precedent to further action. Because the erection of the nursing home was prohibited by the zoning regulation, the court reversed the judgment for the applicants and dismissed the petition.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment and dismissed the petition of the applicants filing for permission to build a nursing home, which had been denied by the department.


HEADNOTES

Hospitals -- private proprietary nursing home -- State Department of Social Welfare should not be required to process application (to operate private proprietary nursing home) in accordance with statute (Social Welfare Law) and department's rules as they existed prior to October 1, 1964, effective date of amendment of statute, where applicants' delay or failure timely to execute and file documents properly required  [***2] by department in connection with application was due not to department's acts or statements to applicants but to latters' failure timely to receive variance from local zoning board necessary for proposed building -- 1964 amendment to statute was not unconstitutional as unreasonable exercise of police power.

1. A judgment that petitioners' application to operate a private proprietary nursing home be processed by the State Department of Social Welfare in accordance with the Social Welfare Law and the department's rules and regulations as they existed before October 1, 1964, the effective date of amendments (L. 1964, ch. 730) to the statute imposing further prerequisites to the establishment of such nursing homes, should be reversed. The trial court erred in concluding that various documents, demanded by the department in June, 1964 under the then effective statute and rules but never submitted by the applicants, such as certificates of incorporation and of doing business under an assumed name, the applicants' partnership agreement, and certain leases, were not required to be filed and that revised construction plans were timely filed on September 25, 1964 so that the department [***3] with due diligence could have approved the application before October 1, 1964. The trial court found that such papers were not executed because applicants deemed further action futile following the department's advising their attorney on September 4, 1964 of a July 28, 1964 resolution of the Board of Social Welfare reciting that applications received after July 31, 1964 could not be processed in regular order before October 1, 1964 and should, therefore, be processed in accordance with the new law. However, the petitioners' application had previously been given preferential treatment in both the Rochester and Albany offices of the department which had indicated that it saw no reason why the plans should not be approved. Moreover, it is apparent that the proximate cause of the delay in executing and filing the documents was not any act of the department but the applicants' failure to obtain the local zoning board's approval of the variance required for construction of the building planned on the proposed site until September 30, 1964 -- the day before the new law became effective.

2. The 1964 amendment (L. 1964, ch. 730) of the Social Welfare Law, requiring an applicant for approval [***4] of the operation of a nursing home to establish the "need" for such new facility, was not unconstitutional as an unreasonable exercise of the police power. The legislation resulted from an exhaustive study by a joint legislative committee, and it is recognized that the operation of nursing homes bears a reasonable relation to the health, safety and welfare of a community and is subject to licensing and regulation as a valid exercise of the police power.

 

984 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jackson


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


25 A.D.2d 783; 269 N.Y.S.2d 651; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4449



April 25, 1966 - Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed an order of the County Court, Dutchess County (New York), which denied without a hearing his application for coram nobis relief vacating his sentence as a second felony offender and resentencing him as a first offender. He claimed that the sentence was based on a prior Florida conviction, which would not have been a felony if committed in New York and that he was deprived of counsel in the Florida proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted in Florida of aggravated assault in violation of Fla. Stat. Ann. § 784.04. Upon conviction in New York, the trial court sentenced him as a second felony offender based on the Florida conviction. He filed a motion for resentence, which the trial court treated as one for coram nobis relief. The trial court denied relief, holding that the Florida crime would have been a felony in New York and that the contention of unlawful deprivation of counsel had to be made in Florida. On appeal, the court agreed that the application was one for coram nobis relief and that the Florida crime would have been a felony in New York. However, the court reversed, disagreeing with the trial court's finding that petitioner had to make his claim of deprivation of counsel in the Florida proceedings in the courts of that State. That assertion could be made in a coram nobis proceeding in New York under N.Y. Penal Law § 1943, and if it were sustained, it was not correct to use the Florida conviction as the predicate for a second-offender sentence. Petitioner made a sufficient prima facie showing of a deprivation of counsel in the Florida proceedings to require a hearing on that issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of petitioner's application for coram nobis relief and remanded to the trial court for a hearing on the question whether petitioner was improperly deprived of the assistance of counsel during the Florida proceedings culminating in his prior conviction.

 

985 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schenley Industries, Inc. v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


25 A.D.2d 285; 268 N.Y.S.2d 848; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4466



April 19, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Determination of the respondent State Liquor Authority annulled on the law, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to petitioner.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner liquor distributor commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding to annul a determination by respondent State Liquor Authority (New York), which claimed that the distributor violated N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 101(1)(c) and N.Y. State Liquor Authority R. 11, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 86 by issuing credit cards fort the purchase of alcohol at certain selected restaurants.


OVERVIEW: The Authority instituted a proceeding against the distributor, alleging that the distributor violated N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 101(1)(c) and N.Y. State Liquor Authority R. 11, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 6 by issuing credit cards for the purchase of alcoholic beverages in certain selected restaurants. The Authority claimed that the credit cards were improper gifts that improperly influenced potential customers. The distributor replied that the issuance of the credit cards did not violate either N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 101(1)(c) and N.Y. State Liquor Authority R. 11, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 86. The distributor was found to have been in violation of the statute and the rule, and the distributor sought to annul this determination. The court held that N.Y. State Liquor Authority R. 11, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 86 did not interdict services or gifts that were de minimis or customary in nature. The credit cards benefited the distributor's own staff and the related personnel of the distributor's suppliers. The credit cards were not issued to the public in general, and there was no advertising that drew patrons to the participating restaurants.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the determination by the Authority.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- credit cards -- credit card plan put in operation by subsidiary corporation organized by petitioner is not in violation of Alcoholic Beverage Control Law ( § 101), which makes it unlawful for manufacturer or wholesaler to make gift or render service to licensee which may tend to influence licensee to purchase manufacturer's product.

 Section 101 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law makes it unlawful for a manufacturer or wholesaler to make a gift or render any service directly or indirectly to a licensee which may tend to influence such licensee to purchase the manufacturer's product. Petitioner [***2] organized a subsidiary corporation which put into operation a plan generally similar to the pattern of commercial credit charge plans. Credit cards were issued. Holders of the cards could make purchases on credit at restaurants which had previously agreed to recognize them. These restaurants billed the corporation for the sales so made. This company paid the charges and, in turn, billed the card holders. Cards were not issued to the public generally but only to officers and employees who were on a management level and had expense accounts of the petitioner, and to officers and employees of similar grade of petitioner's suppliers and advertising agencies. There was no general solicitation of restaurants to accept these credit cards. Prospective card holders were asked for the names of restaurants which they usually patronized and from this a list was made up. It was pointed out to each restaurant contacted that this was not a promotional scheme to increase sales and, in fact, at least one restaurant on this list did not serve liquor at all. All expenses were borne by petitioner. This plan does not violate the statute. While the service rendered is material, it primarily benefits [***3] petitioner's own staff and related personnel. There was no advertising which drew patrons to the participating restaurants, and the selection was limited to institutions which already enjoyed the patronage of the persons who could use the cards.
