987 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rochester Business Institute, Inc. v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


25 A.D.2d 97; 267 N.Y.S.2d 274; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4860



February 24, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, on the law and facts, without costs of this appeal to either party and declaratory judgment directed in favor of defendant in accordance with the opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court in Monroe County (New York), which held that N.Y. Gen. City Law § 35, the city's official map, and ordinances establishing the map were unconstitutional to the extent that they prevented plaintiff landowner from constructing a building within an area designated for proposed street widening.


OVERVIEW: Rochester, N.Y. Ordinance No. 2174 provided for the widening of a street abutting the landowners' property. The first landowner had a long-term lease of the second landowner's property and sought to construct on both parcels a building containing offices for rent and a school. The city refused to issue construction permits because part of the proposed building was to be constructed in the street proposed in the ordinance. The trial court held that the ordinance and N.Y. Gen. Cities Law § 35 were unconstitutional as applied to the landowners because erecting a building of the same size outside the proposed street bed would cost more than the proposed building. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the building restriction did not result in a taking of the landowners' property without compensation. The court reasoned that the increased cost of constructing the same size building outside the proposed street bed was nominal and the widening of the street was necessary due to increased use and congestion. The court noted that the landowners had been negotiating with the city in an attempt to sell the portion of their land within the proposed street bed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that held that the statute and ordinance that prevented the landowner from erecting a building in a proposed street bed resulted in a taking by the city of the landowners' property without compensation.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- street plans -- section 35 of General City Law forbidding issuance of building permit for land in bed of proposed street, and street plan of defendant city which, on street where plaintiffs own land, requires 14-foot setback of building to permit later street widening, are constitutional as applied to plaintiffs' land -- reasonable use can be had of plaintiffs' land, even with setback, by trivial increase in cost.

1. Section 35 of the General City Law forbids the issuance of a permit for building in the bed of a mapped street laid out in a plan. Defendant [***2] city's street plan, as amended in 1963, requires plaintiff corporations, which propose to build on one such street, to set back the construction 14 feet so as to permit later widening of the street by 14 feet. Plaintiffs' evidence fails to rebut the presumption of constitutionality of the section and of the street plan as applied to their land. The city's requirement of a 14-foot setback is a valid exercise of the police power and consonant with sound city planning.

2. Moreover, reasonable and profitable use can be had of plaintiffs' property by setting the proposed building back 14 feet at an increase of 6% in the construction cost with little or no loss in rental income. Such extra cost is trivial damage compared to the great injury to the general welfare which will result if the city is prevented from planning for this essential improvement in traffic conditions.

 

988 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Roosevelt Raceway, Inc. v. County of Nassau


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


25 A.D.2d 535; 267 N.Y.S.2d 591; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4962



February 14, 1966 - Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner raceway filed an original action to challenge a determination from respondent Comptroller of Nassau County, which was made after a hearing and assessing a deficiency against the raceway on account of county admission taxes. The raceway also sought a judgment that 1956 N.Y. Laws 837 and Nassau County, N.Y., Local Laws No. 1 (1956) were unconstitutional and void.


OVERVIEW: 1956 N.Y. Laws 837, an enabling act, provided that certain cities or counties could enact a local law, which would require a tax of 30 percent on the admission price to a harness horse race meeting, while requiring that the tax was to remain at 15 percent of the admission price in all other cities and counties. The county adopted Nassau County, N.Y., Local Laws No. 1 (1956), a local law imposing a 30 percent tax on admissions to harness horse race meetings within the county. Although the admission tax was imposed on the patrons, the raceway had to collect it and was personally liable for the amount thereof. The court held that: (1) even though there was no dispute as to the amount of unpaid taxes, the proceeding could be maintained to test the constitutionality of 1956 N.Y. Laws 837 and Nassau County, N.Y., Local Laws No. 1 (1956); (2) the raceway was a party aggrieved, with standing to maintain the proceeding; (3) the raceway was not estopped from doing so even though it had availed itself of substantial benefits conferred by independent and separable provisions of 1956 N.Y. Laws 837; and (4) both the enabling statute and the local law were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the determination from the Comptroller upholding the constitutionality of a statute and local law providing for an admissions tax to horse race meetings, and granted the raceway's petition..

 

989 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gowanda v. County of Erie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


25 A.D.2d 18; 266 N.Y.S.2d 439; 1966 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5111



January 20, 1966 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, without costs of this appeal to any party and petition dismissed on the merits, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county appealed the decision from the Supreme Court in Erie County (New York), which entered judgment in favor of petitioner village in its action to direct the county to pay a portion of the proceeds from a sales and use tax imposed by the county. The dispute required an interpretation of the county tax and a state enabling act, 1947 N.Y. Laws ch. 278, as amended.


OVERVIEW: The village was located partially in the county and partially in another county. The enabling act allowed the use tax to be applied to the village instead of directly reducing property taxes. The question was whether the village was entitled to the entire tax benefit or only for that portion residing in the county. The court reversed. Under 1947 N.Y. Laws ch. 278, § 4, the tax only had an application within the territorial limits of the county or city imposing the tax. The court reasoned that that language indicated that the tax could only be applied within the portion of the village in the county. The court looked to other sections of the enabling act, particularly regarding the division of taxes for school purposes, to support its reasoning. The court rejected the self-serving comments of the village that it would only use the taxes for residents of the county. While under the village's statement some taxes would be reduced, the residents of the other county would also benefit. Further, the village probably could not divide up the tax benefit under the rule of the equality and uniformity of taxation.


OUTCOME: The trial court was reversed and the village's petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- County Sales and Compensating Use Tax -- apportionment to village -- village partly in Erie County, with one third of population residing therein is not entitled to share in Erie County's Sales and Compensating Use Tax proceeds where village resolution seeking portion of proceeds does not allocate proceeds to reduce taxes of taxpayers in Erie County -- act enabling county to adopt tax shows no intention to permit use of tax proceeds for extraterritorial benefit -- allegation in village's petition of Mayor's willingness to reduce village taxes of residents of Erie County has no binding effect.

1. Chapter 278 of the Laws of 1947 (as amd.) enabled certain counties, including Erie County, to enact [***2] a County Sales and Compensating Use Tax, and further provided that the county should pay to a village which adopted a suitable resolution, a portion of the County Sales and Compensating Use Tax which would have been applied to reduce the county tax and general town tax levied upon real property in the village. Section 4 of chapter 278 provided that any tax imposed "shall have application only within the territorial limits of the county * * * imposing such tax". Section 11 of chapter 278 provided for a distribution of the taxes raised according to relative population figures, and for educational purposes, taxes paid to school districts should be "in accordance with the total average daily attendance of the pupils in such school district who reside in such county and in any such case, the amount to be raised by school taxes by the district from the portion of such district within such county shall be reduced by the amount so distributed." Erie County adopted such taxes and in the resolution of adoption provided that receipts from sales of items delivered or to be delivered outside the county should not be subject to the tax. Petitioner village is partly in Erie County with approximately [***3] one third of its population in Erie County and two thirds in Cattaraugus County. It adopted a resolution electing to receive a portion of the county tax, the resolution not restricting the use of the funds to the benefit of the real property within the village lying in Erie County. The enabling act, section 12 of chapter 278, provides that such taxes distributed to a village, are available for any village purpose unless restricted by the resolution to a specified purpose. There was no legislative intention that the money of the taxpayers of Erie County could be used for the extraterritorial benefit of nonresidents. The payment of the moneys involved to petitioner village for general tax purposes was never contemplated by either the enabling act or the county resolution. It would be violative of the general principles of equality and uniformity of taxation to permit such a distribution.

2. An allegation in the village's petition, verified by its Mayor, that it is willing to apply its share of the tax to reduce the village taxes paid by residents who are residents of Erie County is of little binding effect. It does not allege that the share will be used only to reduce village [***4] taxes paid by residents of Erie County. Moreover, the Mayor had no authority to depart from the specific language of the resolution itself or to impose restrictions upon the use of the money not contained in the resolution.

 

990 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bugeja v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


24 A.D.2d 151; 266 N.Y.S.2d 80; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2836



December 9, 1965 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action to enjoin a bond issue, plaintiff citizen appealed an order and judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant city and declared New York City, N.Y., Laws ch. 440 (1965) to be constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The city proposed to issue bonds to pay for civil servants' pensions. A citizen brought an action to enjoin the bond issue and to declare New York City, N.Y., Laws ch. 440 (Law) (1965) unconstitutional. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the city, and declared the Law constitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that a verified debt statement from the New York City Comptroller was prima facie evidence that the proposed bond issue would not exceed the maximum debt margin allowed by the Law, and that the citizen failed to submit any proof to counter the evidence. The court further held that the city's payment of mandatory pension and retirement liabilities was a matter of concern to the state of New York; therefore, the Law was exempt from the home rule requirements of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2. Similarly, the city's power to issue bonds was also an area of concern to the state of New York. The court found that payments of pension and retirement liabilities were not payments of purely transient usefulness, and that the Law did not clearly usurp any prohibited power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment granting summary judgment in favor of the city and declaring the statute constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- New York City bonds -- chapter 440 of Laws of 1965, which empowers Mayor of City of New York to authorize issuance of serial bonds to provide for city's payment of its pension or retirement liabilities during fiscal year 1965, is constitutional with respect to its objects and its aims.

1. Chapter 440 of the Laws of 1965 empowers the Mayor of the City of New York to authorize the issuance of serial bonds to provide for the city's payment of its pension or retirement liabilities during the fiscal year 1965. The statute is constitutional with respect to its object and its means.

2. Although plaintiff argues that a factual issue exists in support of his claim that the bond sale will cause the city to exceed its constitutional debt limitation (N. Y. Const., art. VIII, § 4, subd. [c]), he failed to submit at Special Term any proof of any evidentiary [***2] fact which tended to contravene the prima facie validity of the net debt margin figure contained in the City Comptroller's verified debt statement submitted by respondents (Local Finance Law, § 109.00; CPLR 4520).

3. Payment by the city of its mandatory pension or retirement liabilities is a matter of State concern exempting the statute from the home rule article of the Constitution (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 2).

4. The power of the city to issue evidences of indebtedness constitutes an area of State concern. In such area, the Constitution contemplates enactment by the Legislature of special laws affecting the power of local governments to finance by issuance of evidences of indebtedness.

5. Payments by the city of current pension or retirement liabilities do not involve payments of "purely transient usefulness".

6. It is not necessary that the city's mandatory pension or retirement liabilities must first be reduced to judgment before the Legislature may constitutionally enact a special law to enable the city to finance those fixed liabilities.

7. There is not, in the statute, a clear usurpation by the Legislature of prohibited power, nor is there any warrant to go beyond the [***3] face of the statute in determining its constitutionality.

 

991 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Petition of Orans


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


24 A.D.2d 217; 265 N.Y.S.2d 49; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2839



December 9, 1965 



DISPOSITION: Order, entered on September 2, 1965, affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Governor of the State of New York challenged an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term, New York County (New York), which granted a motion filed by respondent majority leaders of the New York Legislature to intervene and ordered that reapportionment legislation that complied with U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the New York Constitution be enacted on or before February 1, 1966, or that the lower court would formulate a plan.


OVERVIEW: A United States District Court found that the State's apportionment legislation was unconstitutional. The Legislature adopted interim legislation subject to the approval of the U.S. District Court. The U.S. District Court found that the legislation did not violate the United States Constitution. The action in the case at bar challenged the constitutionality of the interim legislation under the New York Constitution. The lower court found that the interim legislation was unconstitutional in that it violated N.Y. Const. art. III, § 2 because the legislation called for 165 members in the Assembly when § 2 only authorized 150 members in the Assembly. On appeal, the court found no error in the lower court's orders. The court concluded that judicial review of apportionment legislation included the power to retain jurisdiction pending corrective legislative action. The court determined that the lower court had the power to formulate an apportionment plan if the Legislature failed to formulation such a plan under decisions of the United States Supreme Court. The court ruled that all three branches of the State government had a duty to ensure constitutionally compliant apportionment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- apportionment of Legislature -- order of State Supreme Court properly provided that if Legislature failed to enact reapportionment plan, valid under Federal and State Constitutions, by February 1, 1966, latest feasible date for such plan if election is to be held in 1966, court would take appropriate steps toward formulation of such plan -- no violation of doctrine of separation of powers -- failure of Legislature to respond to its obligation to apportion State to effectuate compliance with Federal and State Constitutions as directed by State and Federal courts will not preclude reapportionment by State courts which have constitutional obligation as to reapportionment equal and coextensive with that of Legislature.

1. An order of the Supreme Court of New York State properly provided that if by February 1, 1966 a reapportionment plan had not been enacted in accordance with Federal and State court directives for legislative apportionment in compliance not only with the Fourteenth Amendment of the Federal Constitution, but also with those [***3] provisions of the State Constitution not in conflict with the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, then the court itself would take appropriate steps toward the formulation of such a plan. Although the Legislature has the primary obligation to apportion, its failure to respond to such obligation will not preclude reapportionment and the courts under such circumstances are not required to stand by helplessly when this primarily legislative function is not discharged. The judiciary, which also has a primary obligation, to review a legislative apportionment (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 5), has a constitutional obligation in respect of reapportionment equal and coextensive with that of the Legislature, and has the duty, as well as the Legislature, to preserve the State's constitutional government.

2. Accordingly, the State courts would be derelict in performing their duties if they were not to intervene, upon request, to insure that the People of the State have the right to which they are entitled of constitutionally valid representation in a Legislature elected in accordance with both Federal and State constitutional standards, so that the present method of apportionment,  [***4] directed by the United States courts but concededly in contravention of the State Constitution, will not continue indefinitely or until a Federal court formulates a new plan.

3. For the legislative apportionment to be effective, it must be timely. Although it may not be assumed that the 1966 Legislature will not discharge its obligation to reapportion in compliance with the order of the United States District Court, nevertheless, such reapportionment must be enacted by February 1, 1966 if it is to be reviewed in the light of applicable provisions of the State Constitution, inasmuch as legislative and judicial action thereafter toward a reapportionment valid under the State and Federal Constitutions is, indisputedly, not feasible for elections to be held in 1966.

4. The language of the order that respondents "shall submit such a plan" is not considered a directive to the Legislature. The order in its entirety merely states that if such action is not taken the court will take steps to formulate such plan and thus provide for filling the vacuum which would then exist. Thus the order does not violate the spirit of the doctrine of separation of powers. The judiciary is not arrogating [***5] political power but, on the contrary, has been and is giving the Legislature every opportunity to exercise its primary obligation of reapportionment with the caveat that if it fails to do so timely, the court will fashion reapportionment to comply with State and Federal constitutional requirements.

5. The orders of the United States District Court directing an election in 1965 of a 165-member Assembly, pursuant to "Plan A" (the Reapportionment Compliance Act -- L. 1964, ch. 976), served only to suspend until the end of 1966 the State constitutional requirement of a 150-member Assembly (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 2). "Plan A" is but a temporary measure; the Federal courts have abstained from passing on the validity of the plan under the State Constitution, and the determination of the plan's invalidity thereunder (15 N Y 2d 339, affg. 45 Misc 2d 616) fails to present any substantial Federal question.

6. No potential confrontation of power is discerned among co-ordinate elements of the State government. Where the State constitutional formula and the legislative apportionment based thereon fail to comply with the Fourteenth Amendment to the Federal Constitution, there is concurrent [***6] Federal and State jurisdiction under Federal aegis to effectuate compliance not only with the Fourteenth Amendment but also with those State constitutional provisions which do not impinge on the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. The Supremacy Clause of the Federal Constitution is operating to compel conformity with the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment which the State constitutional formulas for apportionment have been held to violate, and under Federal mandate the 1965 and 1966 Legislatures have been elected in contravention of the State Constitution. However, the Federal court has afforded several opportunities for legislative reapportionment complying with Federal and State constitutional requirements; has deferred to the State courts and abstained from passing on State constitutional reapportionment questions, but has retained jurisdiction pending a permanent reapportionment satisfying both State and Federal constitutional provisions.

 

992 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


De Sena v. Gulde


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


24 A.D.2d 165; 265 N.Y.S.2d 239; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2865



December 6, 1965 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with $ 10 costs and disbursements to plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant village trustees challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Nassau County (New York), which granted a motion by respondent landowner for summary judgment and adjudged a purported amendment of the zoning ordinance of the village to be illegal and void.


OVERVIEW: The landowner was possessed of a vacant parcel of land in the village. Claiming that a zoning ordinance adopted by the village trustees deprived him of his property in violation of his constitutional and statutory rights, the landowner filed a cause of action for a declaratory judgment that the ordinance was illegal and void. After neighboring property owners were allowed to intervene in support of the ordinance, the landowner sought summary judgment, which was granted. The village trustees contended that: (1) the ordinance was presumptively valid; (2) there were facts in existence which sustained the adoption of the ordinance; and (3) a trial was required. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court rejected the village trustees' claim of justification, which rested on the broad objective of "general welfare". They urged that the conditions of public disorder and prospective economic loss within the village were fairly within the competence of a municipal legislative body to consider in the adoption of a zoning ordinance. The court found that the village trustees had authority to address the problem, but their zoning power was not the correct method to achieve it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- after Board of Trustees of Village of Hempstead adopted amendment to village zoning ordinance placing plaintiff's property in light manufacturing district in accord with master plan which had been prepared and approved, board changed zoning to residence "A" district because of boycotting, picketing and threats of economic damage -- change of zoning to residence "A" district was illegal and void -- presumption of validity rebutted -- board did not follow its own master plan or its own conviction as to most appropriate use of land.

1. After plaintiff had acquired his property, the Board of Trustees of the Village of Hempstead adopted an amendment to the village zoning ordinance by which plaintiff's property, along with adjoining property, was placed in a light manufacturing district [***2] in accord with a master plan which had been prepared and approved. Opposition subsequent to the adoption of the amendment took the form of threats of economic boycott against the merchants of the village, of picketing the village hall and the shopping section, and of demonstrations. At a hearing subsequently held, the Mayor stated that, while the fact that the property was in a negro neighborhood had no bearing on the decision of the board, the boycotting, picketing and threats were made by those who sought to make a race issue out of the action. The board then adopted an amendment to the zoning ordinance placing the same area, including plaintiff's property, in a residence "A" district. The amendment of the zoning ordinance was properly declared illegal and void.

2. Judicial duty requires that an ordinance be declared invalid whenever the municipal power has been exceeded, or has been exercised in an arbitrary or discriminatory fashion. Here the board, for reasons stated in the Mayor's address as "the obligation to prevent riots and injuries to residents, merchants, and shoppers", and the duty not "to make the merchants suffer", passed the amendment changing the proper zoning.  [***3] Plaintiff's proof has thus rebutted the presumption of validity.

3. General welfare and the possibility of public disorder and prospective economic loss within the village do not justify the board's action.

4. The board did not oppose the use of the Mayor's statement on the ground that the motives of a legislative body may not be the subject of inquiry in determining the validity of legislation. Where the motivation is disclosed on the face of the act, that rule does not apply. The statement was properly admissible in weighing the validity of the action.

5. Section 177 of the Village Law directs that the zoning regulations "shall be made in accordance with a comprehensive plan". The board did not follow its own master plan in passing the amendment, nor even its own conviction regarding the most appropriate use of plaintiff's land.

Mutual Life Ins. Co. v. State Tax Com.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


24 A.D.2d 853; 264 N.Y.S.2d 862; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2905



November 30, 1965 - Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant requested review of orders of the Supreme Court at Special Term in New York County, New York, which granted respondent government's motion to dismiss the complaint pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 199, and which granted reargument and adherence to the original decision.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a domestic insurance company, sought a declaration that the franchise tax required to be paid on premiums received for insurance, under N.Y. Tax Law § 187(2), did not apply to the cost of life, accident and health insurance under the employee benefit plan operated by appellant for its employees. Appellant contended that any demand for payment of a premium tax on such costs was illegal and unconstitutional. Special Term dismissed the complaint on the ground that, under N.Y. Tax Law § 199, an article 78 administrative proceeding was the exclusive remedy for reviewing determinations regarding corporation taxes. The merits of appellant's claim were not reached. On appeal, this court affirmed without additional comment.


OUTCOME: Orders affirmed without costs or disbursements to any party; appellant's exclusive remedy to review determinations regarding corporation taxes was an administrative proceeding.

996 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Common Council of Albany v. Town Bd. of Bethlehem


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


23 A.D.2d 381; 261 N.Y.S.2d 144; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3624



July 8, 1965 



DISPOSITION: Motion to dismiss petition denied, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner common council filed a petition pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 711 for a determination of whether a proposed annexation was in the over-all public interest. Respondent town board filed a motion to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(d) directed the Legislature to enact legislation for the resolution of annexation disputes between the governing bodies of municipalities. The Legislature enacted N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law art. 17, which permitted courts to intervene in such disputes. The common council of a city approved the proposed annexation of a town, but the town board disapproved the proposed annexation. The common council filed a petition for a determination of whether the proposed annexation was in the over-all public interest. The town board filed a motion to dismiss the petition. Upon review, the court denied the motion. The court held that art. 17 was not unconstitutional because the State was permitted to delegate the legislative function of determining municipal boundaries to the courts. The court determined that art. 17 complied with N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(d), and that the standard of "all-over public convenience" was not unconstitutionally vague. The court ruled that the decision of annexation would ultimately be decided by the majority of the qualified voters in the town and the city.


OUTCOME: The court denied the town board's motion to dismiss the common council's petition for a determination of whether the proposed annexation was in the over-all public interest.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- annexation of territory -- article 17 of General Municipal Law implementing article IX (§ 1, subd. [d]) of New York State Constitution, which provided for annexation of territory of one local government by another, not in violation of Federal Constitution or of New York Constitution, and did not constitute abdication by Legislature of its power and duties.

1. Article IX (§ 1, subd. [d]) of the New York State Constitution provides that: "No local government or any part of the territory thereof shall be annexed to another until the people, if any, of the territory proposed to be annexed shall have consented thereto by majority vote on a referendum and until the governing board of each local government, the area of which is affected, shall have consented thereto upon the basis of a determination that the annexation is in the over-all public interest. The consent of the governing board of a county shall be required only where a boundary of the county is affected.  [***2] On or before July first, nineteen hundred sixty-four, the legislature shall provide, where such consent of a governing body is not granted, for adjudication and determination, on the law and the facts, in a proceeding initiated in the supreme court, of the issue of whether the annexation is in the over-all public interest." When, pursuant to procedure provided in article 17 of the General Municipal Law, the Town Board of the Town of Bethlehem disapproved the annexation of territory in the town to the City of Albany, the Common Council of the City of Albany applied to the Appellate Division for a determination (§ 711) of whether the proposed annexation was in the "over-all public interest" and, in accord with section 712, the Appellate Division designated three Justices of the Supreme Court to hear and report. Article 17 of the General Municipal Law is not in violation of the Federal Constitution. There is no infringement, discrimination or other violations of the Federal Constitution.

2. Article 17 of the General Municipal Law was not invalid as an attempt by the Legislature to amend article IX (§ 1, subd. [d]) of the New York State Constitution.

3. While the Legislature may [***3] not delegate or surrender its authority to say what the law should be, by outlining the legislative policy and fixing the controlling principles it may delegate, as in this instance, to the courts the power to determine facts and conditions upon which the operation of the law depends. There was no abdication by the Legislature of its powers and duties but rather a clear fixing of power by a constitutional amendment. The implementing legislation was in compliance with the constitutional requirement.

4. Permitting the court to base its decision upon the conception of what is the "over-all public interest" did not constitute the delegation of a legislative function without proper standards and was not invalid because of vagueness. The argument that the passage of a statute by the Legislature might be unconstitutional, if not limited and defined by proper and sufficient guidelines is not applicable where, as here, the constitutional amendment delegated the authority.

5. With all of the safeguards provided and having in mind that it was the intent of the Legislature to provide a workable form of annexation, the statute has not exceeded the bounds of the constitutional mandate and the [***4] procedure is proper and constitutional under the New York law.

998 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Kaganovitch v. Wilkins


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


23 A.D.2d 178; 259 N.Y.S.2d 462; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4190



May 13, 1965 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed and matter remitted to Wyoming County Court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant unsuccessfully sought the issuance of a writ of habeas corpus in the Wyoming County Court (New York). He sought appellate review and alleged that the sentencing court's failure to have had submitted to it for its consideration a psychiatric report under N.Y. Penal Law § 2189-a before imposing an indeterminate sentence, together with the penal institutions' failure to give him psychiatric treatment was improper.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, prior to being tried for committing a sex offense, was reported insane, sent for psychiatric evaluation, and committed to a State hospital. After being certified as sane, defendant pled guilty to second degree assault with intent to commit the crime of sodomy. The court found that there was no proof that the sentencing court had or considered a psychiatric report submitted under N.Y. Penal Law § 2189-a before it sentenced defendant to an indeterminate sentence of from one year to life, nor was there any proof that he received psychiatric treatment while incarcerated at two prisons for a period of nine years. The court held that a prisoner serving an indeterminate sentence for a sex offense should be required to receive a thorough psychiatric examination not less than once every two years, as well as consideration by the Parole Board. In light of the failure to comply with § 2189-a and the court's mandates, the court ordered that defendant be remanded to the sentencing court for a full and complete hearing as to the extent of the psychological and psychiatric treatment afforded him and to assess his capability for release on parole.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of defendant's petition for habeas corpus and remanded the case to the sentencing court for a full hearing. The court also directed that defendant should be given a complete psychiatric examination to determine whether it would be dangerous to release him.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- psychiatric reports -- rehabilitation -- relator, who pleaded guilty to charge of assault in second degree, alleges sentencing court failed to have psychiatric report submitted in compliance with section 2189-a of Penal Law and that he was given no psychiatric treatment following sentence of one day to life -- matter remanded for hearing on questions of compliance with statute and extent of psychological and psychiatric treatment and whether he should be released.

Relator, who pleaded guilty to a charge of assault in the second degree with intent to commit the crime of sodomy, alleges that the sentencing court failed to have submitted to it for consideration a psychiatric report in compliance with section 2189-a of the Penal Law, and that the penal institutions in which he has been incarcerated failed to give him any psychiatric [***2] treatment, as mandated by the statute under which he received a one day to life sentence. His contention that he was not given a psychiatric examination pursuant to the Penal Law ( § 2189-a) was not explored at the hearing in this habeas corpus proceeding. Even if the psychiatric report was before the sentencing court the Attorney-General fails to refute, or in any manner deny, the statement made by relator that during his nine years' imprisonment at no time did he receive any kind of psychiatric treatment or any modern rehabilitation or any rehabilitation except imprisonment at hard labor. Relator alleges that he appeared before the Parole Board on four occasions and the board did not consider him for parole despite the report of the supervising psychiatrist that relator was not dangerous and a good risk, and should be released on parole. If, as claimed, relator has been in custody approximately 12 years under the circumstances set forth in his petition, this is a direct violation of the recommendations of the committee whose report was the basis for the statutory enactment. The matter is remanded to the Wyoming County Court for a full and complete hearing dealing specifically [***3] with the questions of compliance with section 2189-a of the Penal Law and the extent of the psychological and psychiatric treatment given relator during his many years of imprisonment. Relator should further be given a complete psychiatric examination by a duly qualified psychiatrist to determine whether it would be dangerous to release him and whether he is now capable of rejoining society on the outside. At the hearing the statement made by the supervising psychiatrist of Attica Prison should be investigated and there should also be secured such reports concerning relator as Attica Prison has and, if possible, there should be an examination, as a witness, of the supervising psychiatrist.

 

999 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fire Dep't of Rochester v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


23 A.D.2d 183; 259 N.Y.S.2d 517; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4191



May 13, 1965 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed on the law, without costs of this appeal to either party and Local Laws Nos. 5 and 6 of 1963 of the City of Rochester declared to be constitutional and valid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision of Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County (New York) enjoining appellant from interfering with the administering of funds of respondent city volunteer fire department pursuant to Local Laws No. 5 & No. 6 of 1963 of the City of Rochester.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Ins. Law §§ 553, 554 provided for tax money directed to volunteer firemen's fund. This money was also collected for city paid firemen although prior to the enactment of Public Laws No. 5 & No. 6 of 1963 of the City of Rochester, volunteer firemen had the authority to administer their own money through a board of trustees, which were not part of city government, but whose only function was to receive and distribute tax funds for the benefit of the volunteer firefighters. When the local laws were enacted, volunteer firemen challenged the validity of the laws. The lower court ruled them unconstitutional and enjoined the city from administering the money of volunteer fire department. On appeal, the court reversed holding that the local laws merely provided for a change in which the money was administered, not its purpose. The city exercised its home rule power properly.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the ruling of the lower court because the city had the right to change, through home rule legislation, the manner in which the funds were administered. The local laws at issue were constitutional and valid.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- tax on foreign insurers -- "property, affairs or government" -- Local Laws of City of Rochester transferring responsibility for receiving and distributing tax moneys paid by foreign fire insurance insurers and foreign mutual fire insurance insurers from "Fire Department of City of Rochester" which had been created by Legislature (L. 1864, ch. 140, § 2), to Treasurer and Comptroller of City of Rochester, enacted pursuant to home-rule powers (N. Y. Const., former art. IX), relate to property, affairs or government of city (Home Rule Law, § 11, subd. 1) and are valid.

The Insurance Law ( §§ 553, 554) provided for the payment of a tax imposed upon foreign fire insurance insurers and foreign mutual [***2] fire insurance insurers to local fire departments. Plaintiff "Fire Department of the City of Rochester" was created by a special act of the Legislature (L. 1864, ch. 140, § 2) with the sole purpose of administering the tax moneys paid over to it annually, pursuant to these statutes. Local Laws, 1963, No. 5 and No. 6 of the City of Rochester transferring the responsibility for receiving and distributing these tax moneys, as well as control over the present assets of the fund, to the Treasurer and Comptroller of the City of Rochester, were enacted pursuant to the home-rule powers granted by former article IX of the New York State Constitution. Section 12 of the former article provided for the adoption by the city of "local laws not inconsistent with the constitution and laws of the state relating to its property, affairs or government." In section 11, the Legislature was authorized to "confer on cities such powers of local legislation and administration in addition to the powers vested in cities by this article as it may * * * deem expedient". The City Home Rule Law (§ 11, subd. 1) granted to local legislative bodies the power "to adopt and amend * * * local laws in relation to [***3] the property, affairs or government of the city". It provided further that "Such local laws may change or supersede any provision of an act of the legislature theretofore enacted which provision does not in terms and in effect apply alike to all cities." The 1864 statute to be superseded "does not in terms and in effect apply alike to all cities." These local laws do relate to the property, affairs or government of the city. The election of the city to have the fund administered by its own officers, as mandated by these local laws, was properly and constitutionally within its home-rule powers.

Ferdon v. Rogers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


23 A.D.2d 851; 259 N.Y.S.2d 187; 1965 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4318
